THE RED PAGE.
Verses by Ethel Turner.
GUM LEAVES.
X asked the red gum tree :
“ Why red 1 Why red ? ”
“ The blood-red suns rise up and die o’er me,”
It said.
“ And you, O gaunt gum tree,
Why white ? Why white 1 ”
“ The ghosts of all the bush passed, creepingly,
One night.”
“ And you, O blue gum tree,
Why are you blue ? ”
“ The far, sweet seas thrill every leaf of me
Till I grow blue.”
“ And why these nuns in grey,
With silvered hair ? ”
The grey gums sighed, and answered, sobbingly,
“We bear.”
A CANVAS.
A child’s face, small and grave.
With wind-blown hair,
Brown, with a laugh in it,
Sweet eyes that stare
Out of the canvas., Lips
Childishly red,
Drooping with grief as if
For early bed.
Ever the eyes smile out
To me below—
Ever the grieved lips droop
As if they know
How quiet I am alone. . .
There is a chair,
The lamp-light leaves it out,
But it is there.
The shadows make it black
And large ; I know
How very- small it is
And very low.
The lamp-light does not reach
The cup and plate,
The doll without a head,
The small, smeared slate.
It falls upon the wall’s
One pictured place.
Oh, child-soft hair and lips l
Oh, little face !
A TREMBLING STAR.
“There is my little trembling star,” she said.
I looked ; once more
The tender sea had put the sun to bed,
And heaven's floor
Was grey.
And nowhere yet in all that young night sky
Was any star,
But one that hung above the sea. Not high,
Nor very far
Away.
“ I watch it every night,” she said, and crept
Within my arm.
“ Soft little star, I wish the angels kept
It safe from harm
Alway.
“ I know it is afraid,” she said ; her eyes
Held a sweet tear.
“ They send it all atone into the skies,
No big stars near,
To stay.
“ They push it out before the sweet, kind moon
Lights up the sea.
They laugh because it fears the dark. ‘ Soon, soon,
You’ll braver be,’
They say.
“ One night I climbed far up that high white tree
Beside the beach,
And tried to stretch my hand across the sea
And tried to reach
The grey.
“ For something made me feel my heart would break
Unless that night
I in my hand my trembling star could take
And kiss its fright
Away.
“There only blew a strange wind chillily,
And clouds were swep t.
The angels would not let my own star see
That someone wept.
I pray
“To Christ, who hears my little'prayers each night,
That he will seek
Through all His skies for that sweet, frightened light,
And stoop his cheek
And say
“ ‘ My angels must not send so frail a thing
To light the West.
Lift up the little trembling star to cling
About my breast
Alway.’ ”
The Holy Medical Church.
HAVE you ever seen a goat tethered to a
gum-tree ? That is the image and sym-
bol of Melb. R.C. Archbishop Carr,
pamphleteering about “ The Morality of
Medical practice.” Within the limits of his
creed Archbishop Carr is acute and forcible—he
eats the herbage logically bare. But he cannot
strain farther than his rope will permit. Beyond
his bare patch is the universe; and he cannot
reach it—tethered to his Dogma.
What troubles the Archbishop and the Dogma
is Man’s immortal soul —as invented, and defined,
and described by Holy Church. What troubles
the logic of the Archbishop and the Dogma is that
Man hasn’t any immortal soul—so far as scientific
evidence goeS. There is no room for a soul in the
human carcase. The utmost that we can see is
brain ; and we can trace the growth of the brain
through various stages of evolution till it cul-
minates in the human brain—the best of which
we have any knowledge. Even the hu-
man brain has not always, had the same
powers. It has. not even the same powers in
different human races to-day. The brain of the
stone-age man was inferior to the brain of the
European of .1900. The brain of the local abori-
ginal is inferior to that of the Australian of Euro-
pean parentage. And there is no human idea, or
act, or fact, which cannot be accounted for in
terms of the brain. Precisely how the brain
The artist who drew this for The Dome turned it t’other way about and called it a
portrait of Walt Whitman. As every local sonneteer will see at once, it is really a
portrait of Sargasso—not the plumber or the oysterman, but the Genius of the Weedy Sea.
works *to achieve the processes of thought we do
not know, but we are learning.
“Soul,” “spirit”—these words, when used in
the religious sense, are physically meaningless.
And they are unnecessary. The brain explains
everything. “Soul” and “spirit” have no exis-
tence apart from the brain. When the brain is in-
juredor destroyed the man becomes insane or diet—
just like any other animal. Humanity has invented
many creeds to account for the things about
life which it did not understand. Some
of these creeds have given souls to animals other
than man—even to vegetables. It was more
logical to do this ; because a dog’s brain, for
example, differs from a man’s only in the degree
of its development. A dog has just as much
scientific title to a “soul” as a man has; and
there is just as much scientific evidence for the
dog’s, “soul” as' for the man’s—that is to say,
there is none. A dog’s “soul,” if it existed,
might be different in kind from a man’s ; but it
might just as reasonably exist. Thus the creeds
which sent the warrior’s dog and his horse and his
other belongings to keep him company in the
happy hunting-grounds were rather more consist-
ent than the creeds which say that only humanity
has an immortal soul.
The idea of a “soul” is flattering to human
vanity. It gives many people pleasure to think
that when they die, and are buried, and decom-
s pose, and become at last as indistinguishable
from “this compost” of earth as the myriads
who have gone before, they will nevertheless
retain a “spiritual” individuality somewhere
among the stars. They feel themselves such fine
fellows that they are quite sure their identity
deserves preservation to all eternity. And when
one fine fellow marries another fine, fellow of the
opposite sex, and from their physical union, as a
result of delicate physical processes, a new human
being or a dozen new human beings may be pro-
duced, it is “ God’s ” business, according to many
religious creeds, to provide “ souls” few or many,
as the whim of the tine fellows may require.
And just here the creeds, including the creed of
the R. C. Church to which Archbishop Carr be-
longs, are in a difficulty. The teaching of the
R.C. Church is more less based upon the Hebrew
Scriptures ; and the Hebrew Scriptures are sadly
indefinite as to the “ soul.” They do not explain
what it is, or when and how it is supplied.
So the Church has had to make interesting
guesses on the subject. At different times dif-
ferent guesses have been approved by the Popes
and the Fathers, and the clergy and the laity have
changed their beliefs in accordance with the
dictates of superior authority. You see, the
subject is so fascinatingly vague ; and you have to
bring your dogma, whatever it may be, into accord
with the other dogmas of the Church. The old
Platonic theory held that the soul is eternal—
that is to say, there are a multitude of loose
souls wandering about space, and when a
human child is born one of these souls is caught
and fixed somewhere in its body. This theory,
however, fell into ecclesiastical disrepute, because
it does not chime with the doctrine of total
depravity and that original sin which we are all
supposed to have inherited from Adam. So
it was for a long time contended by the people
who settled all about souls that the soul is in-
herited from the father, who inherited it from his
father, and so on back to the same old Adam.
This particular belief was called Traducianism.
Archbishop Carr, however, announces that the
Church is now solid on the Creationism theory,
which holds that as soon as a man and'woman
cometogether, and a child isconceived, “the human
soul is created by God, and united to the body at
the first instant of conception.” Considering
ecclesiastical dignitaries, one wonders if the
Almighty is sometimes a little bit late.
Archbishop Carr assures us that we may take
for granted, on the united testimony of medical
and sacred science, ■ that this theory is correct.
His sacred authorities are Scripture, Pope Inno-
cent 11, Pope Sixtus Y, Pope Innocent XI, and
David the Psalmist. His medical authority
—only one—is Aristotle. Aristotle died B.c. 322
—a trifle of 2222 years ago. Shall we say that
Aristotle, as a medical authority, is just a little
bit passe '/
You see now how Archbish op Carr’s mind grazes
in its tether. He cannot quote modern medical
science to prove that there is a soul, or to show
when or how the soul arrives and where it is
situated in the body, because medical .science
knows nothing about souls, and confines
itself strictly to the things it knows something
about. No accoucheur ever heard a soul scratch-
ing at the door of the bedroom in order to get in,
or saw a Soul jump down the throat of a newly-
born infant. No one ever saw a god or an angel
hovering about with a soul under his wing,
patiently awaiting “ the first instant of conception.”
There is nothing about souls in the medical dic-
tionary. But if you will accept the alleged utter-
ance of David as science, and the blind guess of
Aristotle as science, and the unproven dogma of
Innocent XI as science, then Archbishop Carr
will prove you anything you like about souls. He
merely wants you to give him all the data without
scrutiny. Then he will give you all the inferences
without winking.
Archbishop Carr is. arguing about the morality
of medical practice in this his recently-published
pamphlet. What he wishes to do is to find a reli-
gious reason for all his conclusions. And the
interesting thing about Archbishop Carr is
that for the most part his conclusions
are defensible for social reasons. By
the wrong road he reaches the right end.
He denounces craniotomy—the dissection of the
skull of an unborn infant in order to save
the mother’s life when natural delivery is inadvis-
able ; and he denounces it because unless that
infant is baptised its immortal soul will perish.
(Archbishop Carr does not bother about the sense
or j ustice of creating a soul for a destruction which
it cannot possibly avoid ; but that is not the main
question.) Medical and social reasons condemn
craniotomy also, as long as there are' other means
which give a fair chance of preserving the
mother’s life as. well as the infant’s. But if a birth
Were brought to this cleair issue—mother’s life or
infant’s —surely no husband, no surgeon, would
shrink from saving the mother? ' Even Arch-
bishop Carr 'seems to find it difficult to
reconcile himself to the thought of slaying the
mother that the infant may be saved. Apparently
it is only.the thought of the “soul”—for which
baptism is necessary—that decides him. The
mother’s “soul” having been saved by bap-
tism, the child’s “soul” comes before the
mother’s life, from an ecclesiastical point
of view. But what intelligent man un-
trammelled by creed will accept the ecclesiasti-
cal point of view ? Yet if Archbishop Carr did
not carry his dogma to so bitter an extreme, if he
would content himself with declaring that cranio-
tomy is nearly always unnecessary, instead of
always, he would be in agreement with human
feeling and modern medical practice. It is the
fetish of the “ soul ” which ruins his reason.
Eor the rest, Archbishop Carr is incidentally
concerned with the divine institution of marriage
—the use of “ preventatives ” and the prevention
of illegitimacy—and his feud with illegitimacy
and “preventatives” would be thoroughly justifi-
able on social grounds if religion had uttered no
word about marriage. The mischief is that the indi-
vidual will not always do what the community
wishes him to do ; and unless you can show that
it pays him, there is no earthly or ethical reason
why he should.

This is A.G.T.’s opinion—
Re statement (July 7) that Lambert “ has not one gleam
of imagination visible in his work up to the present.”
What do you mean by imagination ? Lambert has imagi-
nation, and plenty of it ; you can see it in his painting of
heads. If a man can divine an interesting side to a sitter
and get it into his painting, surely he gives a clear proof
of the possession of imagination ? Tom Roberts has it;
and paints good portraits therefore. Julian Ashton lacks
it; and his portraits, as a rule, are hopelessly had and un-
like the model. Have you seen Lambert’s illustrations to
Miss Whitfeld’s fairy tales ?—the stories are of little ac-
count, but Lambert managed to get imagination, delicacy
and brilliance into every picture he drew. Wolin-
ski’s picture has n’t one touch of imagination ; it is
anguish to the square inch, and skilful painting—nothing
more. The Latouche influence is not in the choice of sub-
ject, but in the handling of the subject, the colour
scheme, &c., and is sufficiently strong to discount the
value of the originality of Wolinski’s picture. Wolinski’s
work has 'achievement to a certain limit; promise it has
none.
Imagination is the faculty of forming mental
images; but, in the high and special sense, the
images must be created by the mind, not merely
reflected from a physical object. That Lambert
“can divine an interesting side” to a jjortrait-
subject does not prove he has imagination in this
sense, since he only paints what he has seen. If
he has not seen it, he does not know it exists, and
in painting what he does not know to exist he will
probably paint a bad portrait. A good portrait is a
likeness of the outer man suffused with the inner
spirit. But this inner spirit is not “imagined” : it
shines out to be watched, studied, realised. Lambert
has never made an effective painting of an idea—
a something intangible to the senses, invented and
thrown forth ; his effective work has always been
a representation of something visible, which could
be studied and imitated; —hence the statement
that he has not shown imagination. As to Wolin-
ski: his picture is far from perfect, but it is the
best shown.
Erom a bookseller’s point of view, trade has
rarely been worse in .Sydney. From a book-
buyer’s view-point, it has never been better.
There are clearance sales on’ all hands; and
several firms advertise new books at a substantial
discount from publishers’ prices. For five shil-
lings, nowadays, you can get twenty shillings’
worth of very fair literature.

“The Hill of Visions and Other Poems,” by
John Harrington Lenane, is published in London
(Kegan Paul) and dated Melbourne, 1899. The
contents are verses destitute of originality or
value.
Ethel Turner’s graceful verses, ashine with sym-
pathy, are borrowed from her recently-issued
“‘Gum Leaves’’—which, by the way, is a book
uncommonly well printed by the publishers, Wm.
Brooks and Co. “ A Trembling Star ” originally
appeared in The Bookfellow, deceased.
Significant passage showing in what estimation
the medical profession was held by an “ inspired ”
Hebrew chronicler (11. Chron. xvi., 12, 13): —
And Asa, in the thirty and ninth year of his reign, was
diseased in his feet; his disease was great, yet in his
disease he sought not to the Lord, but to the physicians.
And Asa slept with his fathers.
Neat, eh?
Brisb. Telegraph dexterously reported a recent
hanging as “ passing off without a hitch.”
Told of an Omar Khayyam Club lecturer who
went to spread light in the provinces that, when
he had finished his genuflexions before the idol
and the audience, the mayor-chairman thanked him
effusively. “So pleased to have heard you, Mr.
——. Do you know, until this evening I never
quite comprehended which was Omar Khayyam
and which was Hunyadi Janos.”
Many people have treasured the poignant little
verses of “ M. J.C.” (Mrs. Gilmore) in The Queens-
lander and The Bulletin. Her Paraguayan history
reads sadly. Writing recently, she sums-up her
experience of the tragic dream called “New Aus-
tralia”—
Travelled alone from Sydney to Paraguay. Lost father
and mother. Married; had a son. Resigned from
Colony. Baby nearly died. Husband absent four months
in Patagonia, out of the way of mails ; no word from him.
Fever and disappointment. Getting grey, but quite pre-
pared to begin life again on nothing.
Poor little woman !

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bairnsdale Advertiser wants to know :
But would these rabid journals be prepared to practise,
in their private business, that liberty of the subject to
do whatever he pleases which thev have been so fond of
preaching all through this wart Would the Sydney
Bulletin, the organ of the Boer cause—or any other
anti-British cause—in Australia, be prepared to tolerate
on its staff a red-hot bigoted specimen of the class which
admits no equal to the English as a race, and to listen
patiently to his wholesale and incessant condemnation s
of the paper, its principles and its policy ? We think not.
A paper like that shouldn’t think. The Bulletin
has two such individuals on its staff, and one of
them has written, at various times within the last
nine months, 47 letters to the editor abusing the
paper’s policy. The Bairnsdale organ, in similar
circumstances, would evidently have done some-
thing violent, but The Bulletin isn’t so narrow-
minded.
Work and Wages.
When times are dull and bills are due,
When every prospect’s black or blue,
There’s always One with jobs to spare who pays
what he engages ;
His bellows keep his forge aglow,
And as he sweats he chuckles low,
O Ye who do the Devil’s work—ye’ll get the
Devil’s wages !
And if ye swelter at his oall
In boozing-ken or church or hall,
He hath a myrmidon at hand who all your merit
gauges;
And when ye lay your crafty lies
To trap the Fool and trip the Wise,
He dots it down—“ The Devil’s work shall get the
Devil’s wages ! ”
So grind the poor as grind ye can,
And prance a prince in Money’s van,
And suck the struggling Widow’s blood by com-
fortable stages ;
And, as ye sweat your victims thin,
Be sure there’s one who stands a-grin,
And murmurs low : “He did my work and he
shall have my wages ! ”
And though to-day the Preacher drones
His honeyed tale in honeyed tones,
And puts perdition on the shelf in antiquated
pages;
Let ne’er a knave your wits befool,
Though “ fires below be waxen cool,”
If ye but do the Devil’s work ye shall not miss his
wages !
For this, I hold, is ever true —
We all must drink the broth we brew,
We’re never tied upon the perch before we build
the cages;
Our tender backs are always flayed
Beneath the lash our hands have made:
0 we who do the Devil’s work, we get the Devil’s
wages !
So play the wanton at your will,
And wend among the swine to swill,
Since only drink from out the trough your glorious
thirst assuages ;
But, late or early, this I trow —
Ye’ll grasp a truth before ye go,
O we who did the Devil’s work, we got the Devil’s
wages !
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
Will You Remember
Will you remember when I took
Your hand beneath the white,
Star-printed pages of the Book
Of All the Worlds, that night?
Will you remember ?
Will you forget the distant song
Of dying seas, that told
Their rosaries of love along
Far-shadowed shores of .gold?
Will you forget ?
Will you remember how the leaves
Of sleeping tree-ferns green
Hung drooping from their pillared sheaves,
In rarest Valenciennes ?
Will you remember ?
Will you forget the clinging vine
That to its chosen tree
In tropic passion did entwine —■
Close, close, as you to me ?
Will you forget ?
Will you in fancy treasure yet
The perfume of its flowers ?
Do you remember or forget
What star-lit dreams were ours ?
Do you remember ?
Ah, never till he come, the Shade
Who glooms our dreams with rue,
Will sleep ihat sea, that fragrance fade,
Or Memory fail. . . . But you,
Will you remember?
Q.
E. J. BRADY.
The Titan is Thankful.
One triumph more—another pigmy band
Lay trodden down, but meekly in the mires
Of slaughter knelt the Titan, while his choirs
A psalm of praise quavered through all the land.
“Not mine, not mine the glory, but Thy Hand,
God of the English, hurled those dreadful fires,
And gave me strength to compass my desires,
That are as Thine—and blessed my feeble brand.”
But when he rose self-chastened from his knees,
To drive again weak children to the mill,
And serve his real Godhead of the Till,
The Earth-born laughed around their narrowed
seas.
“ Ah ! that Time could our weary brother ease
Of fulsome cant, his sorest burden still! ”
N.S.W.
C. KENDALL.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem,, paragraph, illustration, or
a iy other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding twa,columns in length,
or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that ive
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient

will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a nood idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
£l, with 3s. 3d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

SATURDAY, JULY 21. 1900.
The Groper Spider and the Groper Fly.
As this issue goes to press the extreme
tail-end of the Federal fight in the last un-
federated province of Australia is in sight.
On the 31st of this month it is known as a
matter of certainty that the Westralian
goldfields will vote solidly for Federation.
The coastal towns are uncertain; in the
aggregate their vote may be equally divided
though more probably it will show a leaning
towards union. As for the agricultural and
pastoral districts, they are supposed to be
the stronghold of Parochialism, and it is
confidently foretold in many unreliable
circles that the bucolic Groper will rise up
unanimously and defend the Separatist
policy to the last gasp. And yet, by the
showing of the leaders of Separatism, it
is the agricultural and pastoral Groper,
above all other Gropers, who should
vote the Federal ticket with all his might.
For, according to the solemn promise and
swear of the Separatist leaders, his very
existence—his bread and cheese, his clothes
and his boots, and the roof above his head
■—may depend on Federation being carried
on 31st July. To the Goldfields it is largely
a matter of national sentiment, based on a
desire for the prosperity and progress of
Australia at large. To the coastal towns it
is a matter of convenience and profit, though
not of life or death. To the agricultural
Westralian, unless the anti-Federal leaders
are the most incredible set of romancers on
this continent, the carrying of Federation is
almost a matter of actual existence. He
may want Federation badly from motives of
patriotism ; he will assuredly want it a great
deal worse if he realises in time that, sup-
posing Federation isn’t achieved, his anti-
Federal leaders, in their anxiety to placate
the Goldfields’ vote, have promised to adopt
a policy which will probably leave him with-
out boots.
* • • •
The food duties are the question which
most nearly interests the Westralian agricul-
turist and pastoralist. In Westralia there
are duties on horses, cattle, pigs, sheep, on
beef, mutton, pork, biscuits, butter, candied
fruits, green fruits, bottled fruits, cheese,
raw cocoa, coffee (whether raw, roasted or as
essence), currants, eggs, fish, flour, oats,
rye, wheat, maize, barley, beans, peas, bran,
pollard, hay, chaff, hams, tongues, honey,
hops, jams, jellies, lard, linseed and linseed
meal, milk, onions, pickles, vegetables,
raisins, salt—in fact, on almost everything
eatable as well as almost everything drink-
able. All these come under the general
term of the food duties. These duties apply
not only to food imported from the other pro-
vinces of Australasia, but to that imported
from all the outside world as well. The
agricultural and pastoral interest is bent on
the retention of these duties ; the Goldfields
population can’t grow any food on the
seared and scorched inland wastes where the
wild-cat bleaches in the sun, and it votes for
the abolition of the food duties with remark-
able unanimity.
• * * *
If Federation is achieved these duties will,
undoubtedly, be abolished as regards imports
from the other provinces of the Common-
wealth. The Federal Constitution provides
that they won’t be wholly abolished for five
years, but sooner or later, so far as the
other provinces are concerned, they will go.
As regards the outside world they will
certainly remain. Queensland, Victoria,
Tasmania and South Australia all have
similar duties ; they all follow the
policy of Protection against the outside
world, and among them there will be an
overwhelming majority in favour of retain-
ing the food duties as against Europe, Asia,
Africa and America. That is the situation
if the people of Westralia accept Federation
on the 31st July.
• • • •
And if Federation isn't accepted there is
the solemn assurance of the anti-Federal
leaders that the food duties will be abso-
lutely taken off. That promise has been
made over and over again as the reward
which Westralia will receive if it will only
vote against Federation and keep out of the
Commonwealth. Minister Throssell, and
Premier Forrest, and their joint and several
organ, the Perth Morning Herald, have all
taken their more or less solemn oath to this
effect. It is also- specially promised in the
manifesto of the National League, which
manifesto was published for many days
on the hinder page of Perth Morning
Herald in these words :
Remember this—That the Government of Western Aus-
tralia has promised that next session the duties will be
taken off meats of all kinds, and a majority of members
is pledged to remove the duties on butter', eggs, cheese
hams, bacon, &c. & ’
It is a painful situation for the past-oralists
and agriculturists of Groperland, and they
have very little time in which to consider it.
To accept Federation means that, after five
years or a little more, the duties which now
protect them against the other provinces will
be taken off, though they will still be pro-
tected against the rest of the world. To
refuse Federation and remain a separate State
means, on the solemn assurance of their
most trusted leaders, that in probably about
a year the duties which now protect them
against all the world will be taken off, and
they will be left with no protection against
anybody. Federation will expose them, after
a time, to the competition of the high-priced
labor of the other provinces. The refusal of
Federation will expose them, almost immedi-
ately, to the competition, not only of the high-
priced labor of the other provinces, but
also to that of all the labour of Europe,
Asia, and Africa. The leaders of the anti-
Federal party pledge themselves to the agri-
culturists of Westralia that if they will only
stand by them, and destroy by their votes
all prospects of Federation, their reward
will be ruin—absolute and hopeless ruin. It
seems an incredibly foolish thing to promise,
but the situation of the Parochialist leaders
is peculiar enough to explain the anomalv.
.* * * •
This is the situation, condensed into
seven propositions :
goldfields population has no personal interest,

as they apply to food imported from the other provinces,
Will get it cheaper food. ’

the lorrest regime strike it as they don’t strike the old

farmer ; but then Federation would ruin the gang in office

* * * *
That is about how things stand with the
anti-Federal crowd in Westralia, Its pre-
tence that it is against Federation because
Federation will involve a decrease of perhaps
£300,000 a year in the Customs revenue is a
thin and miserably incapable invention, see-
ing that it solemnly promises, if Federation
is rejected, to decrease the customs revenue
by about that amount itself. The pretence
that it is anti-Federal because (so it is often
alleged) the Commonwealth will fix on
Westralia “the heavy burden of the Vic-
torian tariff ” is another inefficient fiction ;
the Westralian customs tariff is equal to 17
per cent, on all imports while the Victorian
tariff is much lighter, and only amounts to
llv? per cent, on all imports. And the dirge
about the necessity of standing out of the
Commonwealth till Westralia is older, and
its industries are more deeply rooted, and
it is better able to stand the competition of
the other Australian provinces, is the poorest
fiction in the heap, seeing that the party
which talks in this strain has solemnly
pledged itself, if there is no Federation, to
deprive Westralia’s most important indus-
tries—the most important, that is, apart
from the gold industry, which needs no
tariff support—of all Protection, and let
them face the competition of the whole
world as best they may. The Forrest
crowd is almost undisguisedly hostile to
Federation—either because Federation will
wreck its miserable little political im-
portance, or because it can’t get out
of its old mental groove. It can’t hope to
keep Westralia out of the Commonwealth
and at the same time avert disruption (which
would be ruin to the fragment of the pro-
vince which wag left), unless it pacifies the
stormy Federationists of Kalgoorlie and
Coolgardie. So it proposes to buy their
goodwill by throwing the agricultural indus-
try overboard.
• • * *
The best hope for the agricultural popula-
tion of Westralia, if the Federal cause is
defeated on 31st, is that the Government
doesn’t mean to keep its promise, and that
it won’t abolish the food duties after all.
And that hope doesn’t amount to much. If
Westralia stands out, then the Forrest
Government must either placate the agricul-
tural interest at the expense of the Gold-
fields by retaining the food duties, or placate
the Goldfields at the expense of the agricul-
tural industry by utterly abolishing them.
It will probably give way to the crowd that
it is most afraid of. The miners are concen-
trated and united and formidable ; the agri-
cultural population is widely scattered, dis-
united, and in consequence not so formidable.
The miners threaten separation ; the agricul-
turists don’t. So the chances are that the
Forrest party will keep to its word, for
once, and let the Groper agriculturist go to
ruin. If it didn’t, the Goldfields would
separate sooner or later, and join .the Com-
monwealth ; and the Federal tariff would rise
up round them and shut out the produce of
the W.A. farming districts, and the Groper
agriculturist would be ruined just as surely
that way as the other. A decisive vote against
Federation means ultimate disaster to the
Groper agriculturist either way. What he
has to consider is whether he feels like being
ruined for any narrow-minded politician’s
temporary convenience.
Finland—Queensland—Canada.
After Poland, Finland—the Russian maw is in-
satiable.. Finland became a Russian principality
with strict guarantees of constitutional indepen-
dence, sworn to by the Czars. The guarantees
were flouted ; the constitution broken ; and the
Finnish nation, jealous of its liberties, proud of
its ancient constitution, finds that its country has
become a mere province of Russia, like Moscow or
Minsk. So the bolder hearts of Finland, hating
the heel of despotism, are giving up home and
country and turning westward, southward, any-
where where they may live a free life under free
laws.
* • • •
Some of these Finns—some hundreds—came re-
cently to Queensland. They had heard of the
young nation just born beneath the Southern
Cross, and they had been fascinated by the pro-
mises of a Queensland immigration-agent. They
were fine fellows, modest, cleanly, manly—every-
body who has seen them speaks well of
them. They were educated and enlightened,
but used to hard labor, and liking labor,
lhan the Scandinavian races, indeed, there
are no better men in the world to-day.
Who indeed that has lived among them in
their own hospitable country, or enjoyed their
companionship in a foreign land, has ever
met such genial, generous, honest neighbors?
hey have assimilated modern culture while losing
nothing of savage virtue. In an age of fret and
fever they have kept unspoiled nerves, living close
to the earth, retaining the good primitive qualities
which cities destroy. These Finns who came to
Queensland were excellent types of their race.
JNearly every man was worth his weight in silver
as a colonist. With a little encouragement, a
little care, they would have settled on the soil as
producers, to rear families like themselves for the
good of Australia.
• • • *
What should have been done with them ? This !
They should have been given on perpetual lease a
tract of good land, not too far from a river and a
market, tenure to be conditional on improvement,
and sectional possession to be assured by payment
of rent-value after ten years. They should have
been helped with seeds and tools—helped, and
closely inspected. The shiftless ‘ ‘ unemployed ”
of Australian cities would not repay such a gift
such help. The Finns had the grit of men in them ;
and they would have returned a hundredfold for
every penny expended.
* * • m
What was done with them ? This ! They were
shamefully deceived—there is no other term-
deceived firstly, secondly, and lastly. The Govt.
agent in England bad told them of ample employ-
ment, high wages, easy terms for land. They
found on arrival at Brisbane scanty employment,
low wages, no land available but barren land.
The Govt, gave them so little help that it
gave them none; the Govt., indeed, though
its own paid agents had seduced them to Queens-
land, seemed to regard them as a troublesome
burden. They were sent from post to pillar, from
pillar to post, drifting over the length of Queens-
land. Most of them spoke no English ; they were
thus at the mercy of employers, who took their
labour at the lowest wages, and fleeced them of
even those wages. After a period of heartbreak-
ing idleness, some of the Finns were sent to
the syndicate railway works, near Chillagoe
(N.Q.), where they were employed cutting
sleepers at a rate which local laborers would not
accept. Their spokesman asserts that when their
contract was paid up, they found they had
earned not Chinamen’s wages—2s. 6d. per man
per day. He asserts, further, that numbers of the
sleepers which they had cut were condemned
without cause, and afterwards used without pay-
ment. And now, weary and heart-sick, the deluded
Finns are shaking the dust of Queensland from
their feet, and going to Canada. Distant fields
look green ; but there is reason to believe that
Canada treats its immigrants better than Queens-
land does. Canada has not Queensland’s wealth
of soil and climate; but Tolstoy’s Dukhobortski
are thriving there. And Canada has ceased em-
ploying its Agent-General as a tout to procure
cheap labor for private syndicates.
* * * •
The despair of the Finns of Queensland can be
gathered from a letter by their spokesman, Matti
Kukikka, in a recent Cairns Advocate. This
man was editor of a daily Labor paper in Finland,
and has painfully taught himself English. His
letter breathes honest enthusiasm. He says that
he and his compatriots came to Australia as to a
land of liberty, where industry and integrity would
be appreciated, and they could unite with others
in the development of social ideals.
I have been here now eight months. What did I find
here? Disappointments— only disappointments ! All the
prospects of possibility for the immigrants to gain money
in a short time and get their own land which the Govern-
ment of Queensland had represented in their pamphlets,
have vanished as.soap-bubbles. All my essays to open
the eyes of the Government to comprehend the great ad-
vantage to this indebted country of the bending of the
large Finnish emigration from America to Queensland, got
lost. My task for the “Huguenots” of Finland was im-
possible to realise in that way.
Alright, did I. think, I will try the other, although
harder way. I will become an ordinary worker, become a
good member of the Labor party, win in that
way an influence in the party, and begin then anew
the realisation of my great idea. But, after the experi-
ence I got in Brisbane, in some other places, and at last
by the Chillagoe railway, I have already given up this
programme, too. Meanwhile, I collected some experi-
ences of a worker. I learnt to know that the laborers in
Queensland —there are, of course, exceptions—are too
drunk, too vulgar, and too hateful against all foreigners
that are sober, friendly, and honest as the Finns, to think
that they could become equals with them striving for the
same holy ideals. . . . But that has not pulled me apart
from Queensland and abolished all the dreams of an
active future in this society. Called lately by many
Finns to America to order there a Finn-colony, I say as a
real representative for the Labor party of Finland my
farewell to the brothers of Queensland. I and my fellows
—for after me truly all Finns will leave this beautiful
country—will in future still meet' together under the
English flag. In the present age it is better than all
others, assuring freedom and higher evolution to
humanity, and I will be one true defender of it.
My friendly hope is only that the people of Queensland
as soon as possible will rise to the same level of civilisa-
tion as the other cultured peoples of the world.— Matti
Kurikka.
There is nothing to be said against the truth of
this indictment. The Q. Labor-party, if it did
nothing to injure the friendless Finns, did little
or nothing to help them. It looked on them
askance, as interlopers in the labour market. So
they were; but only because they were ignorant
of English, and helpless in the hands of their em-
Payers. They did not want to be wages-men.
They wanted to work for themselves, and would
have grudged no effort which led them to inde-
pendence. It is nonsense to say that in all the
waste lands of Queensland there is no room for
such men. Even now, it is not too late for the Q.
Govt, to help them. And if the Q. Govt, has not
the brams or the will to help the strangers whom
it has cheated, then surely some other Australian
Govt, can utilise their invaluable manhood. Such
men should never, never, never be permitted to
leave Australia as outcasts.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Tasmania Learns Something.
After four or five years of weary waiting for the
Grreat Western (Land Grant) Railway Co. to get
really to work and build the Great Western Rail-
way, lasmama has at last dropped on an item of
definite information. It has discovered that there
isn t and never was any Great Western Railway
00. at all. Certain people undertook to build the
Great Western Railway (only 150 miles long) pro-
vided they received a grant of 500,000 acres of
land and many other valuable rights. These
people it now appears, do not constitute the
Great Western Railway Co. —they merely con-
iMA U nnn a v sraall co -> with a capital of about
±40,000, which proposes to form a Great Western
Kailway Co. and sell the concession to it. They
refuse to put their own money into their own
railway on any terms. Tor about four years they
boggled over various attempts to float their
G.W. Railway Co., but the owners of the
concession wanted about £890,000 in cash
and shares as the purchase price of the
bare right to build a line which will only be worth
about £600,000 when built, and this enormous grab
blocked business. At length their concession
expired by sheer waste of time. The time allowed
them by the Act of Parliament in which to com-
mence work ran out. Premier Braddon, as an
honest old man, eabled to them that their Act had
expired ; also, as a needlessly generous old man,
he promised to get it renewed. Just at this
moment, so it is alleged, the long-delayed float
was really coming off. The syndicators had really
found some people to buy their concession at their
own price, and were cheerfully selling it—so it
would appear without telling the buyers that
their concession had run out through their failure
to start work, and that they had no longer
legally anything to sell. The float naturally fell
through when the honest old man explained
that there was nothing left to float, and
the co., which lias always been going
to organise the G.W R. Co. and never doing
it, has cursed the honest old man ever
TT n °-vtr H\ e 9 0ncess i° n has been renewed, but
?,° G - W - Railway Co. has been floated, and
still the co. which owns the concession refuses to
put a pick in the ground with its own money.
But it says that if the Government will build
part of the line itself, and give it the same
land-grant for building 110 miles that it was
to get for building 150 miles, it will be able
to float its company—which is just what it
lias been saying for several years now. Con-
sidering how the Tasmanian West Coast is look-


ing up it still seems to The Bulletin that it
would be a fair thing for the Tasmanian Govern-
ment to build the line itself, and hand the 500,000-
acre endowment to its own railway department.
It would at least get the railway that way ; the
other way it isn’t at all likely to get anything ex-
cept the curses of the syndicate and a lesson in
patience and long-suffering. Already the Tas-
manians who are waiting for their G.W. Railway
have put up such a record for patience that Job is
left far behind and is now hardly visible on the
horizon.

The Lost Glenelg - .
After a period of labor extending over 110 days,
Vic. Marine Court has at length been delivered of
its decision in the case of the Glenelg. The vessel
foundered on 25th March last, and the Court finds
that she was properly inspected throughout prior
to her last-issued certificate being given, and that
the surveyors duly satisfied themselves as to her
condition during currency of said certificate ; alsB,
that her owners took proper measures to keep her
in apple-pie order ; that her decks and her engine-
room skylight were sufficiently strong for her
work (though nobody ever suggested that her
decks, or masts, or port binnacle weren’t all they
should be) ; that her cargo was properly stowed,
and that she was justified in putting to sea ; also,
that she was properly navigated; that she was
sufficiently equipped in the matter of boats, rafts,
and life-belts, though a larger margin of boats
should be allowed for in cases of emergency ; and,
finally, that the immediate cause of foundering
was that she sprang a leak, but, as there was
nothing to show the location or cause of said
leak, the Court could not attribute blame to any-
one. Which means that the ship sank through
taking in too much water, and that it was the act
of God. On one small point only is this cursory
Court of opinion that the Glenelg was not up to
requirements : she should have had a water-tight
screw tunnel, or her after bulkhead should have
been carried up to the main deck, but it blames
nobody for the lack of these things. Apparently
the Court has forgotten all about the awful state-
ments thrown at it by witness after witness in the
early part of the inquiry. It is conveniently
oblivious of that crank-shaft with the crack in it;
it is utterly heedless of those alleged cardboard
plates, only l-16th of an inch thick, through which
it was sworn a seaman put his hammer; also, it
takes no notice of those other portions of the hull
which, it was declared, could not be chipped
because they were too rotten, where the paint was
slapped on, in the hope that it would hold for a
while and “ last their time.” (Age, 2/5/’00.) Also,
this futile Court omits all reference to the fact
that the ship’s boats would not have provided
accommodation for all the people on board.
As to .the cause of the disaster, that was
pretty clearly hinted in the evidence of
survivor Thorn, at a very early stage of the pro-
ceedings. Said Thorn, “I was awakened by a
bumping like the racing of the propeller, then
there was a sharp crash, and shortly afterwards I
saw the water coming into the saloon. The noise
appeared to be aft.” (Age, 21/4/’00.) So that in
all probability either a blade of the propeller
snapped, or the splitting crank-shaft broke, and
the broken machinery crashed through the brown-
paper hull, sending the vessel to the bottom. The
verdict of the Court exonerates the owners and
the surveyors and all the live people, and makes
tilings smooth all round ; and if the dead people
have any objections to make the Court probably
thinks that they ought to get up and make them,
and not skulk in an un-British fashion at the sea-
bottom and think evil of their betters.

The A.M.P. Extension Fad: A Last
Word.
To-morrow (Friday, 20th July) the A.M.P.
holds its meeting to decide whether or not the
directors shall be authorised to extend the society’s
business to England, India (where the chronic
plague is), Ceylon, Burmah, South Africa,
Canada, Newfoundland, Sierra Leone (the White
Man’s Grave), Lagos, Gambia, and other more or
less unwholesome places now belonging to Britain,
also every place, however unwholesome, that
Britain may acquire hereafter. The directors
will doubtless say that they don’t propose to ex-
tend the business to those parts of the Empire
where men perish like flies, and where plague,
cholera, yellow fever, and the like are always
more or less in evidence. And they probably
don’t. But all the same they demand full
authority for themselves and their successors to
blew the A.M.P.’s funds in all these places,
and however much they don’t intend to use
that authority now they may change their
minds, or their successors may be of a different
way of thinking. If A asks B to formally endow
him with a power which, if A likes to use it to the
full, is liable to be utterly ruinous to B, then how-
ever hard A may swear that he doesn’t intend to
use the power, it is safer not to let him have it.
The mere fact that he is so intensely anxious to
get it is suspicious. And the reasons chiefly
assigned by directors for demanding authority
to do obviously disastrous things is ridicu-
lously insufficient. They are mainly : (1)
That the death-rate isn’t much worse in Eng-
land than here (no one dares to profess
that it is better, or as good); (2) that the
interest to be earned on investments in England
isn’t so very much less than here ; (3) that the
N.S.W. Government recently floated a 4 per cent,
loan in London, and if the A.M.P. had had an
office there the directors might have secured part
of it, but not having an office, and not having
sufficient presence of mind to instruct their exist-
ing London agent to tender, and not knowing
there was a cable available, they didn’t. Britain
is one of the healthiest parts of the British empire
outside Australasia, and the fact that no British
office earns such good interest as the A.M.P. does,
or has such a favorable mortality experience, or is
able to pay such splendid bonuses, j.re sufficient
proofs that a large admixture of British business
is almost certain to make the average interest rate
lower all round, and the average, death-rate higher,
and the average bonuses less. The argument con-
stantly advanced by the Board’s journalistic bar-
I’acker, the Sydney Telegraph, is that “ the direc-
tors must know best.” It is a silly argument, and
two-edged as well. The almost-purely-Australian
directors of the A.M.P. doubtless do know bast
about life-assurance business in Australia. It
doesn’t necessarily follow that they know best, or
that they know anything in particular, about life-
assurance business in England —which is the sub-
ject under discussion. Where, for instance, is
Joseph Abbott’s experience of English life-
assurance business, and when were most of his
colleagues in it last? The people who should
know best about British life assurance are the
men who run the great English and Scottish life
offices. They say, by their published returns,
that the best interest they can earn is less than
the A.M.P. earns ; that the best bonuses they can
pay are less than the A.M.P. pays ; that the best
death-rate they can showis worse than the A.M.P.
shows; and that, even to show these inferior
results, they have mostly to charge higher rates
than the A.M.P. charges. Either the A.M.P. is
going to England to produce the same results on its
English business as good English offices do, in which
case the less profitable character of its English
business must reduce the profits all round. Or
else the mostly-Australian directors of the A.M.P.
are going to England to teach their grandmother
how to come in out of the wet, and to show the
English life assurance managers that none of them
know how to run their business in their own coun-
try. As for the large, wild, promiscuous demand for
authority to do business in all other parts of the
British Empire—in all its tropical African and
Asiatic plague-spots —the only defence advance-
able is that the Board doesn’t mean to use the
power if it gets it. And if this writer harassed
Director Abbott for a blank signed cheque, and
the only reason he could show why Mr. Abbott
should give it was that it would never be filled in
and presented or used in any way, probably Mr.
Abbott would say to him, as he now says to Mr.
Abbott : “Why do you want it so badly, then? ”

A Very Small Patch.
The Bill for the reformation of Sydney’s municipal
government has made its appearance in N.S.W.
Assembly, and it is a woeful disappointment. It
provides for a Mayor and 22 aldermen, instead of
24 as heretofore. The Mayor is to be elected by
the people, which is good. The Council is to be
wholly re-elected every two years instead of on
the instalment plan as heretofore, which is also
good. The city auditor is also to be elective. The
franchise is to be enlarged, and the new Council is to
have its powers so far extended that it can resume
land in order to widen, straighten and improve
streets, make new streets, and otherwise beautify
the city. And there the good points about end.
The plural vote is so far retained that a citizen who
has property in each of the 11 wards can vote 11
times—once in each ward. And the Bill applies to
the City of Sydney only. There are 41 munici-
palities which make up the real Sydney, and
Premier Lynk proposes to reform one of them
in a small way, and let the other 40 slide
in the good, old, dirty, incompetent fashion. They
are not to have any reform, and Premier LynE
evidently thinks that their highways and bve-
ways don’t require improvement. The city
government can improve its streets if it likes
until it comes to the place where Redfern or Pad-
dington commences, and there the reform is to
stop short. There is nothing in the measure to
hasten the consolidation of the 41 municipal ink-
spots into a real city under a sensible government
system. It doesn’t enact that all current expenses
shall be paid out of revenue, or prohibit the
disastrous old practice of turning revenue-
si lortages into permanent debt. It doesn’t
Erovide full authority to construct public
aths, wash-houses, model dwellings, and all the
other thousand and one things that up-to-date
municipal governments in Great Britain busy
themselves with. In addition to utterly ignoring
40 out of the 41 Sydney and suburban muni-
cipalities it leaves all the country ones
severely alone. It doesn’t extend the area
of municipal government—in fact, it is the
smallest and most gradual piece of reform that
could well be imagined. Yet our late friend, the
plague, broke out in Paddington, Balmain, Manly
—last week it was still at Manly—and other
municipalities as well as in the city of Sydney;
and people died of it in these places as well as in
Sydney ; and the municipal governments of these
places proved about as hopeless and as unclean as
that of Sydney; and, strange as it may seem,
people in these regions, when they die, are quite
as dead as the people of the city, and remain dead
quite as long, and object to dying quite as much.
Lyne’s attempt to- put a patch the size of a
postage-stamp on a hole as large as the Jenolan
caves is pathetic. It is good so far as it goes, but
the spectacle of so very large a man wasting his
time on so very small a patch is suggestive of an
elephant running after a f'y.

Concerning a Small Bank.
Dumbell’s Bank (Isle of Man, England) went
smash recently. It was founded in 1874, and had
£50,000 paid-up capital and £40,000 reserve-fund.
The dividend for the last year of its existence was
18 per cent., and as late as last October the shares
(£2 p lid up) were selling at £3 2s. 6d. There was
nothing wrong with Dumbell’s Bank except that
one director and two managers appear to have
embezzled £500,000 (all the capital and reserve
and over £400,000 of the deposits) and the audi-
tors said nothing about it. It was the City of
Glasgow Bank over again ; there, also, practically
the whole of the assets were stolen and the audi-
tors said nothing about it. The curse of the
private-audit system is that the auditors never
say anything about IT. Among all the
Australasian financial institutions which went
hopelessly broke between ’9O and ’94, after issuing
beautifully prosperous balance-sheets right up to
the day of closing their doors, there wasn’t one
case within The Bulletin’s recollection where
the auditors didn’t cheerfully cert i fy to every-
thing as being all right to the very last. And
nobody was surprised. Nobody expected any-
thing else. It is the private auditor’s business,
his habit, his tradition, his bread-and-butter, to
certify to any balance-sheet that is laid before
him —to do it mechanically in short, to be an
automaton with an autograph. He never inquires
into the value of the assets but just takes them at
the price set against them ; if a shortage of a mil-
lion is balanced by putting in as an asset the pro-
note of Ptolemy Physcon, 2000 years overdue at
the Bank of Egypt, it is all one to the average
private auditor. If he asks awkward questions
the directors won’t employ him (it is really the
directors, in most cases, who appoint auditors,
though the shareholders think they do it), and he
will starve. If he asks no questions he can make
a living, and the prospect of being sent to gaol
when the smash comes is very remote after all.
Until there is a State audit by State-appointed
and State-paid officials, whose bread-and-dripping
depends on telling the solid truth, not on
pleasing directors, not one auditor’s certificate in
100,000 will be worth more than its selling price
at the rag-and-bone store. There is no other
remedy. Even the most intelligent, honest, and
outspoken newspapers can’t warn the public
against such frauds as those of Dumbell’s
Bank and others like it. They can only
judge by the printed balance-sheets, and by
such further information as is accessible to
them. If the balance-sheets are faked with
sufficient boldness and the secret of the swindle is
known only to a few men who keep their know-,
ledge as silent as the grave, and don’t drink or* 1
talk in their sleep, there is nothing to go upon.
When these men have gone so far that a huge
smash is certain, and they must either bolt or go
to gaol, and it is merely a question of keeping '
things going till they are ready to bolt, there is
very little that they can’t do. Apropos of which
The Bulletin appends a possible sample balance-
sheet :
The Bank op Anywhere, Limited.
Liabilities. Assets.
Capital paid-up, £2,000,000 Cash .. .. £1,375,000
Beserve fund.. 1,000,000 Advances .. 5,625,000
Deposits, &c. .. 5,000,000 Property, &c. 1,106,000
Profit .. .. 175,000 Owing by other
banks .. 69,000
£8,175,000 £8,175,000
On the strength of that the Bank of Anywhere
can pay a half-year’s dividend at the rate of 10 per
cent, per annum (£100,000) and add £75,000 to
the reserve, and make a glorious boom. And
all that is necessary is to have £lOO,OOO of the
deposits left to pay the dividend with, and per-
haps £500,000 to meet sudden withdrawals. It
really doesn’t matter, provided the directors have
nerve enough, and are secretive enough, and have
their carpet-bags packed in good time, whether
the other £7,575,000 have been embezzled or not.
The private auditors will never find out the dis-
crepancy, or if they do it is practically certain
that they won’t mention it. The great City of
Glasgow Bank travelled on balance-sheets like
these for about a dozen years after the inside had
been eaten clean out of it; the auditors said no-
thing, and the public, the shareholders, and the
financial papers suspected nothing. Even its own
staff didn’t know. It ranked, until the very day
it closed its doors, almost as high as such institu-
tions as the Australasia or the Union, or the B.
N.S.W., and its shares were bought at high prices
till the very last. Against a huge and utterly
reckless swindle so ably conducted there is no pro-
tection —except a State audit.

The New State Constitutions.
Only one Australian Government —that of South
Australia —has begun to seriously consider the re-
casting of its State Constitution in view of the
near approach of the Commonwealth. When the
Federal Parliament takes over a great deal of the
heavy dragging and dead lift of legislation, with
the prospect that it may take over still more in
the near future, the States obviously want to run
their separate Government machines on a smaller
scale. They want fewer Ministers, fewer depart-
ments, a smaller and cheaper Governor, less pomp,
less expensive frill, not so many ornamental
officers, smaller Legislatures—possibly consisting
of only one House each —and many other altera-
tions. In South Australia the Government’s idea
is, instead of cutting down the old big garment, to
make a smaller new one, and it urges that
the people should elect a Convention to
draft a complete new State Constitution
just as a previous Convention drafted one for
the new Commonwealth. It is a pity that all
the States don’t adopt the same idea. It is a still
greater pity that they can’t agree for all Australia
to elect a Convention by manhood suffrage, as the
Federal Convention was elected, and let it draw
up six new State Constitutions on practically uni-
form lines. No doubt it would be a shock to the
awful dignity of each State that the leading men
of other provinces should have any hand in draft-
ing the Constitution under which its purely inter-
nal affairs are to be managed, but after all there is
no reason why they shouldn’t get over the blow.
Seeing that some kind of new Constitutions are
wanted, or the old ones need repair, to suit the
altered circumstances, the object is plainly to get
the best possible new Constitutions, or the best
possible repairs. Therefore it is advisable to get the
best possible men to do it —and even the largest of
these very small communities is none too well
off for constructive statesmen. No one can deny
that when Victoria or S.A. wants a new Constitu-
tion such men as Wise, Barton, R. E.
O’Connor and Lyne could give some valuable
assistance at drafting it, and that when N.S.W.
wants one, such outside authorities as Turner,
Holder, Peacock, Kingston and Eysh are
better Constitution-makers than any five who
would be likely to form the tail-end of a purely
local gathering of, say, 40 politicians. Even John
Forrest would be vastly better than any probable
extreme-tail-end man in a purely local convention.
By.doing the opposite to what he advised it would
be hardly possible to do amiss; in such an assem-
blage a man who can be relied on to be always
wrong is just as valuable as one who is always
right. The idea of starting Australia afresh with
six absolutely identical Constitutions might be
objected to by some on the ground of monotony,
but if the monotony arose through all the six
being practically perfect it might be endured.
Moreover, the process of divergence would quickly
set in again. It was such a Convention which
drew up the Federal Constitution —described by
Joseph Chamberlain as “a monument of legis-
lative competence.” The States badly want
provincial Constitutions which will also be monu-
ments of legislative competence, and this appears,
judging by experience, to be the way to get them.
Public Office and Private Business.
The average politician who takes office endea-
vours to make the best of both worlds. If he is
dependent on a business or profession, he can’t
afford to leave it; for his office may suddenly
leave him, and he will have neither private pro-
fession nor public position. But there is a wide
difference between keeping a private business
going independently of a public post, and con-
ducting a private business in defiance of the
public interest. The latter is the course frequently
taken by Parliamentary lawyers ; and the latest
lawyer to adopt it is N.S.W. Postmaster-General
Crick. Mr. Crick is at once lawyer and member
of the Cabinet; and he appears in court
before Judges and Acting-Judges, whose
positions or careers may at any time
have to be settled by the Cabinet; and
before police-magistrates who feel that, despite
the Public Service Board, their positions and
promotions may still be greatly influenced by
Cabinet approval or disapproval of their conduct.
(If the Cabinet has no influence upon police-
magistrates, it is a singular thing that the special
commissions entrusted to “stalwart” magistrates
seem to be invariably executed as the Cabinet
wishes.) More, when a Crown-Prosecutor is
sent to plead a case, Mr. Crick may oppose him.
The Crown-Prosecutor knows that it is to Mr.
Crick’s interest to be successful; he knows that
Postmaster-General Crick is a member of the
Cabinet which may at any time have a Judgeship
in its gift ; and what does the Crown-Prosecutor
do? His duty, presumably; but Mr. Crick makes
his duty very hard for him—particularly if the
vacancy on the bench is very near and. very lucra-
tive, and he wants it very badly. Plainly Parlia-
ment should step in to prevent public servants
being placed in delicate positions where their
interests may be opposed to their duty; and
where, consequently, there is room in doubtful
cases for a public suspicion that their duty has
yielded to their interests. There may be no
ground whatever for the suspicion, but if it can
even exist, that is sufficient reason for taking
action to prevent it. The mirror of Justice can
be tarnished by a breath. But there is no need
at this day to pile arguments in proof that the
judiciary should be absolutely divorced from the
legislature. And the lower courts of justice
are no less important than the higher. A police
magistrate should be made as independent as a
Supreme Court Judge—responsible only to Par-
liament. A Minister of the Crown should not be
allowed to plead in opposition to the Crown,
making the Crown Prosecutor handle his case with
gloves, lest his promotion suffer. And Parliament
should end the existing scandal at once.
A minor Victorian Judge had to deal recently
with a well-dressed man who had inflicted brutal
injuries on his wife—among the rest cutting
her head to the bone with a poker. The
case was fully proved, and then the Judge opened
out like this : “You behaved in a shameful and
cowardly way, and there are no circumstances of
palliation in your case. Nevertheless, as you are
apparently a respectable person, I will make the
sentence something lighter than I otherwise
would.” Now even the most foolish Judge must
surely know that a man who beats his wife isn’t
respectable. What was obviously meant was: ‘ You
are a respectably-dressed person, and I will let you
off more lightly on account of your clothes without
asking whether you came by them honestly or not.
If you had come here in bad clothes, even though it
was through paying your debts with rigid honesty
that you were reduced to wearing such seedy
garments, this Court would have fallen on you
like an avalanche.” The law is always a con-
scious or unconscious respecter of Clothes. The
male accused or prisoner with a good suit and a
shiny hat and new gloves and a collar that
isn’t frayed, and the female with a dainty
dress, and glossy hair tastefully done up,
and something beautifully soft and frilly
at the neck and wrists, will always have a
big start of those whose means don’t run to
it, and £5 judiciously expended on clothes may
easily be the hair-weight which, in an intensely
doubtful case, turns the scale as between acquittal
and five years in gaol. It would make justice a
great deal purer if all prisoners were tried in an
absolutely nude state, and witnesses gave evidence
in the same condition (the dock and witness-box
being each supplied with a gas-heater), or if they
were all required to wear long dressing-gowns of
exactly the same pattern. Also,. all lady
witnesses or accused persons might have
their heads disguised in a regulation sun-bonnet
of the sort you see at the Benevolent Asylums
(the State 'to supply these things and to
guarantee that each bonnet and dressing-gown
was washed, boiled and disinfected after use) so
that the Court might no longer unconsciously
lean towards the side whioh had the ravishing
hair-knob. Even then, as the State couldn t well
furnish complexions and artificial teeth, the
parties wouldn’t start quite fair, but they would
get much nearer to it than they do now.
Even the recent news that the Chinese had flayed
a British subject alive and gouged out his eyes
with red-hot irons, and that the fate of the
British population at Pekin was so doubtful that
no one really knew for certain that it hadn’t all
been flayed alive, and that the British at Tien-
tsin were hard-pressed by the enemy, didn’t
raise in Australia the semblance of any really
strong desire to aid the Mother-country in its hour
of trouble. The people who howled with such
unspeakable rage when a Briton wasn’t allowed to
vote in the Transvaal don’t rage worth a copper
about the Briton who is horribly tortured m
Chinkie-land. Even Fitzgibbon is silent as yet.
Surely it can’t be that the whole show of enthusi-
asm for tire Flag, and the alleged sym; athy for
the oppressed Briton who hadn’t a vote, were all
a hypocrisy or a steal or a lie or a
delusion or a drunk—that the rush of volunteers
to the Transvaal arose solely from an idea that
there were land-grants and gold-deposits and loot
there, and that the indifference to the Mother-
country’s troubles in China, and to the insults
heaped on the Flag, and the wrongs of the flayed
Briton, arises from the fact that China is already
too crowded, and there are no land-grants in view,
and no known gold-mines, and no prospective
loot. And if this isn’t the reason —well, why is
Australia so wild about the fellow-subject who
lost his vote, and so tame about the fellow-subject
who lost his skin ? One thing is quite sure : Aus-
tralia is already weary of its new plaything, War.
Ever since the sick and wounded began to dribble
back and extra-taxation commenced to loom near,
you could perceive that tired feeling in the air.

Lieut.-Colonel Burns’s official statement that a
N.S.W. Volunteer officer’s uniform costs £lOO,
and that, consequently, only well-to-do men, or
men with well-to-do friends, can take such ap-
pointments, not only does not excuse the recent
nominations, but reveals a fixed official intention
to keep the higher grades of the service for a
money-caste. Either (Parliament must give the
uniform gratis, or prescribe a modest, small-
priced uniform, or the service will snap in two at,
probably, the very time it will be wanted. A
department which now fixes on a £lOO uniform
will, if let go unchecked, prescribe a £2OO one, or
more, if occasion to serve the ends of extra-gilt
johnnydom presents itself. A £5OO uniform —that
would be as prohibitive as was the old army-pur-
chase system. Will some democratic politician
see to this matter —and keep on seeing to it.

Plain Bill Lyne’s Government is making a good
beginning in the way of democratic legislation.
Another of its ideas is to create a fund for the
relief of persons injured in mining accidents, and
for the support of the widows and orphans of
those killed in this fashion. Mining is rather more
dangerous than war, so the need of some such
measure is obvious enough. Every miner is to
pay a license-fee of 10s. a year. Every mine-
owner is to pay a similar amount, and the Trea-
sury contributes another 10s. per head annually.
Allowances are to be 12s. a week for total per-
manent disablement ; in case of death, Bs. a week
to the widow while she remains unmarried,
or if there is no widow, to the guardian of the
orphaned children; and 2s. 6d. a week for each
child till it reaches the age of 14. If the deceased
was unmarried similar allowances are made for his
mother or his small sisters or brothers if they were
dependent on his earnings. Curiously enough
there is no provision in case of temporary or per-
manent partial disablement, which seems a serious
omission. Temporary disablement may last for
months, in which case it is almost as serious as.
permanent incapacitation.

defect more than another it is that the whole
eight members are, or believe they are, pastoral
experts. Ferguson, who is one of the two Labor
representatives, makes perhaps the least preten-
sions of this kind. It will therefore be a pretty
hard job for the board (of eight) to arrive at a
unanimous decision on the evidence, because,

Donald Macdonald—Victorian Tour.
The “ Absent Minded Beggar,” which
took the incoherent floor on Saturday at Sydney
Royal, proved a rather silly and weak-kneed com-
plication on which much high-priced scenery has
been recklessly wasted. Shirley’s heart was evi-
dently not in the work when he wrote it; for
business reasons he felt bound to seize the flying
scalp-lock of opportunity as it passed, so he
jumbled together a flat, dismal, and nefarious pot-
boiler, and cast it at the head of a maddened
public. Coming to Australia when the war-fever
is cooling down, and looked at in cold blood, the
awfulness of that pot-boiler strikes the eye like a
falling tower. Shirley’s Boers are all coarse and
hairy fiends, who propose to murder the British
women and children wholesale—all save the
heroine, who is to be reserved for a worse fate.
As all reports show the Boers to have treated their
prisoners with reasonable kindness, this is evidently
put in as a tribute to British fair play. The Boers
don’t really do anything to their prisoners, being
interrupted by armoured trains and other imple-
ments, but their intentions are strictly dishonor-
able. Hem Baker, in the first act, has no visible
occupation, but lives on a distant relative. In the
second act, the distant relative having thrown
him over, he and his wife and infant are tem-
porarily hanging out in London on the premises
of an impoverished friend—where a Boer tries
to steal the baby. Later on Baker manfully
borrows some money and goes to S’ Africa, where
he does deeds of heroism, but as the end of the
war leaves him still with no honest occupation, he
possibly ends his days in the fruit-barrow busi-
ness. In melodrama the utterly impoverished
hero enlists, disembowels his country’s enemy,
and then embraces his wife and remarks, as the
curtain falls, that their troubles are now over,
and the average audience takes it as a perfectly
satisfactory ending. Yet, as a man can’t live all
his life by eating one dead Boer, the material
improvement is less visible than it might be.
* • • •
All the acts drag heavily, but the first one,
especially, goes on feet of lead. The scene is on
the premises of a flag-manufacturer in London, '
which gives the hired girls an opportunity to
cheer wildly for the Flag while it is in process of 1
Albert Norman, as a Boer, drops
in and curses the Flag promiscuously, and asks
the manufacturer’s daughter to marry him. She
can’t, being secretly married to Baker, who arrives
later, and also cheers for the Flag, and complains
because Norman has treacherously left it on the
floor. Kemp, Harford, and Mrs. Bland Holt like-
wise call in and cheer for the Flag ; so do Arthur
Styan, who is dependent on his uncle, and follows
no useful occupation, and Bland, who is got-upasa
Jew, and cheers through his nose. The flag-manu-
facturer is ruined later on. Styan, like Baker, is
thrown over by his uncle, and he and Bland
and Baker and a burglar are all Pre occupied
Mendicants together. This gathering of the men
who used to loaf on their rich relatives, and the
house-breaker (who blackens his hungry wife’s
eye before he departs and leaves her destitute), and
the Hebrew usurer, is understood to be the up-
rising of Britain against the loafing Boer slave-
owner. The audience, though much excited at
the Boer who only threatens to batter the British
female, cheers Brown, the burglar, who batters
her in reality, and evidently regards him as a
hero. The principal semblance of a plot lies in
the fact that a Boer wants Baker’s wife to elope
with him, which she declines to do for reasons
connected with the Flag. This is the same Boer
who tried to encumber himself with Baker’s baby
with a view to bringing it up in Africa on the
bottle.
* * • *
An armoured train and a review of troops
by a lady who treasonably passes herself off as our
Gracious Queen, and many kopjes and guns make
the “ A.M. Beggar” an expensive pictorial effort.
In that capacity it calls for high praise, but in a
literary sense it is as slow as a benevolent-asylum
dog on a wet day. In the absence of a single good
part, the company seems to feel its position
keenly. The jokes which are put in the mouth of
Moses Holt or Bland Cohen or whatever he should
be called are weary chestnuts about marriage
and pork. The new Kruger isn’t half so bright
as Ned Holloway’s late Kruger at the Criterion,
and Dampier’s “Briton and Boer” was at least
alive, whereas the “A.M. Beggar” is deader than
the procrastinating animal that was left out of the
Ark. Charles Brown’s repentant burglar, who
beats his wife and resolves to lead a better life, is
about the most live character in the collection.
*# * *
The War Pictures pursue their delusive
way at Sydney Lyceum to the reasonable satis-
faction of an audience which evidently doesn’t
much mind whether the sensation is an obvious
fake or not. Yet the fake is at times a trifle too
outrageous. The. great sensation of the show—
the wiping out of Major Wilson’s little force by
the Matabele—suffers not only from the slight
drawback that the foe would have torn the
Ehotographer in eight pieces if he had been there,
ut from the further disadvantage that the event
happened (speaking from the writer’s memory) a
year or two before the kinematographe got into real
workingorder. The Bulletin has even doubts now
about the veracity of the alleged armoured train ;
for all the evidence to the contrary the view might
represent any single-track American railway and the
alleged armoured car looks very like the bare-
sided van which carries the mails on any ordinary
American train. And it has met a man who is
prepared to swear, for a reasonable compensation
in beer, that the alleged inarch of the troops
through Cape Town was really a perfectly peace-
ful proceeding that happened in Manchester. As
you don’t see enough of the town to make it iden-
tifiable, and troops look much the same anywhere,
and the original man who took the photo, would
probably swear to anything, and The Bulletin’s
informant would swear to anything for beer, it is
impossible, unfortunately, to bring this discussion
to a satisfactory conclusion.

The “Rose of Persia,” by Basil Hood
and Arthur Sullivan, is to be staged at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney, on Saturday. Whatever
Hood’s libretto may be like, Sullivan’s music is
generally a thing which any civilised being is glad
to meet. In any case, it is long since Sydney saw
an absolutely new opera, and there are signs
already that the reception of the Rose will be
great. For the first night d.c. and reserved
stall seats will be 2s 6d extra, the odd half-
crown going to put some meat on our famished
brown brother on India’s coral strand. Coral being
uneatable, said brother is in a bad way just now.
Also a matinee for the benefit of the Children’s
Hospital takes place on Friday, 27th July.

Rickards’ Sydney company still meets at
Caddam Woods in the shirty costumes and large
bibs that have become so familiar. Baby CapeHs
a well-grown infant, though not up to Fanny
Powers in vocal sweetness. Alma Gray suffers
from her songs, her tastes being for the mawkish.
Emmonds, Einmersonand Emmonds continue their
farcical sketch, but as they do their clever turns
all at one and the same moment, even the
cross-eyed man in the audience has to lose
one whole Emmonds-Eminerson, which is dis-
appointing. The Animatographe toils through its
turn without any great signs of nervousness.
These inventions are improving. In the first
Cinematographe this writer saw, a man, supposed
to jump out of a window, floated down slowly and
gently, and stuck fast in the atmosphere before he
got to the foot. Madame Cordelia has lately
blossomed out in a new song, an example that
might be followed, and Wallace King has done
ditto with an old one. “She wore a wreath of
roses” belongs to the period when present bald-
heads were young and innocent, and had not yet
painted their noses in the garish hues of Sin, and
