Donald Macdonald, now ravaging Melbourne
suburbs with his Ladysmith lectures, will do a
five nights’ season at the Centennial Hall, Sydney,
commencing on Saturday, 28th July. Mac. is
coining money in Melb., meanwhile he puts on
flesh at a most inartistic rate. That’s what grieves
business manager Geach the steadily-increasing
bulk of a star who lives by telling tales of suffering
and starvation. Tne gaunt Mac. who landed in
Melbourne from S’Africa not many weeks ago
was a fine stage-effect, anyhow, whereas the new
Mac. looks very like a lot of other men who get
entertained at luncheon by country corporations.
** * *
At Melbourne Princess’s, the virtues of
H. A. Jones’s comedy get about as much ap-
preciation as can be expected of a public that does
little thinking for itself. “ Manceuvres of Jane ”
belongs to the intellectual order of stage things,
and, although it lias to be farcical to live, the best
jokes in the piece are not very obtrusive. That is
to say, they are not the jokes an overfed person
in the ironmongery-line would laugh at unless he
were forewarned of their whereabouts. Mr. and
Mrs. Brough, as they appear after getting washed
ashore, seem (to the present writer) considerably
less humorous than when they go to interview
model pigs in the first act, but, judging from the
roar of the populace, Mr. and Mrs. B. are merely
acting up to their best business-principles in the
cottage scene, where they exaggerate the truth all
the time. The representative of the hardware-
trade must be catered for, or he would stop at
home and breathe heavily in his armchair. It is
for the sake of him and his likes that Constantia
Gage leans so heavily on Lord Bapchild’s chest
whilst the pigeon-toed peer fights for a foothold
with his hind leg.
• * • •
The charm of Miss Noble’s Jane grows on
acquaintance. Jane is interesting from the
moment of her first angry “ Howdyerdo,” to her
last sweet smile at the chaperon she has manoeu-
vred. Her transactions with Pamela are fresh
and vigorous, likewise her rage at having had her
19iin. waist squeezed by the man at the Magpie
pub. Jane quarrels prettily with the tall, un-
yielding steward, and she forgives him prettily
when he declines to be bossed, and altogether she
is a valuable object lesson for the girls of
Federated Australia. Miss Martheze, as the
creepy Pamela of tender years, continues to
occupy a place of honour at the key-
hole, and as regards all the other people who
were praised in last issue it may be said that they
are not more acceptable than some—Leslie Victor,
MTntyre, Miss Rochefort and Miss Temple—
whose names didn’t get a mention. The objection
to W. T. Lovell’s polite rigidity is now partly
withdrawn, but as a set-off against this concession
to Lovell the bilious scribe desires to complain of
Reginald Dartrey’s vocal powers. Dartrey’s tones
are loud enough to fill two theatres at once, and
as he is otherwise an admirable member of an
artistic co. he may as well be advised to draw his
voice mild in ordinary conversation.

“ Camille ” is doing a good second week at
Her Majesty’s (Melb.). Dumas’giddy drama would
pay a third weekly dividend no doubt if the
management hadn’t formed a philanthropic reso-
lution to produce Nance O’Neil in a round of
characters for the sake of the public, wherefore
she will be called upon to Peg Woffington next
Saturday. Camille is Miss O’Neil’s most likeable
effect in Melbourne thus far, but it doesn’t look
like the truth. The actress plays to suit Nance
O’Neil, without much artistic regard for the
woman in the story. Dumas’ heroine was a deli-
cate, dainty young person, who so strikingly re-
sembled a French duke’s daughter that his Nibs
fixed her up in handsome apartments, and went
to gaze upon her once a week with a fond fatherly
eye. There’s nothing of the dock’s daughter about
Nance’s lady of the Camellias; she’s just an ordinary
easy-goer multiplied by two. At the beginning
she is excessively callous, and later she grows too
sentimental, and when she goes in for grief she
gets so upset that she has to sit on the floor and
rock herself. And when she sobs face-downwards
on the sofa the rise and fall of her emotion is
about 18in. for each sob. Camille’s death-scene is
not over-colored (barring a melodramatic blue
light on the corpse’s face), but there the positive
necessity for moderation is obvious. A 15-stone
Camille has to be very careful how she falls a
victim to phthisis. In the present show, Miss
Nance gets very moderate support from the co.—
Kingston, Carne, Mrs. Bracy and Barton Hill
being, three of them stagey, and the other one
tame. Barton Hill plays old Duval. The printer,
in last week’s Bulletin, called him old Daniel,
and the wrong name fitted him fairly well.
#* * *
Paul Martinetfci, with his pantomime co.,
started a speechless career at Melb. Bijou early
last week, and the success of this novel entertain-
ment is apparently solid. In “ Robert Macaire ”
the co. gets a formal introduction through a
printed argument. The argument itself is not a
brilliant business circular, but it helps to put the
audience on friendly terms with the characters
before the panto, arrives, and it curves for a use-
ful advt. after being taken home in the audience’s
pocket. Besides, “Robert Macaire ”is a good
name to work upon, for nearly everybody remem-
bers to have heard of Robert at one time or other,
and hardly anybody can quote the historical bear-
ings of the man. Even the argument omits to
explain why a general knowledge of Macaire’s
dingy past should be taken for granted by news-
paper leader-writers. Paul Martinetti and Co.,
plus auxiliaries and scenery, provide a 50 minutes
show which suffers from weak spots in the first
part, and is a stirring comic tragedy at the other
end. All notable pantomimists have been tragic-
ally disposed, and Paul Martinetti ranks as a
master of the craft. The final somersault that
lays him stiff across the stummick of his brother
Alfred is something to see. Melbourne will put
BP with a little superfluous gesture and wire-
drawn humor from an actor who can achieve the
weird effect of kicking himself off his mortal coil.

At Melb. Royal the “Two Little Drummer
Boys ” will beat a retreat on Friday. Their sex
was not what it professed to be, but under the
circumstances they did very well in holding out
for three weeks. On Saturday the weepful drama
of “The World against Her” will be staged
at _ the Royal, and if the management
revives the long-ago advts. of the Dampier
production, which filled the old Aleck with
grief and woe and orange-peel for about
12 weeks, it is probable that myriads of local fe-
males will come along and shake their heads as
before. Human nature doesn’t alter much in a
few years. The percentage of aggrieved females
remains about the same, also the number of men
who have got to take them where they can drop a
tear. “ The World Against Her ”is a remarkably
frequent opportunity for tear-drops.

has lost his only son Lieut. Kenneth Keith Mac-

Tom Glassey, who has succeeded at the
Bundaberg poll, is said to have toiled for years
m Scotch coalmines. Not for years. At an early
age Tom became a “justice man,’’that is, checker
of the miners’ out-put. His toil in Scotland was
thenceforth to mark up the toil of others.
The Yankee missionaries at Pekin sent
the following appeal to N.Y. World (run by that
clever Hebrew, Joseph Pulitzer) on June 10th
{vide Sydney D.T.. July 10th)
“ Arouse the Christian world immediately to our peril.
Should this arrive too late, avenge us."
Avenge us,” cry the Christian missionary mar-
tyrs of to-day. How like their Divine Master,
who cned, “Father, forgive them, for they know
not what they do.”
Commandant Hixson bobbed-about a good
deal in his recent reason-giving why N.S.W.
Naval Brigade men didn’t volunteer worth a cent
for service in China. The small pay—ss. a day,
as against Victoria’s 7s. fid. —was, he said first,
the principal reason ; then he bethought him that
most of the men are married and could not leave
at a moment’s notice; and finally he settled
down, by way of bed-rock, upon the fact that,
after all, the force was only sworn to defend its
own shores. If there were any need to go behind
the second of these reasons, the third consider-
ation should be an effectual clincher.
Someone long resident in China has been
ridiculing to a Sydney D.T. interviewer, that
Japan wants to “ preserve the integrity of China,”
and says that, though Russia is certain to come
out of the situation with the whole of Manchuria
and Mongolia—about a quarter of the Chinese
Empire—Japan wants to take as much, or more,
for herself, and is merely playing off the other
powers against Russia in order to “ come cranking
in” at the end and “ scoop the pool.” And Japan,
asserts this authority, would in that case be found
more aggressive and dangerous, as far as Australia
is concerned, than even Russia herself.
Died in Sydney at the age of 87, loveable
old John Rae, M.A., first Town Clerk of Sydney.
His Australian history dates back to 1839.
He was an Aberdonian, and was educated at
Marischal College, in his native city. His con-
nection with the civic government of Sydney ex-
tended over 15 years, when he entered the service
of the genera] Government, and was successively
Secretary to the Railway Department, Under-
secretary for Railways, Commissioner for
Railways, and Under-Secretary for Works.
He retired in 18S8. The deceased had keen
artistic and literary perceptions, and his collection
of views of old Sydney is one of the best extant.
Mr. Rae on the night of his death retired in
apparently his usual health and cheerful spirits;
he passed away gently in his sleep. He was a
charming odd man who to his last hour retained a
boyish gaiety and a boyish aptitude for learning.
He read and was enthusiastic over Horace in his
old age, and was privileged by his cherry-like
complexion to wear a red necktie at eighty-five.
The massacre at the Pekin Foreign Lega-
tions—now no longer, unfortunately, a matter of
doubt—is about the greatest horror that has oc-
curred since the Indian Mutiny Cawnpore-
slaughter, to which, by-the-way, the Chinese
affair bears some resemblance in the immolation
of the unfortunate women and children—though
in this case it was at the hands of their
dearest, to save them from the inconceiv-
able atrocities of Chinese torture. The whole
ghastly affair proves conclusively that what it
is customary to call the “ancient civilisation of
China” is, when “scratched,” but a ferocious
barbarism after all. What has the 19th century to
do with such men as those who recently, at Tien-
tsin, skinned an English missionary alive and then
burnt his eyes out with hot irons ? It is claimed
for Christianity that it would alter this ;
but proselytism in China has touched but the
merest fringe of the people, and can never
hope to make any appreciable impression on the
countless hordes in the background—fast bound in
the adamantine conservatism of the East, frantic
with immemorial tradition, and imbued with a
native ferocity which has no equal upon the face
of the earth. It is those who know China best
who despair of her most.

COLUMN
The Queensland Government has floated a 3 per
cent, loan of £1,400,000 in London. It got the money by
a hair’s-breadth, the whole amount tendered being only
£1,411,000. The price was £94, less 255. per cent, for
underwriting, which reduces it to £92 15s. ; and expenses
which will probably be from £1 to £1 10s. per cent. ; so
the Queensland Government will get less than £92. In
other words, it gets about £1,250,000 and has to repay
£1,400,000. Therefore it appears that Queensland’s credit
is barely good enough to enable it to borrow at 3J per
cent, just now. Under these conditions, if it must borrow,
the honest process is either to float a 3£ per cent,
loan at par, or, if it floats a 3 per cent, loan at a loss, to
make the loss good by instalments out of revenue. On
the present loan Queensland saves a few shillings per
cent, per annum in interest by adding about £150,000 to
its permanent debt, which is just the same fraud
as if it openly paid part of the interest out of loans.
In fact it is absolutely a case of paying part of
the interest out of the loan itself. If Queensland went
a step further and floated a 2 per cent loan at £75 or a 1
per cent, loan at £4O and thereby saved more interest in
the present by adding more to the permanent debt in the
future, ora nothing per cent, loan at t'2o thereby adding
all the interest to the permanent debt, the public would
perhaps begin to dimly realise the nature of this political
and financial swindle.
Premier Philp says that the reasons why this
3} per cent, loan (it really is that or thereabouts) wasn’t
put on the local market were that the amount
(£1,400,000) was too large and because “all surplus money
in Australia is required for development.” There are the
following reasons for saying that this explanation is a
disgrace to Philp’s alleged intelligence. (1). He never
tried the local market, but as there are £23,500,000 of coin
lying idle in the banks the off-hand statement that
£1,400,000 couldn’t be got seems absurd. The local mar-
ket has been tried lately with 3 per cent, long-dated loans,
certainly, but that is another thing. It has also been
tried with a short-dated 31 per cent, loan, and rushed
it—though that is also a different thing. (2). The banks
are getting plenty of money at 3 and 3} per cent., and
the inference that the Queensland Government’s securitv
is worse than theirs is very rough on Queensland. (3)
The Queensland loan is to be used for development pur-
poses unless Premier Philp is going to waste it all, there-
fore the plea that Australian money should he used for
the development of Australia is just the reason why he
should have borrowed Australian money. At present,
the 23.| millions is not being used for development; it is
lying in the banks. The simplest explanation of the
desire to float loans in London seems to be this : In
London, the Australian Governments act on the advice of
their bankers and agents, and, if a loan is a failure, the
moral responsibility rests on the bankers and agents. In
Australia, they would have to act on their own knowledge
of the money-market, and take the responsibility of
failure themselves. And they haven’t the courage or the
confidence in their own knowledge. And Australia has
to pay for their ignorance.

At 31st March, 1900, Maoriland had 11,285 old-
age pensions in force, and the bill £193,718 annually.
The working expenses of the Pensions Department were
only £2360 for the year. According to population, there-
fore, the M’Shiels Govt, should be able to establish a
similar old age pension system in Victoria for about
£300,000 or £330,000 a year. Victoria pays £340,000 a
year in pensions now, but they are not to the aged poor.

The N.S.W. Auditor-General’s report for the
year ended 30th June, 1899, is just to hand—it usually
takes 12 months to produce this profoundly intricate
document. One notable feature in it is that the N.S.W.
railways, though fast improving, are still a losing concern.
Recent results are :
31st Dec., 1892 ( year) Loss .. ..£228,362

The accumulated N.S.W. railway deficitisnow £7,844,994,
all the result of building railways with money borrowed
at a rate of interest which the lines couldn’t pay. On the
whole the N.S.W. Government lines have yielded a good
deal more than the average English rate of profit on rail-
way capital, but an enormous amount of the N.S.W. rail-
way capital was borrowed at 5 per cent, and it takes a
very prosperous railway system to earn that amount in a
very empty country.

Sydney Telegraph’s defence of the proposed
granting of power to extend the A.M.P.’s business to
England, South Africa, India, Burmah, the fever-stricken
coast of West Africa, &c. :
“We were again treated yesterday to a homily upon
the text, ‘Contract your business and flourish exceed-
ingly ; expand it, and perish.’ We were assured that
‘ Empires break down from their own weight, and in busi-
ness the theoretical advantage of extension is met by the
practical difficulty of management.’ We wonder whether
our two leading and most expanding banks, or the
greatest and most progressive insurance offices of the
world are troubled that way. Amongst the largest com-
panies in the world are the National Provincial Bank of
England, which has amassed £50,000,000 of deposits
accumulated at 191 offices, and Arthur Guinness, Son,
and Co., Limited, which has a turnover of a great many
millions annually drawn from all quarters of the globe.
Are they to be reckoned as decaying in consequence ? If,
like Empires, they are breaking down from their own
weight, the evidences are, at any rate, hardly so striking
as they might be.”
Pity the Telegraph didn’t think of hunting for illustra-
tions a little nearer home. The City of Melbourne Bank,
and the Federal and the Mercantile all extended their
business to England on the Telegraph’s principle that
size, and bulk, and corporation, and stomach, and dropsy
are the only essentials ; and their English business killed
them. They got more deposits than they could find safe
investments for, so they had to put the money
in all kinds of shaky specs, in order to earn the interest
on these extra deposits—and when the shaky investments
broke they broke also. It was extending the business to
England that was responsible lor nearly all the bank
failures of ’93, and all the public failures as well. Banks
and other institutions went hunting for English deposits
so anxiously that in four years they brought out
£49,000,000 of them to invest in Australia, and not being
able to find sufficient good investments they put much of
the money in bad ones—and the smash followed. Now it
is proposed that the A.M.P., because it is difficult to find
enough good investments in Australia for the income it
already has, and the average interest earned has conse-
quently declined, should go to hunt for more income in
England, and send it out here to make the difficulty of
investment greater arul the average interest loiver. It is
exactly what the banks did before their trouble came.
And the Daily Telegraph, which utterly failed to see
then what the banks’ extension-to-England policy was
leading to, is equally unable to see what the A.M.P.’s
extension policy may lead to now.
Also, by the way, the Telegraph’s argument
knocks its own case badly. The National Provincial Bank
of England didn’t make its pile by spreading its business
to Australia or South America or Hong Kong, but by
sticking to its own country. And the Guinness firm did
not make its millions by opening branch breweries at the
far ends of the earth. They both show what can be done
by the policy which the A.M.P. has hitherto followed, and
which The Bulletin wants it to still follow. And the
Daily Telegraph urges it to abandon the sound example
of the big bank and the big brewery, and quit the policy
which made it and them the great and sound concerns
they are.
The Telegraph, as usual, brings its fine talent
for misrepresentation into the controversy. Here is a
sample from its issue of 6th July :
“ The vast majority of the members of the society will
think their directors know better than either those poli-
ticians or members of the legal profession (most are both)
who have agitated against a wise step being taken for
some reason or reasons which they have been quite unable
to formulate. But the only crime they can suggest is
that the society may be growing faster than Australia is
growing, and so it ought to be restricted. Well, the
restriction argument has proved vastly amusing to a
number of rival insurance offices, and probably they might
not object to see it carried into effect as thoroughly as
might be."
Thus the Telegraph accuses its opponents of
using its own argument. The extension-to-England
crowd pleads: “The A.M.P. Society is growing faster
than Australia is growing. It may not be always able
to keep bloating out at this rate. Therefore, as size is
the one essential, we should spread the business to other
parts of the British Empire.” And the anti-extension
party replies : “The A.M.P. is growing much faster than
Australia is growing—much faster than any English
office, the Prudential alone excepted—much faster than it
ever grew before. Are these results not good enough?”
They never suggested restriction. They simply suggested
that.the A.M.P. should go on as at present, and continue to
be one of the fastest-growing, most cheaply-managed, and
most profitable offices in the world, and be satisfied with
that. And the Telegraph crowd and the extension party
want to follow the example of the Commercial Bank of
Australia, which was also doing well in Australia, but nob
being satisfied went to England for money, and boomed
its deposit business in six years from 5 j millions to nearly
13 millions—and then felt very sorry afterwards. In that
case also “ the directors knew best.”

The B.N.Q. adds another £2OOO to reserve fund
this half-year, making it £BOOO, and carries forward £154.
In the days before the reconstruction reserve fund was
only £3OOO, so the management is evidently profiting by
experience and taking more care to strengthen the insti-
tution than of old. The profits, which include £9Ol
recovered from debts previously written off as bad, equal
the very handsome figure of £6 10s. 6d. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ capital and reserves; not
reckoning these recoveries the actual earnings of the half-
year are £2556, equal to £4 16s. 6d. per cent, per annum
bn the capital and reserves. The 8.N.Q.. has a large cash
reserve equal to per cent, on all liabilities to the pub-
lic, and a further reserve of £10,500 in Government stock.
The latter is a new item in the balance-sheet, and shows
a wise desire to have the reserve fund represented by
tangible assets.

The following are the results shown by the Vic-
torian State Land Bank, or Credit Foncier, or Advances'
Department of the Savings Bank, so far as it had got when
the last balance-sheet was made up :

1898 .. Profit 1,608 .. 23 17 11 .. 1 2 10'
1899 .. „ 5,547 .. 14 17 2 .. 0 13 7
Profit .. £5,693
This is very rapid progress, considering that the Credit-
Foncier had to pay interest on its entire capital from the
day it commenced business—unlike a private bank, which
seldom pays anything on its capital till its second or third
year. The Victorian State Land Bank had, at 30th June,
1899, a reserve-fund of £5697, being above profit balance
and some small interest earnings thereon. The profits
for 1898-99, after paying interest on borrowed money,
are about 16s. lid. per cent, per annum on the
average capital. At present, the State Land Bank
borrows money at 3 per cent, and lends it out at 41, but
even in its third year it had made such progress that it
could borrow at 3J and still have a good margin. And
there will soon be a serious necessity for putting up the
borrowing rate to 3}, as well as for making other reforms.
At present the great bulk of the State Land Bank’s bonds,
are taken up by the Savings Bank —the public won’t sub-
scribe to any great extent. The interest, rate is too low.
At a time when the N.&.Wi Government is giving 4 per
cent, for a short-dated loan in London, and the Queens-
land Government has just floated a long-dated London
loan that will cost it about .‘i.j per cent., the demand that
the Australian investor will be content with 3 per cent,
is especially ridiculous. The bonds are liable to be paid
off at anj* time (though the paying-off process, so far as.
the public is concerned, is temporarily suspended), and
this is eminently unpopular. The Victorian Government
had so little faith in the success of its experiment when it
founded the Land Bank that it didn’t feel confident of
always finding- mortgage investments for its borrowed
money, even at 4J per cent., and it reserved the power to
hand the money back to the lenders if the business came
to a dead stop. And the fact that trustees are not
allowed to invest trust-funds in State Land Bank securi-
ties is a grievous handicap. Yet, despite these drawbacks,
the Vic. State Land Bank is a great success, and the re-
turns for the year to 30th June, 1900, when they arrive,
will show still further progress.
* • • *
There is nothing at all bashful or retiring about
the British investor. The Imperial Congress of Chambers
of Commerce, sitting in London, proposes that all British
investments in Greater Britain should be exempted from
income-tax by the provincial Governments because
Britain also charges income-tax on them ; consequently
the Briton with money invested in N.S.W. or Victoria or
S.A. has to pay twice. The fact that it is Britain which
is doing the swindle by charging income-tax on incomes
not earned in Britain apparently doesn’t occur to these
modest individuals. Britain levies income-tax on all
incomes earned in Great Britain, even if they are the
incomes of absentees, and are spent abroad, and pay an
extra income-tax abroad. It also levies income-tax on
money' earned abroad and spent in Great Britain. And
when it doesn’t care about double-banking the income-
tax-payer, why should Australia ?

The Citizens’ Life Assurance Co. (Sydney)
issues a circular, stating that during the first half of 1899
it has largely reduced expenses, that the amount of new
business done promises to break even last year’s very
remarkable record, and that the rate of interest earned
on investments will be up to 4 per cent, by the end of the
year. The 1899 interest-rate was a very humble £3 14s.
And even 4 per cent, isn’t much.

They have ideas in the United States. There
the national banks have to deposit with the State as
security for their note issue United States 2 per cent,
bonds to the full amount of the issue. They also pay five
shillings per cent, note-tax. This works out remarkably
well for both parties. The State gets loan-money at 2 per
cent, and guarantees the payment of the notes, which it
can safely do, seeing that it holds their full value. The
banks have to keep practically no cash reserve against
their notes, for the State guarantee prevents the public
rushing for gold even in times of panic. So a bank can
turn, say, £lO,OOO of its gold into 2 per cent, bonds, and
still have the £lO,OOO (in notes which are practically as
good as gold) to lend out at interest. If it can lend out
the whole amount it reall.v, deducting the note tax, is
making £1 15s. per cent, extra profit, less the cost of
keeping its note issue in repair and plus any gain from
notes that never turn up for redemption. The State,
meanwhile, is saving at least 1 per cent. The scheme
would, probably 7, work about as well if the U.S. abolished
the note tax and made the banks deposit the amount of
their note issue in 14 per cent, bonds.
* * * •
• F. W. Browne, gen. manager of the big Aus-
tralian carrying firm of Wright, Heaton and Co., possesses
a unique record. Some ten years ago, at Melbourne, he
managed the M’Culloch Carrying Co., and even in the
face of the expiring boom paid its lucky shareholders 40
per cent, divs. He then took hold of Wright, Heaton’s
business when at low ebb as a profit-producer, and after
four years’ control —four years that synchronised with
four years of drought—he has raised the company to a
40 per cent. div.-paying power. Tub Bulletin is not
acquainted with F. W. Browne, and wouldn’t know him
if it met him in its porridge, but it is distinctly of opinion
that a man who has such a genius for contriving 40 per
cent, dividends during eras of depression is made of the
very sort of material that successful Railway Commis-
sioners are built of.
* * * •
B.H. Prop, has now got its complete concen-
trating mill at work ; it has a 10,000 ton weekly capacity.
Also the co. has got one of the imported briquetting
plants going at Port Pirie ; the object of the latter is to
mould the very fine concentrates and the lead-dust into
cubes, so that the working capacity of the smelting fur-
naces may be increased. It was the choking of the fur-
naces by the fine stuff which lately led to a serious decline
in metal output without any reduction of the fuel bill.
• * •- *
The three-year-old fire in the underground
workings of the B.H. Prop, still smoulders ; it is, of
course, now confined to a small area. There was some
good lead ore over one heavily-timbered portion lately
reached in the open cut, and at some time or other the
heat had been intense enough to melt out the metal,
which was lying in irregular blocks on the floor. At one
time a few months back workmen got close enough in to
ladle out still molten lead.
* * * *
The metallurgy-classes at Sydney Technical
College are still grossly neglected—to such an extent that
several hard-working and necessitous students who last
term paid for instruction which they did not receive
(there being no competent person on hand to give it to
them) have this term declined to pay fees.
• * • *
“ Fuze ” : Re recent remarks in Bulletin about
Cullinga (N.S.W.) field going to be good labor-employer
presently. Can’t see it ! Xmas Gift mine has worked on
its big lode for 12 months, and it is a splendid show, big
and good, and runs ten head of.stamps when water is at
hand. Yet a few face-hands are all that are needed to
keep the battery going; 15 men, all-told, on the mine.
The other prospectors along line as stated, will have their
crushings through this week. Months of labour, and
about four different parties concerned, but writer would
wager the lot will not turn out 25ozs. of gold. Tub
Bulletin is a paper looked to by miners for reliable in-
formation, so I call your attention, as par in question
might fetch men.where no employment, or very little, is
to be had. Scab-labor is already on the field— viz., £2 2s.
per week for miners ; also the other curses—contract-work
and private property. Last-named drags along with it
the sleeping-share—so this is a fairly well-cursed field.
* * » •
Adelaide Critic talks foolishness re Burraga
(N.S.W.) Copper Mine, now known as “ Lloyd’s” Copper
Co., by advising all Australian shareholders (there ave
■none) to get out of it. However, as regards Burraga the
Critic is not alone in its folly. Several mining “experts”
examined and refused the old show at less than half the
priee at which it was eventually sold. The last expert
demanded his deposit back, and threatened action
against the owner for misrepresentation ; whereas, as a
matter of absolute fact, at the very time he and others
examined and declined the mine nearly the whole of the
purchase money was represented by copper on the sur-
face in the form of dressed ore ; matte on the road, at the
mine, and at the Lithgow works ; and copper in the
bottoms of the old furnaces. One furnace alone gave up
some 70 tons of copper when it was cleaned out by the
present co., which has turned out over £50,000 worth of
metal in less than a year, and should certainly double
this production during the next. Writer would like to
give a list of the “ mining men ” who refused Burraga
mine at £35,'000 and £40,000. They are all much wiser
now.

Wertago, the resurrected copperfield, out where
the dead men lie, beyond Wilcannia, talks already of one
of The O’Sullivan’s light railways. The distance from
Tarrawingee, a present railway terminus, is about 90
miles. A syndicate is building furnaces there, so the talk
should soon be worth listening to. So far the more one
has heard about [the field the less one has known for
certain.

The Great Central Freehold at Mt. Hope, which
was reported on by Mr. A. T. “ Condemnatory ” Brown,
was underwritten by the vendors of the Intercolonial
Options Development Co., who took 68 per cent of the
shares, the guileless public taking 32 per cent. The £l5O
shares of the Great Central Freehold touched £4OOO each
in Melb. It is a case of the Chillagoe Syndicate over
again, as it is understood that several years will be needed
to bring the G.C.F. to producing-point.
* * » *
So far the Chillagoe directors have not explained
why they have sold £50,000 worth of debentures in
London at £lOO when the Melb. price, plus the Sept, pay-
ment of £25, was equal to £lll. Considering that the Co.
paid £83,000 in commission to get £317,000 in cash—26
per cent of the money gone in brokerage—surely the share-
holders are entitled to some explanation.
* • * *
The Raub Australian Gold Mining Co. (pro-
perty in the Straits Settlements but office in Brisbane)
lately declare a Is. div. and makes a Is. call payable same
day. Pleasant for paid-up scrippers, no doubt, but very
rough on the contributing crowd. When there is a sum
of money on hand, half of which is wanted to pay for
improvements or dead work, the obvious and honest
course seems to be to pay for the work out of the money
on hand, and cut down the div. one-half. Thi9 new
device is simply a method by which the paid-up share-
holder gets his div. while the contributing one gets
nothing. And if the paid-up shareholders are the major-
ity they may expand the system almost indefinitely. They
can decide that almost any work is the kind of work
which should be charged to capital, and keep on paying
themselves handsome dividends, and giving the con-
tributing shareholders no divs. at all, to any extent.
If the idea comes into general use the man with
money and brains will take very good care not to be a
contributing shareholder in a co. where the paid-up person
is in evidence.
* • • •
Croydon tinfield properties are being developed
systematically. Big samples of ore sent to Sydney and
Melbourne have given payable returns. Tin-miners are
agitating for a State battery to help developing field.
Lodes so far are losing nothing in size and richness with
depth, and the stanniferous area is extending on all sides.
Experienced men who have visited field say prospects
equal those of Herberton district.
Re Q. Premier Philp’s assertion that “it had
been abundantly proved that private enterprise could
work mining railways better than the Government/*
“Private enterprise” isn’t working any railways in Q.
The Ohillagoe line isn’t yet open, and, of course, hasn’t
had time to be a success or anything else. And Philp
seems inclined to burk the feasible Atherton Garnet route
in favor of the branch line from the Chillagoe co.’s road.

• • * »
Mt. Lyell.—The offer of £lB,OOO or 6s. per
share for the Royal Tharsis refused by the Directors, with
an intimation that they would recommend £24,000 for
the consideration of shareholders ; the parent co. deter-
mined not to increase the offer. The offer of £45,000 for
the South Tharsis not yet dealt with. Safe to bet they
will want more, though it is far more money than the
mine is worth to present co. At the same time it suits
the Mt. Lyell Co. to buy it at that figure. No doubt they
would get it much cheaper by waiting, but meanwhile
more than the difference would be spent in using barren
silica flux. Somewhat remarkable that the management
did not acquire properties containing siliceous ore-bodies
with a little copper a year or two ago. The Mt. Lyell’s
adversity—the want of silica—is the South Tharsis people’s
opportunity for the sale of the mine at a bigger figure
than it is worth to themselves, and, if they are fools
enough to refuse it, the Mt. Lyell people have the conso-
lation that it is not the only mine in the district where
the siliceous flux want can be supplied.
* » * *
Crown Lyell (Tas.) : As anticipated, the im-
provement was short-lived. A few days’ working and an
intrusion of schist appeared. It was seen and wired to
Melbourne before the manager got below. Most of the
miners sacked ; probably those taking their places will act
likewise under similar circumstances.
* * * *
Tasmania’s agricultural boom of. a year ago
seems to have dropped dead. In 1898 the island produced
2,303,512 bushels of wheat and 2,271,070 bushels oats.
For 1899 the results were—1,093,576 bushels wheat and
1,027,750 bushels oats, or much less than half.

SOCIETY
The first to wear the title of Gov.-General
of Australia was Charles Augustus Fitzroy, whose
memory still smells in N.S.W. As in the appoint-
ment of Hopetoun, Australia was not consulted as
to what sort of a man it liked or disliked. But at
that time Earl Grey, the Joe Chamberlain of the
moment, was not so wholly insolent as the Im-
perial Govt. now is, for the Earl made offer to pay
the salary of the Gov.-General. “I should not
think it fair,” he wrote, “to claim the right of
appointment and impose on you the duty of pay-
mg the salary.” The inconsequential Legislature
*1 however, instead of straightening out
the difficulty, decided that, whereas it could not
appoint, or have a voice in the appointment, of
the chief official, it would, at any rate, retain the
unique honor of paying the whole of his salary.
* * * •
The probability now is that the Federal
Parliament will be housed, during its sittings in
Melbourne, not in the Exhibition Building, as at
J 1 I s , P ro P°sed, but in a specially-erected wooden
bunding m Parliament House yard. It will cost
at least £25,000 to put up the place, which will be
made to communicate with the library and the
palatial halls in which. the local Parliament will
talk about roads, bridges, drains, and parish
pumps.
* • * *
John Ferrier, under arrest for shootirm
Miss Aubrey, at Dalton (Yic.) on July 2 is
receiving characteristic treatment from the Aus-
tralian press in town and country. His record
true or imaginary, has been printed by scores of
papers. His shooting in Sydney, alleged em-
bezzlement in Victoria, and general career are set
ao\vn m naked type, and the Vic. police are repre-
sented as saying that they believe he is a much
worse scoundrel than the narrative discloses,
iriow, in these circumstances, is Ferrier to get a
fair and square jury trial under the law ? One of
the uses of a Public Defender would be that such
an official would be in a position to take cognis-
ance of press or other reports which jeopardised
tair trial, and to immediately indict the offenders
for attempting to interfere with the course of
justice. At present such offences are nobody’s
special business, and if the accused be without
influential friends, he is practically convicted
before trial.

One can tell pretty well what sort of a
drought it has been by reading even the report of
the N.S.W. Federal Electorates Commission,
lately tabled. At Thackaringa, partly mining
and partly pastoral, in Sturt electorate, 71 per
cent, of the names have been struck off the rolls
through removals ; at Yandarly (Wilcannia), 70
per cent., at Nuntherungie (Wilcannia) G 2 per
cent., Pooncarie (Wentworth) 48 per cent., Menin-
die (Wentworth) 42 per cent., Wentworth 37 per
cent. For the whole district of Wentworth the
removals total 33 per cent., for the Lachlan 30 per
cent., for Bourke 24 per cent. In the last four
years there has been in these districts an absolute
net loss of (say) 20 per cent, of the adult males.
* * * *
The Orange complaint about the special
Gqvt. grants to the Sydney R.C. hospitals (St.
Vincent’s and Lewisham) is useful in drawing
attention to the lack of Govt, inspection of all
institutions receiving help from the Consolidated
Revenue. Under the Public Institutions Inspec-
tion Act, 18G6, all such places are required to sub-
mit to it ; but it is no one’s particular province
to make these inquiries, and, as a matter of fact,
no inspection is ever made. Presumably the
large institutions pay professional accountants to
do their work, but many of the smaller ones em-
ploy honorary amateurs only ; and, though the
matter is brought under the notice of succeeding
Governments, nothing is done. Huge sums of
money go out of the Treasury yearly, and there
are only the societies’ receipts to show what is done
with them.
* * * *
The coincidence of the President of
N.S.W. Public Service Association being elevated
by Govt, to the Public Service 'Board has mate-
rially altered the views of many of the. higher
officials in the departments towards that body.
The elections for the incoming Council are to be
held at the end of the month, and several of the
big men are known to eagerly await a “ numer-
ously-signed requisition ”to stand. The rank and
file of the service, however, look askance at the
idea of a council unduly officered by the heads of
departments or their chief clerks, the effect of
which would be to ruin their object in establish-
ing the Association.

All the Sydney tram-accidents do not get
into the papers. Soon after H.M.S. Europa
reached this port a petty officer on leave attempted
to board a tram at Circular Quay. The driver
started before the man was ready and he fell
underneath the wheels. One foot had to be
amputated and he received other serious injuries!
The Railway Commissioners, with the Lawson
case in view, offered him £6OO compensation,
which was accepted. Under the regulations of
the British navy no pension is given to men
disabled off duty and consequently the unfortunate
man will receive nothing from the Admiralty.

Ihe whirligig of things. Six or seven
years ago, before drought wiped the surplus stock
off the Western N.S.W. plains, John Howell
and one or two others put up a big boiling-down
and meat-preserving factory at Menindie, on the
Darling; and among other plant a big stock of
tin-plates was laid in. But the huge plant never
had a chance to. work ; and the other week the
jump in the tin-market made it worth while
taking back the stock of tin-plates from the heart
of Australia to the port from which it was shipped
six years or so ago.
* * * *
Died recently, aged <B4, Mrs. Robert
Lowe, of Wilbertree, near Mudgee—the first
white'female to take up her residence in Mudgee.
Which recalls a “black” story that shows how
scarce white women were in Mudgee even in 1840.
An old man, still living in the West, says that in
that year he was one day at Blackman’s Inn,
Mudgee, when a. well-dressed man came riding
into the yard with a young gin seated behind
him. She was good-looking and clean, dressed in
a clean skirt and ’possum rug. The horseman
was a Govt, official, who had come in to open the
court. He lived down the river, but always
brought the gin with him for fear her blackfellow
would take her away in his absence. Mrs. Bur-
gess, the cook at Blackman’s, always got half-a-
crown to provide dinner and keep the gin sober.
The latter belonged to the Bathurst tribe, was
quite a beauty (for a gin), and was named Dithabba
(meaning flower) corrupted to Biddy. That
official founded a First Family, too !
One portion of Thomas Rose’s law reform
bill, now dangling .in N. S.W. Legislature,
provides that in all civil actions, the Judge shall
determine “ all questions of law and fact, but upon
tne application of either party to the suit, the Judge
snail order a jury to be summoned to try questions
of fact.” Which comes simply to this: one party
will say, “Your Honor, I’m quite satisfied that
your Honor’s decision on the facts will be abso-
lutely correct.” Then the other will have to agree
or say: Your Honor, I’m not willing to trust your
judgment on the facts. Gimmeajury.’’ Inthelatter
case, the Judge—a certain kind of Judge, anyway
will purse up his mouth, scowl at the impudent
suitor and grant a jury, whacking into said suitor
on pomts of law. . If there must be a change made
IP t* lis branch, it. would be better to leave the
’ i ge to decide without direction of suitors, as to
whether the facts are so obscure that the assistance
of a jury is necessary.

Vic. Govt, bacteriologist, Dr. Cherry,
attributes to infected milk the great majority of
the 800 tuberculosis deaths per annum in Victoria
of children under 10. Hardly to be wondered at,
in view of the fact that—as the 3 to 4 per
cent, of the animals found at the Melbourne
Abattoirs to be infected with tuberculous
are nearly all bullocks—it is impossible to
estimate the extent to which dairy-cows are
affected. And, in any case, Dr. Cherry points
out, the big business mistake will come home to
roost the moment tubercle-bacilli are discovered
m Victorian exported butter. Perhaps, when the
pocket speaks, the wholesale milk-murderer will
listen.

extras will all disappear when the veterans retire,
and as at their age they are not required to do
much —anyhow, they are much better off in har-
ness than out of it. The loudness of their cry
is proportioned to the barrenness of the Fund.
* * * *
Cardinal Moran, referring to the recent
eviction of the Carmelite nuns at Marrickville,
Sydney, said that in purchasing the property the
nuns had made a bad speculation, and the Catholic
Church was not prepared to pay thousands of
pounds on a matter of sentiment. It is certainly
reassuring tp know that Holy Church has ceased
to spend money on “sentiment.” Meanwhile
the nuns are being housed privately by friends,
and the Cardinal cheerfully adds: “As regai ds
any future arrangements, I have no doubt that
Providence will arrange all things for the best.”
Good old Providence !—it always comes in handy
when people are not inclined to “ part.”

Melbourne Health authorities were lately
wondering how that fatal case of plague happened
in one of the outer suburbs. Chief Factories’ In-
spector Ord, in a report to Prahran Council, sug-
gests how it might happen. He had inspected
the premises of a firm of marine dealers and
bedding manufacturers, and found “five women
picking rags in a half-open shed exposed to the
winds and to the dust from a flock-teasing
machine. Walls and windows were covered with
dust and cobwebs. Rags and boxes of rags and
flock were all over the shed. In a low, ill-lighted-
and ill-ventilated room upstairs men and women
were making mattresses. The whole place wasdirty
and wretched and should be pulled down.” Only
a few weeks before the local health-inspector had
overhauled the whole city and had discovered no
nuisance worth mentioning. Apparently the
gentleman thought a marine store was the proper
place to manufacture nice, new, clean bedding.
* * * *
Understood that the next Bishop of Bal-
larat will get a reasonable salary at the start, with
every prospect of fatter times in store for him.
Bishop Thornton was drawing only £6OO a year
when he decided to shift camp back to England.
No wonder that Thornton felt tired of wearing
episcopal gaiters on these terms. As a clerk at
£3 a week it would have been easier for him to
“ live up to his position ” and escape calumny on
the score of meanness.

F.C.” : Ran across my St. Kilda
acquaintances —the alleged Greek Gypsies—at
Ringwood, 16 miles outside Melbourne, t’other
day. Such property as they have acquired during
the past year is wholly of the landed variety, and
is carried on the person. They possess two ex-
press wagons in an advanced stage of senile decay,
a couple of ponies of a frugal breed, and two
alleged tents, structures composed wholly of bags
sewn together and thrown over poles. Seven
men, ten women and a tribe of children make a
push of 38 altogether. Apparently all their
clothing is worn permanently—and they need it
this weather. The wagons were empty ; the
“tents” luxuriously furnished with carpets of
spear-grass and gum-leaves; their only visible
garments, other than those worn, were a few red
bandannas spread out to dry. Several of the
kiddies were almost naked, while the thermometer
was vainly trying to get into the forties. They
had no fire, though there was abundance of wood
at hand. Asked one of the men if he didn’t find
it cold sleeping in the tents this weather. “Oh,

THE BULLETIN bEING rather tired of the
Marshall-Hall discussion publishes this zoological
view of the situation.
DIGNITARY (up the tree): “My dear Professor,
I can't let you play that tune in my yard.
Hence this cocoanut.”
no ! we sleep in ze wagons. Ze vomen sleep in ze
tents.” They get themselves disliked by visiting
adjacent houses, knocking, and, when the door is
opened, calmly entering and taking a seat as if
they had come to stay. Also, they demand
money, in return for which they will condescend
to read your hand. Sixpence seems to be the
lowest coin accepted, though a “ tray ” and a loaf
of bread are not refused, but donor gets a poor
“ fortune ”in return for that. So far as can be
gathered their intention is to travel round Vic-
toria until they finally peg-out from cold or
a too great aversion to work or something. No-
body really knows where they came from.. They
certainly are not Greeks. They look unaccount-
able enough to be extinct Babylonians.

Victoria’s war-celebration postage-stamps
came out so late, and consequently fell on such a
flat market, that they seem likely to do little
more than pay for paper and gum. Instead of
the expected £3600, net proceeds are so far only
about £6OO, and the demand is almost dead.
Query by Dr. Ross, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), the
meteorological crank who is always asking the
Ministry if it will ask Astronomer Russell why
rain falls down instead of up ; or why it is colder
in cold weather than in warm weather; or whether
he is of opinion that a four-sided triangle standing
up horizontally during the recent wet weather,
with one end of it leaning on the isobar and the
other end in a public-house drinking with the
barometer, if it met an oblong square that had
cold feet, was calculated to make Borchgrevink
feel better towards Wragge than he does now, or
whether it would cause £ll9s. to go mad and
bark on a milestone :
1. Will he obtain from Mr. Russell, 8.A., Govt. Astro-
nomer and Meteorologist, a report on the recent cold
weather and phenomenal fall of snow that occurred all
over the colony, and the probable cause of the same com-
pared with the previous prolonged and disastrous
droughts through which the colony has passed of late
years ? 2. If similar cold weather and an unprecedented
fall of snow has ever previously been experienced in these
colonies ; if so, when, and to what extent ? 3. Is there
any likelihood (meteorologically or otherwise) of similar
cold and phenomenal fall of snow recurring in the future ;
if so, from what cause or probable causes are such atmos-
pheric perturbations likely to occur ; and, if so, are there
any means or known meteorological laws by which their
advent may be accurately or approximately foretold ?
It might occur to any less devastating gentleman
than the ever-inquiring Ross that if the late fall
of snow was unprecedented there couldn’t have
been a similar one before. That point didn’t
occur to the devastator apparently.

Owing to the fact that Maoriland girls
decline to work for the wages offered by Bell and
Co., wax-match makers, of Wellington (M.L.),
the firm has imported girls from ’Ome to do the
work. The damsels have now arrived and express
disgust at a land where there are no penny doctors
(poor girls !), no cheap gaffs, and where Govt,
interferes with sweating and “phossy-jaw.” No
doubt the ’Ome lydies will soon assimilate M.L.
democratic principles and necessitate the firm
again having to face the music.

The soi-disant democratic S. A. Advertiser
(run by a knight) proudly claims the credit of
having suggested sending the toy local gun-
boat Protector to China. As is only natural, the .
’Tiser’s loathsome contemporary, S.A. Regis-
ter, simply thunders against the idea. Both
papers are painfully Jingo, and the Register
opposition is therefore merely prompted by
annoyance at the opposition rag having forestalled
it in this crawlsome matter. The Protector’s
commander and crew are all, in a sense, amateurs.
Walcot, the first commander, long since returned
to England. Capt. Creswell (his successor) was
recently promoted to Queensland, and the original
crew were paid off years ago.

Extract from letter from an Australian
mining-engineer in China: —
The strictures of Dr. Morrison (a Geelong boy, now Lon-
don Times correspondent at Pekin) on the missionaries
are, in my opinion, rather overdone. . Of course, there are
many narrow-minded ex-grocer’s assistants amongst'the
missionaries, of whom it is well to fight shy if yon can >
but, on the whole, they are excellent company, ahd ever
ready to oblige. Dr. Morrison’s name is, of course,
strictly tabooed in missionary circles. The attitude
of the natives towards us is one everlasting bland smile.
However, we are always accompanied by a large armed
escort, which would bring smiles to the face of even the
most malignant Chinaman.
* • * *
It is estimated that five million heathen
have been baptised during this century. No doubt
baptism is, in most cases, a form with the heathen,
as it is with people in this nominally-Christian
country. Granted that the five millions are
genuine, how small the number appears when it is
considered that the heathen world has increased
by 200 millions during the same century ! 35 mil-
lions of these heathen pass annually to the grave,
not having heard of Christianity. It is said that
about 4000 converts (of a kind) are made every
year in China. What an insignificant number
compared with the fact that 1400 people die every
hour in China, so that in one day 33,000 of them
have for ever passed beyond missionary reach. Is
it worth while, then, to send missionaries to
China to stir up anti-foreign hatred which results
in massacre and war ?
• . • *
Two lady-missionaries in China—a Scan-
dinavian and a Yankee —have taken unto them-
selves indigenous hubbies. And the other mis-
sionaries “no-likee”; the men-missionaries, that
is. The Chow seems to succeed with the female
missionaries just as he so unaccountably does
with so many other alleged white women. And
once a woman takes to a Chinaman there is no
attraction left for her in any other sort of man
whatsoever.

Editor Bulletin.— A last word re N.S.W.
Education Minister Perry and the Patriotic Fund
(in Jingo capitals). The “official” by whom
teachers were circularised to “part up” to the
fund aforesaid was “F. Bridges, Chairman of
Committee no underling, anyhow ; official
envelopes being used. Minister Perry “knew
that some could hardly afford donations.” Yet to
every teacher was a copy of this insinuating circu-
lar sent. Also, enthusing over the “magnificent
result” of the audacious cadge, the sending of
which hundreds of teachers resented as a mere act
of blackmail, Mr. Perry remarked, in effect, that
not one, he thought, refused to subscribe. Of at
least a dozen teachers of writers personal
acquaintance, there was not one who didn t have a
distinct abhorrence of the movement and its
object: yet all subscribed. Ugh ! Let Bulletin
readers judge!— Pedagogue.

“ Grafter ” to The Bulletin :
At a certain Q’sland mining-town hospital the cook
is an ex-railway navvy, and a brother-navvy, who
was a patient in the hospital, having had some words
with said cook, hauled him out of bed to have u satisfac-
tion.” Next morning the “patient” was arrested and
charged with the larceny of two eggs ; but practically
as a breaker of the peace in the company-bossed hospital
—he got six months’ “ hard.” Said co. owns the whole
neighborhood, body and soul!
The Jingo plague appears to be subsid-
ing as well as the bubonic. At a banquet
and presentation t’other night by leading
inspectors and public-school teachers of Syd-
ney and suburbs a fine musical programme
did not include one patriotic song. Yet
the Minister for Public Instruction pre-
sided. Not so long since this “ominous
omission” would have been made the
ground for a departmental investiga-
tion and “ disloyalty’’-hunt.
One healthy sign about Maori-
land is that the Anglican Church is
complaining that “colonials” won’t
enter the church, the pay is so small,
and that parsons have to be im-
ported. The sect which won’t pay its
parsons a fair screw, and the sect whose
parsons aren’t willing to work for an
unfair screw, has got to Go.

George Lynch, in Westminster Gazette :
We are coming back to England in a ship laden with
the human wreckage of war —the wounded, the maimed,
the sick, who to their graves will carry the maiming of
their sickness. There are, amongst these men, those who
will crawl about the world lopsided, incomplete cripples,
or those who will he perpetually victims to intermittent
or chronic disease ; but there is a worse than any of these
disasters to the victim. The man without a leg can get
along with a crutch. The man who loses his sight from
the earth-scattering shell can at worst carry a, label to
tell that he was blinded in the war, and his charitable
fellow-countrymen will give him enough to keep him
enjoying life through the channels of the four other
senses, and he will still admit that it is good to be alive.
We are carrying back the men who have lost their
reason at Magersfontein and other battles ; two have
been mercifully treated and have lost it completely the
padded cell must mean a certain unconsciousness ; but
the greatest, deepest pity of which the human heart is
capable is called forth by those who are maimed in mmd.
Long lucid intervals of perfect sanity give them time to
learn the meaning of the locks and bars. Yes, I know ,
I went Off my head after Magersfontein,’ one poor fellow
tells you ;• another repeatedly asks, “ Will they put me
into an asylum when I go home ? ” What a home-coming .
The Glory !
• • *
The luck of drunks, again ! A Brunswick
(Melb.) constable lately swore that a carter, whom
he arrested for being “under the influence, tell
off his dray and rolled beneath the wheels, and
that, though a ton and a-half of metal passed over
both his legs, he escaped with a few bruises. Ihe
Bulletin doesn’t guarantee this yam, it merely
states that the constable swore it. But is it really
luck, or does alcohol induce, with insensibility to
pain, a state of imperviousness to injury ?

In the funeral column of the Melb. Argus
recently the friends of one Casey, deceased, were
notified that the corpse would receive the atten-
tion of a certain undertaker, and that the funeral
would start at 11 sharp. A second advertisement
however, notified that another undertaker would
conduct, and that the funeral would start from
the hospital at 2.30. The first ghoul won.

W.C.T.U. people are urging Premier
Holder to use his influence to close all S. A. hotels
on Peace Celebration Day, owing to the “terrible
drunkenness ” which characterised the Mafekmg
outburst. But Holder, although a pious temper-
ance man himself, says he daren’t. Moreover, a
Peace Day mob, yellow and sullen under the bloat-
ing and dyspeptic influence of tea and buns, would
be as dangerous as the beer-sodden multitude of
Mafeking Day.

The legality of the Sunday shave has been
re-established in England. By an Act of the
sainted Charles 11. barbers are placed on a footing
with surgeons, and entitled to let blood. And
they do so even unto this day.
“ Alba ” : Recently a certain Sydney
citizen nearly lost his wife. He didn’t quite
lose her, but only great promptitude and
calmness and presence of mind on his
part averted the disaster. A bagman
distilled poison into her ear, and the
poison worked so that she eloped
with him to a convenient watering-
place much used for that purpose. The
husband heard the news a few
minutes after they were gone, and
ran them down in a cab. I made
friends with the hotel boots,
and he told me the rest of the
story in a rich foreign accent :
“It was aftare dinnaire ven
ze husband come in ze cab.
He says, ‘My vife is here
wiz a big drummaire.’ Zen
he go to ze sitting-room door,
like ze perfait zhentleman wiz
his hat in his hand, an’ says,
‘Mary, you come home wiz me
now?’ An’ she says, ‘Beel, is
zat you?’ An’ he says, ‘Yes,
Mary, it is me, Beel. You
come home, Mary.’ Zen ze
drummaire turn on zem wiz
terrible oaths. ‘Stan’ ’way
from my samples,’ he says,
* an’ for hevvm’s sake close
ze door an’ keep out ze
bloomin’ dashed cold
vind.’ Zen ze man an’
wife go outside. ‘Beel,’
she says, ‘ did you
bring my cape?’
An’ Beel says, ‘Yes,
Mary, I did bring
your cape.’ Zen zay
go away in a cab. I
could not sleep zat
night for ze snore
of ze big drum-
maire.” But
some
men
are
not gentle-
men. Writer
has a friend
known as Ironbark
Bill who chased his wife
and her bagman to Paris.
On hearing that Bill was
in France the guilty pair
fled to the top of the Eiffel Tower. Bill
followed them to the highest turret, caught
the bagman by the hair, and cast him out into
space in a southerly direction. A few hours later
there was a great thud of bagman somewhere on a
pavement in Toulon.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
~ _ Sydney, July 17, 1900.
My Dear Myek.—
Lord Hopetoun as Governor-General is the only
subject talked about this week. Sydney’s first
impression was that the appointment would give
Melbourne a heavy pull over Sydney. But some-
how deeper reflection reveals a doubt. “To
know people too well is the curse of modern
society, where remoteness yields to civilisation
and high pressure.” Hopetoun knows Melbourne
in and out. He only knows Sydnfey out. Then
add to his inspired knowledge the Wallington’s
scientific assessment of Mrs. Smellbum Jones’
claims to an invitation-card, and you will see that
Sydney has really something to congratulate
itself upon in this appointment.
The only item to be chalked on Melbourne’s
little slate is the heavy attempt Potts-Point once
made to marry Wally to an heiress. A man
doesn’t object to the attempt if successful, but
nothing came of it in Wally’s case, and he may.
Lord Hopetoun lately confided in “M.A.P.”: “I
went out to Australia as Governor of Victoria and
spent quite the happiest years of my life there.
The first impressions I got of the colonies were all
thoroughly and unmistakeably British. At my
first levee held at Government House one of the
earliest presentations was that of a Waterloo
veteran.” But he omits to say that these
thoroughly and unmistakeably British institu-
tions umped him out, and brought him to a dead
faint across an arm-chair, after shaking a thou-
sand odd hands at his first “ let-’ein-all-come.”
His British institutions are further belied by
the following yarn, which he spun with much
relish. “In the Australian back-blocks I have tra-
velled a great deal, and wherever feasible always
wore the native bushman costume —riding-pants,
colored shirt, and broad-brimmed hat. I recollect
once, at the close of a hard ride—undertaken at
the instance of some members of the Ministry,
who were my travelling companions—l emerged
from the mallee scrub well in front of the others.
Mud-covered and generally dishevelled, I rode up
alone, to the heart of the township where the
principal residents were waiting to present an
address of welcome. After a few minutes I ven-
tured to ask what they were waiting for. * We’re
waiting for the Governor,’ said the spokesman of
the party. ‘Well, I’m the Governor!’ I said.
My remark was greeted with incredulous laughter,
and it was only when my friends overtook me
that the unbelieving mallee-scrubbers were per-
suaded of my identity—small blame to them ! ”
Some wholly unreliable person told me this
story about how a warm-hearted person at the
Bush .Evening Party took leave of Lady Mary
Lygon and gave her his fatherly blessing. He
was more or less husky; one of Nature’s gentle-
men—which is said to be the worst sort. He
thanked Lady Mary for a pleasant evening, and
added impressively : “ An’ I hope ye’ll find a nice
young Australian man to keep company wid
before long.”
Society made an episode of Mrs. Willie
Mitchell’s sale. For two days, everybody from
the Points gathered at Longwood. The sale
assumed the proportions of a great “At Home.”
As for the selling, prices ran high. The lively
interest always shown in this society - leader’s
movements was livelier than ever, and, for the
first time, practical. Buyers even carried off the
“Holy Family” and other Old Masters bought at
the Wentworths’ sale a few months back. A
Florentine mirror was a cause of a little excite-
ment. The fair purchaser, after eager attempts to
obtain the glass, became suddenly discontented,
and tried to get out of her bargain by compacting
with a dealer. The mirror of Venus didn’t trans-
mogrify Cybele, or Pallas, or anv others of them —
did it ? By the way, Mrs. Mitcbell will keep up
her retirement at Moss Yale until she goes to
London next year.
It has just come out that the quilt raffled by
Mayoress Harris at the Sydney Press Bazaar last
year, was won by Dr. Salmon, who is going to be
milady’s son-in-law shortly. The lucky man has
kept it carefully since with a view to furnishing,
probably next October just before the Cup.
Elsie Hall’s Indian-Famine Concert filled the
Town Hall last Wednesday. Like most artistic
temperaments, the gentle pianist can buffet
harder for others than for herself. The fund pro-
fits by her efforts to the extent of over £l5O.
A very toney performance will be Madame
L’Eclair’s theatricals at the Lyceum this week.
All the dress-circle sold as soon as the plan
opened. Everyone is asking, “Who is Madame
L’Eclair? She is Mrs. de Pentheny O’Kelly.
She was Miss Sophie Forbes, the vivacious
daughter of Judge Forbes, and sister of the gentle-
man who used to be so well-known here in
private theatricals. Her dancing classes are
heavily patronised by Society. She teaches high-
kicking, and all that sort of thing, but the dancing
at her entertainment will not be as French as her
name might lead you to hope—l mean suspect.
For a committee of well-known women went
down to watch a rehearsal last week. They sat
in the front row of the stalls. They inspected
with gimlet eyes. Finally, they passed the ver-
dict that society was not going to show too much
nether tuck. Of course, I wouldn’t tell you this
only that the entertainment will be over before
you read it.
All the dances are coming with a rush. The
“ Pretoria Ball,” in aid of the Boys’ Brigade, is
fixed for Thursday, 2nd of August, at Pad-
dington Town Hall. Mrs. Playfair and Mrs. J.
A. Dean are working hard to make a success of
this hop. The Freemasons’ ball comes off on July
27th at Sydney Town Hall. St. Vincent’s belated
ball is dated Sept. 2nd. . \
The concert that goes to the suburbs or the
country is becoming quite a paying institution.
John ■ Lemmon© and Marie' Nacelle have been
touring the backblocks with splendid lurk. I hear
loud rumors of an exceptionally clever elocutionist
from the country who is helping largely with the
co.’s successes, and later on, I believe, we are to
hear him in Sydney. Another travelling co. has
just been formed by Frank Downs, including
Maud Dalrymple, Florence Mack, and Reginald
Goold. The new co. starts operations at Chats-
wood, and work outwards.
From Paling and Co. come the “Mafeking
Waltz,” by Zara Strahan, a bright, fetching piece
that doesn’t depend on its title altogether; and
the “Soldier’s Last Message,” by G. Vincent, a
song that suffers from the debility the name would
lead you to expect.
“The Absent-Minded Beggar” was received by
a goodly audience at the Royal on opening
night. The scenic effect was splendid. Miss
Frances Ross, as Kathleen Ivor, looks much
too fresh and clean for an outpost camp, and the
nurses also have a picture-book effect. Miss
Harrie Ireland saunters through her part, not by
any means looking her best. Mrs. Holt makes
a plump and pleasing bugler-boy. The phe-
nomenal baby plays its part with aplomb;
how they, trained it to the perfection of
quietness is a query many would like to hear
answered. The palm in frocking goes to Miss
Fitzmaurice Gill, who artistically combines tur-
quoise silk and black chiffon. The armoured train
was quite a feature of the evening, and for a few
moments it came at such a pace we wondered if it
would land among the audience.
Our artists are getting ready a very special
entertainment, to be given to Nelson Illingworth,
who has lately suffered a severe illness. As Mr.
Illingworth is personally most popular in art
circles, apart from his reputation as a sculptor,
the affair promises to be heavily patronised. His
Ex. will be there—although the name, “A Night
in Bohemia,” will characterise the fgte. I believe
the Town Hall is to be draped to represent the
Latin Quarter, and a fraternity called “L’Ecoles
des Beaux Arts” will burlesque certain pictures
in the Art Gallery, and exhibit a realistic show of
pictures—whatever that may be. All this sounds
very new and inviting, so I will only add that
August 29, at 8 o’clock, will see the novelty begin.
A theatrical engagement is much buzzed over
this week : —Dorothy Vane and young Mr.
Menzies, of Melbourne. They are going to be
married in August, and the Convent—l presume
—is indefinitely postponed. It is ever so much
more pleasant to live at Menzies’ hotel than at any
Convent. I fancy that the only person who ever
took the Convent notion seriously was Dorothy
herself, and even there I have my doubts.
Apropos of Menzies’ Hotel, the present man-
ager thereof, Mr. Smith, is coming to Sydney to
manage the Australia as successor to Mr. H.
Edwin Moore, who leaves shortly for his native
America. Great alterations are to be made at the
Australia, which is in future to cater for a much
wider circle.
At Grafton Police Court the other day, the
solicitor asked a lady witness : “ How do you
know that the looking-glass was in the room on
the day before you missed it ? Was there any
special reason why you should have noticed it ’ ”
The P.M. answered the question by interjecting:
“Special reason! Fancy expecting any woman
could resist the temptation ! ”
While grief for the death of Mrs. Julian Ashton
is still so new and poignant, it is hard for
those who knew her well to coldly sum her titles
to affection and esteem. She was justly considered
a clever woman, a woman who thought for herself,
a sincere and conscientious woman. For many
years literary critic for Sydney Daily Telegraph,
her work was always interesting, and always
kindly. Before that, there was a time when, on
the platform and in the press, she argued enthu-
siastically for the freedom and advancement of
women, even (what is hard for a woman to do) in-
curring social blame rather than yield her honest
convictions. With Mrs. H. E. Russell and Mrs.
Gullet, she founded the Sydney Women’s Literary
Society. Mrs. Russell died about a year ago;
Mrs. Gullet within the last few weeks, and now
Mrs. Ashton follows her friends and co-workers.
She was aged only 49. Many will hold her long
in memory.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ One reason for the popularity of the Earl of
Hopetoun in Victoria was that he knew how to
be easy without being free. Walking down
Collins-st. one day, Howard Willoughby, editor
of the Argus, met the fresh-faced young Governor
m ordinary walking attire and by himself. ‘ How
do you do, Mr. Willoughby?’ said the Earl,
affably. ‘Now, where have I seen that face
before ? ’ mused the absent-minded pressman ;
‘no doubt it is some young fellow I’ve helped to
a billet. How are you ?’ he responded, still more
affably. ‘ Getting along all right, I hope. That’s
good, that’s good.’ Afterwards, when the mistake
flashed across Mr. Willoughby, he explained the
matter to the Governor, who was so tickled that
he used to repeat the story himself, saying that it
was a great tribute to his air of innocence and in-
experience.
“Melbourne will undoubtedly be glad to get
Wallington back with the Governor-General.
True, he is a bit of a snob (this not meant offen-
sively)—any Governor’s private secretary must
necessarily be that—but he is, a most amiable and
competent snob who thoroughly understands
snobbing and doesn’t make a boggling mess of it.
Gov. Brassey couldn’t understand ‘Wally’ any-
how. When Uncle Tom first came to Victoria he
wanted to do all sorts of wild things, and the dis-
creet Wallington used to rub his smooth chin and
suavely murmur, ‘ Better not, better not.’ ‘lt was
all better not, better not,’ said the de Bressi im-
patiently after he and Wallington had parted ;
‘ bless my—er —um —soul, it was—er —better not
from morning to night.’ But, observe, when
Brassey ‘chucked’ Better - not - Wallington he
began falling off in the sea, in popularity, in
Albert Park, and in all sorts of ways.
“At Govt. House ball on Friday night (the
crowd was chiefly women) Lady Madden’s glassy
green gown glistened like a summer sea. Sprays
of flowers broke through the stretch of sea. Dia-
monds made the dress still more luminous.
Neither of the Madden girls wore gloves, by the
way. Many vapor-baths are said to be partly
responsible for the clear complexions and match-
less freshness of these charmers. A black and
silvery net veiled Miss Ruby Madden’s white
satin frock, while her sister, in uniform pink,
tripped round like an animated rose. Mr. Frank
Madden’s daughter—a tall fair girl in spectacles—
made her debut in a straight-hanging Princess
robe with virgin flowers thereon. Most mothers
followed their budding daughters round the ball-
room with their bouquets and a hungry look.
Miss Woollard, daughter of the official with the
long moustache who makes such brilliant bows at
the Opening of Parliament, was the most attrac-
tive of a large crop of debutantes. She is tall,
slim, and graceful. Watching them dance in a set
of their own, one came to the conclusion they were
a remarkably innocent crowd of comers-out.
“ There is a pretty little to-do about some invi-
tation cards, sent out by Acting-Gov. Madden’s
aides in franked envelopes. A conscientious
postal official did the right thing in declining to
pass them, making the recipients pay 4d each.
Col. Campbell says it was all a mistake—done by
a subordinate —you know the sort of thing.
Franked envelopes are only for use on official
business. Of course the Acting-Governor himself
is not to blame, and it was enough to take the curl
out of his moustache for such a mean thing to be
done in his name.
“ The Skating Gymkhana at South Yarra Rink
was the social honey-pot where the Naicest
gathered on Thursday night. The excitement
and the draughts were intense. Onlookers deve-
loped colds in the head, and varied the entertain-
ment with sneezing crescendoes, while the rinkers,
arrayed in little more than a short white skirt and
bloomers, stirred up a blue-nose breeze as they
whisked round. The majority of the performers
wore very skimpy skirts and glove-fitting jackets,
which set off the figure to advantage. In the
four-in-hand competition Mrs. Wesley-HalPs pink
and white powdered team struck the brightest
note. Cavalier hats dotted with pink roses,
clipper-trim white frocks and reins of roses formed
the equipment, but the skating gyrations not
being extravagant, it lost the prize. Lord Richard
had to allot the casting vote, but as he had sug-
gested the rose reins and so on, he didn’t think it
fair to give it. So Mrs. Charlie Lempriere driving
the fittest four—the tallest and most dashing—
with unpretentious reins of cerise ribbon, carried
off the much-contested prize. Miss Templeton’s
hunting team came third.
The Paster Ball agitates the heart of woman,
and ev&ry feminine eye reconnoitres the hoardings
to discover a suitable poster-girl. One fetching
young widow whose glory lies in a wealth of fair
tresses is going to appear as someone’s hair-
restorer. A musician of considerable circumfer-
ence talks of going as Bubbles. The Naicest who
have pioneered the idea, are not concerning them-
selves with sets ; they are urging the rank-and-file
to do so, while they intend to review the poster-
panorama from the balcony. Some rare curios in
the way of demi-semi-nude studies are promised.
. “ Quite recently a Jewish maiden was rehears-
ing the marriage ceremony as a dumb show, at a
Masonic Hall, when she espied something silvery
on a background and what looked like wreaths of
flowers at the furthest end of the room. Flitting
down to investigate, the damsel came upon—a
coffin. She has discontinued dumb-show nuptials
for the present.

for 150 photos, to be sent to the various Melb.
people he enumerates on four sheets of fcp.
Before said Adonis left these parts, he dumb-

be struck off, and sent to his nearest and dearest.
This second order must be a list of those next
near and dear.
“Canon Potter has launched against Marshall-
Hall the charge that he recommended some stu-
dents to read Tolstoi’s ‘ Kreutzer Sonata ’ for the
sake of the art-teaching in it. The Canon pro-
bably got the fact from The Bulletin. He says
of the book : ‘ There is almost nothing about music
in it from first to last, but it is throughout a very
dirty and disgusting diatribe against Marriage.’
It is truly amazing that any man who has read
Tolstoi’s book should have written that sentence.
The ‘ Kreutzer Sonata ’ is no more a diatribe
against marriage than Shakespeare’s 4 Othello ’ is.
Mr. Potter also seems to have skipped the
passages in which Tolstoi with great subtlety
traces the relations of music and emotion—
feverishly anxious, no doubt, to get to the other
parts of the book. And, anyway, the spectacle of
the author of such immitigated piffle as ‘The
Germ-Growers ’—probably the trashiest story ever
written and published in Australia—throwing
gutter mud at a literary artist of Tolstoi’s attain-
ments is something like a dirty little boy befouling
the robe of a prophet.
“The Philharmonic’s “Damnation of Faust”
was a big success. The plot of the music, so to
speak, was unravelled on the programme for the
benefit of the Philistines. There must have been
lots of Philistines present, for whenever you read
to the end of the leaf the sound of turning over
the page was as the noise of a flight of pigeons.
Faust’s adventures make an exciting allegory, and
grown-ups seem to relish music in the narrative
form after the manner of a youngster who pleads
“ Tell me a story with a cake in it.”
“Mr. Sugden, the Master of Queen’s (Wesleyan),
was responsible for the readable Faust programme.
He’s a blue-eyed, broad-shouldered, rather blunt-
tongued Englishman, who has made the leaders of
his denomination very angry by putting in a good
word for Marshall Hall. Mr. S. says that for
years he has gone to the ex-Professor’s orchestral
class and his daughter has attended the Conserva-
tonum -and 'though they’ve both listened their
hardest they’ve never heard the director do any-
thing but roar harmlessly as any sucking dove.
Good people say that if Mr. Sugden hasn’t heard
anything bad more shame for him. He that has
ears, &c. The master of Queen’s has never been a
prime pet of his employers. When Governors’
wives, &c., are taken to functions at the College,
their host is apt to tread absent-mindedly on their
trains or to forget to offer his arm. And Wes-
levans set such store by manners, top-hats, and
tail-coats. Somebody wrote to the paper this
week to wish that another John Wesley might
arise at the present moment, presumably for the
express benefit of poor Mr. Sugden.
night Janet Lady Clarke saw
Manoeuvring Jane ’at the Princess in one of her
numerous blends of white silk-muslin, black bro-
cade and jet. The lady with her wore a lovely
