“ Nance O’Neil, alleged apostle of stage realism,
unwittingly converts many of feeble faith in
realism to a strong belief in more artistic, if less
‘natural,’ methods. For instance, Camille’s
attacks of sniffles are life-like weep tactics, but so
long as a stage-mourner contrives herself to feel
the grief she pourtrays, I guess her audience will
feel it too without much assistance from ’kerchief
demonstrations.
“As Camille in country retirement, Nance
looks really sweet and womanly with her stray
fair curls, innocent white muslin and meek black-
velvet touches. Don’t you hate four-post beds on
the boards ? Such prosaic, utilitarian objects !
One looks instinctively for a small table at the
side,, laden with night-lights, spirit-lamp, and
baby s bottle. Then when a moribund crawls
from the blankets in . a crumpled night-gown, on-
lookers vow inwardly never again to grumble at
the histrionic heroine who pines away in a becom-
ing silk-crdpe neglige on an artistic couch.
doesn’t somebody speak seriously to
Ldith Berry touching her absurd appearances as a
stage handmaiden ? Ambition to scintillate in
tragic roles (r6le is the word where tragedy is con-
cerned) must really be the matter with Miss
Edith, for when required to speak a few lines and
look spry in an apron part, her air of settled
melancholy gives one the ‘blues.’ No reasonable
novice could ask for a brighter little show than
feu to the damsel of the rueful countenance in
Magda,’ and yet she talked to Magda’s sister
about a bouquet of flowers as though there were a
death in the house and the expensive blossoms
had been sent with a black-edged card. Miss
Berry, I take it, ought to have been a casual
gleam of sunshine in the dull German home—the
sort of gal who gets joyfully excited over the least
disturbance of the domestic routine and lives in
hopes of marrying the baker. Instead of which
she seemed to come into the parlor like a forebod-
ing of disaster. She’s just the same in ‘ Camille.’
Was there ever a French lady’s-maid who con-
versed about Madame and her wraps in an Edith
Berry manner ?
“Touring Bananalanders, who remember the
present Mrs. Mount Morganaire D’Arcy (nee
Nina Boucicault) as a light-hearted, leggy school-
girl, sometimes pay their respects to their old
acquaintance at her gorgeous diggings in London.
Compatriots give this Cinderella of to-day no
credit for beauty, though they describe her as
‘smart ’ and divinely clad. Her married step-
daughters are on friendly terms with their pro-
parent, though their elder sister, the wife of a
captain in the Guards, declines to chum with her
mother’s successor. Mr. Nutting (Mrs. D’Arcy’s
former husband) did not languish long in single
bliss after the divorce. His present wife is a
connection of a well-known Northern legal clan.
“Miss Mary Boucicault, who was given a
benefit in Melbourne when she went ’Ome to
train her voice, is heard of as singing ‘ dainty
little French songs ’ at London concerts in aid of
wounded Australians. Violet D’Arcy, her step-
niece, a young lady of dark foreign appearance,
has also studied under Marchesi, and thereby
hangs a tale—for while taking lessons in Paris,
the millionaire’s daughter was placed in residence
with her stepmother-to-be, then Mrs. Nutting.
. “ME Rignold is said to tell droll stories of the
time when he witched American women with the
blonde beauty of Henry the Fifth. The hotel in
New York where he kept himself shut in from
observation was besieged by worshippers intent
on meeting their god face to face. They bom-
barded his mysterious laager with letters, bunches
of roses, verbal messages, trinkets, locks of hair—
but all in vain; Handsome George would see no
strangers. He made his journeys to and fro the
hotel in a closed carriage, and he just rushed along
the corridor with a man on either side to hide
him as much as possible from the languishing
gaze of maids, wives, and widows. ‘lf they had
got a fair look at me it would have given the show
away,’ says Mr. R., who never was half as vain as
he was painted. To see a certain popular actress
at the races in an unpretending black jacket and
grey hair sets one thinking of how Henry the
Fifth took care to preserve his glamour.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
There has been only one Smith Ministry
in Australasia under Responsible Government.
One F. Smith was Premier of Tasmania for 3£
years in the late ’so’s.
One of the late squatter Donald Cameron’s
troubles in the heyday of his prosperity was the
persistency of people in taking him for the
Donald Cameron who used to write the Australian
bush romances.
“ Scribe ” : If asked to sum up the leading
characteristic of Editor Windsor, of the Age, the
only man who has edited both Age and Aiigus, I
would say, “ Contempt for news.”
Editor Wyse, of the Pastoral Times, has
died at Deniliquin, aged 55. He was the first
white child born on the river Murray, and lived
his half-century there and thereabouts.
Mr. W. P. Reeves, M.L. Agent-General,
tells people in London —“I am an Imperialist
and a Radical—just like Campbell-Bannerman.”
So far, Reeves has not worn Court dress.
Died in Adelaide a few days ago J. F.
Thom, who reached S.A. in its first year, and was
in Col. Light’s survey-party which laid out the
city. Two others of the party are still alive.
R. C. Baker, who was Q.C.’d the other
day, has been 36 years at the Bar. He was the
first native-born South Australian to sit in the
Leg. Assembly and Leg. Council of that province.
The ten members of the present Yic.
Cabinet divide equally the £10,400 set apart for
Ministerial salaries. The first time in Victoria
that every Minister has been placed on the same
level.
The new secretary of the Victorian
Licensed Victuallers’ Association is named Robert
H. Lemon. Lemonade! A few years ago,
Sydney’s grog-paper was edited by a man called
“ Lush.”
Earl Belmore, Gov. of N.S. W. in the early
seventies, is now senior Irish representative peer,
having been elected in 1857. He was born in ’35,
married a niece of W. E. Gladstone, and has three
sons and nine daughters living.
John Dennistoun Wood, who was Attorney-
General and a prominent politician in Victoria
40 years ago, now lives at his native “ Dennis-
toun,” near Bothwell (Tas.). He has thrown law
aside for life, and the other day was advertising
for a “ Bullock-driver —good wages.”
Of the four notables who were named for
the Commonwealth Gov.-Generalship, the suc-
cessful Hopetoun is the youngest—born in 1860.
Selborne is a year older—lBs9—while Jersey and
Lome were both born in ’45. Lome practically
dropped out of the contest when he became Duke
of Argyle. Selbome married a daughter of Pre-
mier Salisbury.
Geo. Hutchison, Conservative-elect of
Patea, M.L., who has been a thorn in the side of
Seddon’s Govt, for years past, finding his political
cause hopeless announces his departure for
B’Africa to settle there. He is known as the M.L.
Joe Chamberlain, from his really extraordinary
likeness to the illustrious screw-maker —which is
enhanced by his orchid and eyeglass.
The golden wedding of Edward John
Eyre, celebrated early in April in Devonshire
(Eng.), calls back nearly 70 years of Austra-
lasian history. Mr. Eyre, now in his 85th
year, landed in Sydney in 1833, and almost
at once embarked in sheep-farming. He
took up runs in South and Western
Australia. He was appointed magis-
trate and protector of aborigines in
every province he resided in. He was
made Lieut.-Governor of two divisions
of Maoriland, and after a visit to Eng-
land was appointed Gov. of St. Vincent,
and later of Jamaica. This last was his
Waterloo, his methods of crushing re-
bellious natives smacking too much of
the distinguished Draco. His wife, a
Miss Ormond, of Melbourne, he married
in 1850. He still enjoys life.
E. H. Wittenoom, the new Wes-
tralian knight, doesn’t deserve even
that. But the conferring of the
knighthood on him shows how cheap
the bauble is. His grandfather was
first chaplain on Swan River penal
station; he himself began as a
jackeroo up north, got a few head of stock
together, took up country on the Murchison,
started a store and made a pot by supplying the
diggers, was in the old nominee Leg. Council for a
year or two, and in ’94 got a seat in the Assembly.
Six months later, being a good Groper, he was
made a Minister, and after that did nothing in
particular except make the absurd “ ten-foot rule’’
which was pretty well at the bottom of all the
subsequent alluvial diggers’ trouble. Presumably
as a reward Bigjohn sent him off as Agent-general
—and the other thing followed quickly.
“ Smiler ” Hales when on his way to
S’Africa offered his services to Hackett, of the
Western Australian. Said Hackett, “Your
style does not suit my class of readers. It is
too much after The Bulletin fashion.” Hales
trudged across St. George’s-terrace to Lovekin, of
the Morning Herald, and quoth Lovekin, of the
Morning Herald ; “Do you seriously think you
can be a war-correspondent ? My paper would be
discredited if I announced you as a special war-
correspondent.” Then “ Smiler ” wired to David
Syme. Answer: “ Arrangements already made.”
.And yet “ Smiler” is now a top-man among the
correspondents—getting £lOOO a year from London
Daily News. The prophet and his own country
once more !
It is related that a recently-appointed
M. engaged in trade had a unique method of
sacking employes. “ Patsy ” (his son) doesn’t like
ye, and “Denis” (another son) doesn’t like ye,
and I begin to think I don’t like ye myself.”
Chief Justice Way has the nomination of
Q.C. in S.A., and the coincidence that President
Baker ratted on his Federal colleagues to support
the Privy Council appeal is said by some to ac-
count for his promotion to silk. It would be hard to
find any other explanation.
The Alex. Berry whose representatives
are pressing N.S.W. Govt, for 6J acres of fore-
shore at Ball’s Head Bay, near Goat Island, has
been dead over a-quarter of a century. Most of
the land he acquired went to him as free
grants from complacent Governors. He explored
Shoalhaven district nearly 80 years ago, and sat in
N. Leg. Council for many years.
Win. Bradley, saved from the Hawkes-
bury flood when a 6-year-old boy (8., 14/7/’00),
was one of the partners in the big coaching, squat-
ting and contracting firm of Cobb and Co., the
other partners being Walter Hall, Jas. Ruther-
ford and Frank Whitney. Bradley built Went-
worth Court (Sydney), on leasehold ground, and
“batched ” in one of the top-storey offices.
“Sammy” Tomkinson, M.L.C., who is
85 years old, and who is the most conscientious,
consistent, and obstinate Conservative S.A. lias
ever seen, has been seriously ill of late, though all
reference to the fact has been kept out of the
papers. He sits for Central District, and should
a vacancy occur the seat would be a gift to ex-
Premier Kingston if he still desires to enter the
Council.
Edward Bulwer Lytton Dickens (son of
the famous journalist) has just been gazetted
inspector under N.S.W. Prickly Pear Destruction
Act. When Dickens first arrived in Sydney as
M.L.A., Premier Parkes invited him to his house
in Balmain and assured the new member that the
highest post in the political service was easily
within his reach. You mustn’t now talk of politics
to Dickens.
Apropos of the impending big R.C.
shivoo at Sydney to celebrate the partial comple-
tion of St. Mary’s Cathedral. Long ago old Jack
Robertson, when N.S.W. Colonial Secretary,
officially attended an elaborate R.C. ceremonial
in company with a political friend belonging to
that creed. After awhile Jack got tired of the
(to him) somewhat dry proceedings, and devoutly
whispered : “By Jove, Tom, what I’d give for a
fine cool brandy-and-soda ! ” “ That’s all right,”
replied his friend, who, at an opportune moment,
led him out to the adjoining presbytery, where
the desired draught was produced. “My word,
Tom,” remarked old Jack, wiping his beard,
“ This is the only true church, after all.”

At dropping fortunes and picking others
up there are few in Australia to equal John de
Baun, now of Westralia, where, according to daily-
paper reports, he has sold his inuch-talked-of Mt.
Higgins claim for £20,000 and an interest. De
Baun, an Anglo-French Canadian, made and
dropped one or two piles first at Silverton and
then at Broken Hill, and he has repeated those
performances at the W.A. goldfields, and later at
Perth. When not otherwise occupied he always
has a big law-action on hand.
C. G. Duffy owed his knighthood to Glad-
stone, whom he called “the liberal leader of the
universe.” Almost the first speech made by the
G.O.M. in the Commons was in defence of slavery.
An enemy once said that the cost of his education
and all he possessed had been wrung from the
bleeding backs of his father’s negro slaves in the
West Indies. Gladstone p'ere might have been
present in the Liverpool theatre when a distin-
guished actor, who had been hissed, thus hit out:
“Who are you that dare to insult me? You
wretches, do you not know that every brick in
your accursed city was cemented with the blood of
a negro?”

Captain Hixson, head of the N.S.W. naval
forces, first touched Sydney in 1848, but it was as
surveyor of N.S.W. coasts in 1861 that he made
himself a thorough Australiaif. He was then a
man of great strength and tireless energy, lhe
present generation know him best as president of
the lately-executed Marine Board. The captain
stands in the relation of father-in-law to two of
the Messrs. Fairfax, of S.M. Herald.
One of the certainties for Yic. Federal
Senator is Dr. Jack Quick. He arrived in Ben-
digo as a boy, and used to feed stampers. Then
he got into Bendigo Advertiser office, fold-
ing papers and doing odd jobs. Next he bios-
somod into a police-court reporter, studied and
passed his matric. From Bendigo he went to
Melbourne, and while on the Age took his Uni-
versity degree. During the Constitutional fight
in ’7B he was returned for Bendigo at the top of
the poll, and Victoria knows how well and per-
sistently he has fought for Federation.
“ Long 'Un ” to The Bulletin :
Recently struck in one of the dailies a yarn headed
“ Karri Davies and the old Boer,” relating how, in the ’Bl
war, the much-boomed Ikey and an old Dutchman shot
at one another from behind rocks for over an hour, both
being finally carried in insensible, with their hats riddled
and heads furrowed like a ploughed field. Knew it was
a fairy-tale because Karri, whom I knew a decade later in
Australia, could not have been over 12 years old at the
time. He did not seem, when I knew him, to be the sort
of man who would revel in such a risky pastime ; still,
they run in all shapes, and you never can tell. A week
or two after, came across same story, word for word, in
Tit Bits, giving the names of both parties, and Karri
wasn’t one of ’em.
Jethro Brown, Professor of Law in Tas.
University, who has just resigned and gone to
England to be married, is one of the coming Aus-
tralians. He is only 30, and although educated
at Cambridge and possessing a most irritating,
quite-English drawl, is a thorough democrat. It’s
utterly unnatural to hear Radical principles
pumped up by that English-University-haw-haw
when you quite expect to hear ponderous periods
about "the rights of Proppahtee and the danger of
giving the mob a voice in the government of the
country. The Professor was born in 3.A., and
last year was Acting-Professor of Law at Sydney
University.
That venerable Sydney practitioner, Dr.
Horatio G. A. Wright, figured prominently at the
trial of O’Farrell, who attempted to assassinate
the Duke of Edinburgh 40 years ago. Dr. Wright
had gone to Clontarf m the same boat as O’Farrell,
and had passed the trip discussing with him the
comparative merits of Melbourne and Sydney
architecture. O’Farrell had then the loaded re-
volver in his pocket and his purpose was fixed,
but to the doctor he seemed an entertaining and
well-informed man. Dr. Wright was emphatic in
stating in the witness-box that O’Farrell exhibited
at that stage no indication whatever of a diseased
mind.
“Old Jack”: Tom Moser, veteran jour-
nalist, who lately died at Sydney, was at one time
editor of the Western Post, Mudgee, N.S.W.,
then run by one Laurence, popularly known as
“ Rum-and-Onions.” This scribe succeeded him.
“ Moser was all very well,” said Rum-and-Onions,
“and a wonderful clever man. But he was a
sight too clever for this ranch. If he reported a
agricultooral show he’d always lug in some damn
fool named Virgil. He’d tell yarns of garlanded
bullocks and skite about ox-eyed Junos. But he
did good, clean work, only it teas aggravating to
be asked by this and the other what it all meant.
I didn’t know, and it ain’t pleasant for a man that
runs a paper to say so ; Don’t you write over the
’eads of this community, young man.” I didn’t.
The tug was to get to the level.
Mainly about Way :
Samuel James Way, who hopes to climb to a Life-Peer-
age with a seat in the Lords and personal acquaintance
with “ Wales,” is a little man of slightly over 5 feet, and
with equal proportions of volubility and energy. Samuel’s
father, a Bible-Christian parson, came to S.A. half-a-cen-
tury ago, and the future great and little Chief-Justice
was then a humble lad of 14. They apprenticed him to a
farmer, but the precocious youth rebelled, took to law,
and had the most brilliant Bar career in S.A. history.
Symon, recently a bitter critic, was one of his partners ;
and Kingston, articled to him for years, rebelled and
went on his own because he wasn’t made a partner.
Samuel appointed himself Chief-Justice in ’76, over the
heads of Gwynne and Stow, and what Gwynne said is
another story! Way has an ample fortune, and owns the
very farm to the owner of which he was apprenticed. His
recent indiscreet action in meddling in political matters
was the great mistake of his life, and alienated the in-
terest and friendship of his two most powerful friends—
Kingston and Symon.
An ecclesiastical link with the early days
of Victoria (then Port Phillip) died last week at
Launceston, Tasmania —Archdeacon Hales, well
up to his 80th year. He was born in the North of
Ireland and when four years old came with his
father, an Army officer, to Sydney. Was taken
soon after to Hobart and remained five years on
the Derwent. Thence he went to India, then
back to Ireland, graduated at T.C.D. in ’45, and
was ordained next year. Having married, he soon
after joined Bishop Perry and Dean Macartney
en route for Port Phillip (1847). He was stationed
first in Gippsland, then practically a terra
incognita, and afterwards at Heidelberg. In 1854
he became incumbent of Holy Trinity Church,
Launceston, a position he held until his death.
He was a very broad-minded man, an accomplished
scholar and writer, and was possibly the oldest
clergyman in active work in Australasia —Dean
Cowper, of Sydney, being now practically out of
harness.
Of S. Australia’s four Judges, including
Dashwood, of the N. Territory, all have been poli-
ticians, and all three southern men have been
Ministers. Judge Boucaut was Chief Justice
Way’s political chief ; but Way, being Attorney -
General, had the disposal of the vacant Chief
Justiceship. Boucaut was a member of six
Ministries, and led four of them. At different
times he held every portfolio except Education
and Chief Secretary. The lovable Bundey’s
Ministerial work was confined to the Attorney-
General’s, Justice and Education offices. J. H.
Symon had his one brief experience of office
as Mr. Bundey’s successor.
There is a suburban M.P. known through-
out his electorate as “Promising (say) Billy.”
He never straight-out refuses his constituents
anything, but ingeniously puts them off ; procras-
tination has been reduced by him to a fine art. A
carpenter out of work lately approached him with
a request to find him a job. “Well, well,” an-
swered the member, “What a pity ! I’ve just
this minute heard of a good thing for a plumber—
Government too. Suppose you can’t do plumb-
ing ? What hard luck ! But I’ll see what I can
do.” Applicant was a little doubtful about the
good faith of Promising Billy,” and sent a pal of
his to him to pose as a plumber hard-up for a
job— Govt, job, if possible. “Anything of a
stonemason?” queried the M.P. “Now that is
a pity. Everybody got his plumbing done when
the plague-scare was on. A big contractor down
the city has just asked me to look out for a good
stonemason. Couldn’t you take that on? Well,
1 am sorry. However, I won’t forget you next
chance I get.”

Aboriginalities.
“ Ghost ” : Aboriginals have some curious
methods of carrying things. The ear is a favorite
place for money. Any silver-piece, up to a “ bob ”
in size, can be safely stowed away therein —3d.-
pieces and 6d.’s inside, and a shilling to act as a
hd. Knives are generally carried on the head, the
blade being clasped down on to the hair. Matches
are also stowed away.in the hair, amongst other
things. Talking of hard heads, I once saw an
aboriginal nipper of 10 sitting on the ground and
holding one end of a steel drill in his hands, and
every now and then letting the other end fall on
his head, in .time with the song he was droning.
“My word,” I said, “you gotein hard fella
butta ’ (head).” “ Yaas,” sayshe, “ all same that
feller ! ’ —pointing to the drill.

“ Tin ray ” : “ W.J.” (B. 19/5/’00), re
ploughmen. Can’t recollect seeing a woman
ploughing, although have often heard cookies’
daughters talk about it. I have only worked for
cookies in harvest-time, and have then seen
women at all sorts of farm-work. Out from
Blanchetown. (S.A.), _ 1 once saw two girls
(Germans) driving a stripper each. I have worked
on haystacks with cookies’ daughters several
times, and once had a job wheat-cleaning on
Murray Flats (S.A.), with a young woman and
myself at the winnower, and the old man strip-
ping. I found winnower very heavy turning, and
got knocked-out quickly, whereupon the damsel
took the handle and turned away merrily. For
shame’s sake, I presently offered to give her a
spell, but smin. tired me, and she went ahead
again. At dinner-time, I told her I should have
to give up, whereupon she explained that she had
screwed a nut up every time I took the handle
and opened it when she did—just for devilment.

“R.H.”: What 1 no noxious rodents in
Bathurst ? Rats ! Years ago a mate and myself
camped in an empty house in that city of parks
and sharks, and, coming home late first night, we
found our flour-bags gnawed to pieces, and con-
tents scattered about the floor, while four or five
white-snouted ratshastened to their holes. Watched
next night, and killed seven. But, alas! they
weren’t worth sixpence a head then. This is im-
pressed on my memory because of the terrible
oracles we had to work along a hungry track to
accumulate said “ dust ” for a camping-day. Also,
in a casual way I here remind you that we’ve all
got rats —and some of us wombats.

‘ R-H.” : “ J.S.T.” (B. 30/6/’00) is right.
Galahs and cockatoos do strip the bark off limbs
containing their nests to keep goannas from raid-
ing the eggs or young birds. All Australian par-
rots do this as soon as a nest-place has been
chosen. Most of them only strip round entrance
of nest, but galahs often strip the whole limb
down to the trunk. But, alas !as soon as the
undressed limb is dry the goanna can climb it
easily. Also, the bark-stripping advertises the
situation of the nest to crows, snakes, goannas,
men, and other enemies; which goes to show that
even natural instincts are fallible.

“G.R.H.”: Re ca
nivorous plants. The pit
cher-plant (nepenthes) Fas
the mid-rib of its leaf
elongated into a stout ten-
dril, terminating in the
“pitcher.” This tendril,
between the blade and the
termination, is covered
with small hairs pointing
towards the cup, and per-
mitting insects to reach
same, but greatly retard-
ing, if not preventing,
their return. On reaching
the lid, they discover, on
the lower surface, a sweet
exudation; consumption
of which stupefies them
(“ Quaff, O quaff this kind
nepenthe !”) so that they
fall into the interior well
of plant, where they be-
come “beef-tea.” Looks
like deliberate catering,
doesn’t it ?

“ Matabele” to The Bulletin :
Be the suggested. acclimatisation in Australia of the
S. African eland. This animal is now so scarce south of
the Zambesi thah the British Government has proclaimed
it “ royal game,” thus bringing it under the protection of
a £lOO fine for its destruction. It will, nevertheless, in a
few years be quite extinct, save upon a few specially-
guarded reservations. The eland is the only antelope
which carries any fat, the rest, though sometimes as big
as a bullock, being as skinny as kangaroo. The largest
eland I have seen went 23001 b., and stood about 18 hands
high. We got two kerosene tins full of fat from it, and
the marrow of its leg-bones served as butter for days.
The bull eland has very thick, though short, horns, and,
despite his bulk, he can go at a long, swinging trot for
hours ; though he’s soon done if you gallop him hard from
the start. By the way, Elands-Laagte, of which we have
recently heard so much, means “ Elands Valley.”

And the pig is perhaps more susceptible to tuber-

“ S'elpme ” : Re animal chivalry. I know
of a camel (lead) and horse
(shaft) turn-out, in which the
pair work together as quietly
as bullocks: but when loose in
the yard, where they each have
a feed-box, trouble begins. The
camel usually gets outside his
feed first, and then charges the
moke open - mouthed, scares
him from his box, and sur-
rounds the balance of his feed.
When, however, the horse was
invalided for a time and the
camel went to toil on his
own, the latter each night
when turned-out would walk
up to the moke, have a look
at him, and then dispose of
his own supper without his
eu s t omary commandeering
proceeding. But as soon as
the horse was put to work
again, the camel renewed his
old games.

“ Canip ” : The centipede is an eagerly-
sought part of the echidna’s diet. In the bush
will often be seen pieces of heavy bark and flat
slivers of wood, recently turned over as if by man.
This is done by “ porcupine ” in search of ’pede.

“ Vril ” : Was for long baffled by a sen-
tinel wood-duck that used to post himself on a
dead tree whilst the flock fed, and give warning
with his “ nark-nark ! ” as soon as I was getting
nearly into range. So I jammpd a No. 1 wire-
cartridge into a 12 shell, with 3Jdr. of powder
behind, took a choke-bore, and sidled-up till the
old boy on guard began to get restless. Then 1
flopped on the ground, used about 2ft. Gin. eleva-
tion, and let her go. Result, a badly-bashed
cheek to me and death to the duck—single pellet
through head. Distance to foot of tree from firing
point, 106yds.

“Delta”: Re size of goannas (8.,
30/G/’00): Was camped with three others once
in S. Gippsland, near Tarwin. River, and one
morning a hoary old goanna came crashing like a
bullock through the ti-tree, steered straight to the
camp, and proceeded to get. outside potato-peel,
decayed fish and all the camp refuse scattered
round. Came to breakfast every morning after
that, and kept the camp clean of scraps till we left.
Don’t know his length for a certainty, but one day
he crawled through the 6xB tent, and, before his
tail was half through the door, his head was out
prospecting under the canvas at the back.
“ Sfcon ” : Curious that a male emu will
sit on a batch of eggs until they are half-hatched ;
then leave, and, when the young are hatched, try
at every chance to kill them ; though his mur-
derous intentions are generally defeated by the
care the hen takes to keep the chicks out of their
unnatural sire’s way.

off the share 1

No demand for wheat in M.L. this year
—nothing but “oats,” “oats.” An Otago farmer
lately placed a line with a local firm for sale. An
offer was obtained, and submitted to a farmer who
indignantly rejected same, saying it would pay
him better to feed rats on the wheat and sell them
at the current price of 4s. a dozen.
f “ A.J.H. ” : Near Moss Yale, about Nov.
’B9, I chased a fair-sized black snake into a clean-
bottomed waterhole, where, after crawling around
awhile, he lay still. I thought, “ All right—you
can’t stay there long, I’ll wait for you; ” but
after watching him for fully an hour, I became
tired, and got a pole to jab him where he lay.
Had often seen snakes swimming, but did not
know they could stay under water and on the
bottom for so long. Query: Is there any limit to
the time they can do so ?

“ Thurla ” : Re the inquisitiveness of the
emu (“ Eller,” B. 16/6/’00). I have often brought
emus into easy gunshot by just waving my hat
Once brought a mob of nine to within 20yds of
me by waving my bridle and then standing quite
still. Two shooters on Calga station profited
much by this characteristic, as, when the squatters
on the plains were giving Is. a head, they scored
700 m three weeks, using for a lure a red parasol
Emus at times are very destructive to the young
lambs, racing after and killing them by jumping
on and kicking them; hence the head-money on
stations during lambing. J
THE MULGA MAIL .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Here no swift hoofs fling the gravel,
Here no golden headlights glow;
There are ways for news to travel
Other than by Cobb and Co.!
Like a worn and storm-tossed sailer,
Broken-masted and distressed,
Now and then some bluegrass whaler
Drifts upon us from the West .'
And we gather items daily
As these derelicts go by;
How they’re shearing on Belalie;
How the Cuttaburra's dry;
How the Border’s red with ruin
Fiom the rabbit and the drought;
How the floods have filled the Tuen
From the big rains further out.
And we bring the beef and damper,
And we boil the billy slow,
While the blear-eyed river-camper
Names the names of long ago.
Heavy roads and knocked-up horses,
Storm-stayed steamers, rifted rail l ,
Flooded creeks and watercourses
Never stop the Mulga mails.
And the talcs of passing shearers,
As they steer their lean steeds South,
Are like music to their hearers
And like honey in the mouth.
So they name the well-known places,
And we listen as they speak
Of the fun at Culgoa races
And the feed on Wild Horse Creek.
Western mails! And we are reading!
And we feel to them like friends!
Let them leave their horses feeding
On our richest river-bends !
Let them stay and talk a season —
There is time enough to roam !
And our hearts make plain the reason—
They have brought us news from home!
WILL OGILVIE.

A beautiful story, which ought to be true,
though it probably isn’t, comes from the United
States. A big brewery was being erected in a
Pennsylvaniatown, and the local Methodist parson,
having used in vain all ordinary means of preven-
tion, prayed that divine vengeance would fall on
the accursed beer emporium and wreck it. A
few days later lightning struck the brewery and
set it on fire, and it ivas wrecked. Some other
places that had not been prayed against were also
wrecked—but that is a side issue. Now the own-
ers are suing the parson for 50,000 dollars—the value
of one brewery destroyed by some person acting
at his instigation —and he lias either to disclaim
all responsibility by saying that his prayers were
utterly useless and had no influence at all in the
matter, or else admit responsibility for the
damage. And as the destruction of breweries is
illegal the latter isn’t a thing to be done lightly.
K uppers Elberfeld Lager.
“ Geoff.” : Daring the ’9B heat-wave
I was, with a mate* putting up a line of
split-fence on a N.S.W. station, and we
had to hump the timber a considerable dis-
tance on our backs (112° in shade). After
finishing that job we were put on to weed
the corn-crop with our hands; but after a
day of this my mate “performed.” “ First
of all,” said he to the boss, “you make a
horse of a man, carrying timber; then you
turn him into a scarifier. Why, d you,
you’ll want me next to swallow a ball of
'twine and make myself into a reaper-and-
binder!” Then he kicked nearly half-an-
acre of corn over the fence with his great
feet, and left.

“Moa” : Re cowdung as fuel (B.
l/5/’OO). In many of the elevated mining
regions of Otago (M.L.), where there's no
timber but manuka-scrub and brown lignite
coal is expensive, cowdung is gathered and
stocked in summer for use as fuel in winter.
A thin, dry cake burns brightly, but if the
stuff is thick or damp it smoulders and
smokes. I remember once how tw
thrifty Scotch women, who were neighboi
and each owned a “ coo,” used to havemucl
altercation at the gathering of the fuel, a
to which particular coo it belonged to.
Matters even came to a court-case.
“ B.B.B.” : Wat T. (8., 23/6/’00), in
inquiring about the “ Coolawine,” refers, I
think, to that rare native animal known to
a few South Australian aboriginal tribes as
the “ Koo-loowin.” It is similar in shape to
the river-turtle, and has a soft down on the
body; which latter, when “plucked,” feels
like gutta-percha. The creature burrows in
the earth, and is of nocturnal habit. Have
never seen one myself, but possibly some of
your contributors can enlighten.
Gran. : The “ coolawine ” enquired about
(“Wat T.,” 8., 23/6/’00) is simply our old friend
the koala, or native bear, under a new name. I
fancy the term is aboriginal, and has references to
the animal’s peculiar voice. “ Coolawine ” and
“wherang” (orthography not guaranteed) for
bear and rock-wallaby, are commonly used on
some of the coastal rivers.

“ Imray ” reminisces interestingly :
A departed industry that is worth an inky tear is cod-
fishing on the Murray. The plant was inexpensive—£2 or
£3 would buy boat, lines, hooks, net, and a month’s
tucker. Then you drifted down stream till you saw a
likely fishing-ground, and you pitched your camp on the
river bank in a land where it never rains. With your
tomahawk you then cut (say) 50 “ springers ” (a springer
is a tapering mallee rod, perhaps 10 feet long, pointed at
the thick end), which you carried in the boat, and where
you thought fit drove one into the bank, overhanging the
water. Five feet of schnapper-line hung to the end of
this, with half-way down a swivel like that of a dog-chain,
a sinker and a large hook. Then at the end of the rod a
bell —the further from the camp, the bigger the bell ;
nearer, a jam-tin with a stone in it would do.
Then for bait you netted silver bream in the
shallow lagoon close by, cut a large one into
four and baited your hooks a little before sun-
Bust of aboriginal woman, by Nelson Illing-
worth, recently purchased by the Trustees of
N.S.W. National Art Gallery.
down. Presently you heard a bell ring. Into your
boat for perhaps half a mile pull up stream. The boat (a
light flat) went fast, and the bell kept jangling. Leaning
over the boat you felt the line, and the springer replied
to the fish’s plunge. Carefully you placed your hand in
the gills and pulled in, maybe, a 401 b. cod. With a pack-
ing needle you threaded a tether (swivel and all like your
line) through the frog of the under-jaw or through the
nose, and tying up to a stiff peg in the running stream —
not in an eddy or a backwash —your fish lived for a fort-
night. Then, listen! Another bell, down stream this
time. Away you went, ran up alongside and repeated
the process. About 11 they ceased biting, and you turned
in till daybreak. When you had a ton or two, you lashed
your dry pine logs together, hitched the tethered fish
around the frame, tied the raft behind your boat, and
drifted down stream to Morgan (S.A.) At first the
fish would plunge and rear like a mob of colts, and some
“ AFTER LIFE’S FITFUL FEVER ” THE PICTURE PAINTED BY MR. JOE WOLINSKI, IN
COMPETITION FOR THE N.S.W. SOCIETY OF ARTISTS’ TRAVELLING SCHOLARSHIP.
would drown, but they got quite tame by and by, and you
could lean over and scratch tbe backs of some of the
biggest and most sensible with a piece of stick, and they
liked it too. The big fish, however, were not the
best to eat; and anything over 301 b. or 401 b.
was rank—6 or 7-pounder being the best meat. On a po’e
near the railway-station you hoisted your fish, prepared
them according to instructions, and packed them on the
train. At the post-office you would find your cheque for
the last lot waiting. Ah, me 1 Going to town. Those
glorious nights upon the Murray 1 Thebroad, moonlitriver,
the gaunt gums arid towering cliffs; the wail of the
distant dingo from the muttering scrub ; the opium-
lethargy of drifting. And then we’d meet one of Ohaffey’s
three-deckers ploughing up-stream, with its electric-light
ablaze on the trees, and the awakened cockatoos a-shriek-
ing. Its after-wash made quite a storm for our little
boat, but we lay back and listened to the music and the
singing as we drifted round a bend. Well Iwe earned a
decent living, but they turned their King William street
cookies loose on us, ploughing, ringing, building and
destroying, with their haggard looks and bent backs,
sordid and slaving because of the earth earthy, and we
passed on.
Officially reported that during 1899 the
drought reduced the number of sheep in N.S.W.
by 5,027,490, and cattle by 83,693.
* * • »
The determined front which so many
shearers are showing against shearing cancerous
sheep is to the credit of the men and the interest of
the public at large. In fact, the law should keep
off the market the wool of sheen recognised to be
cancered. The latest rule of the Pastoralists’
Association, “ that no shearer will be compelled
to shear wet or cancered sheep,” does not go far
enough. Its very statement makes its meaning
suspicious.
When the Rockhampton butchers lately
raised the price of meat, a Cheap Meat Co. started,
and did roaring business for two days, when an
inspector appeared and kerosened all the meat on
the co.’s premises, alleging it to be emaciated and
“unfit,” &c. Then the C.M. Co.’s manager (who,
by-the-way, previous to purchasing, had had the
meat passed by a Govt, inspector) called in the
principal Health Officer, who pronounced it
wholesome. There’s a fish-like smell about that
kerosening transaction strong enough to taint all
the meat in Rockhampton.

The notorious Isaac Gordon, probably
England’s cleverest and most infamous usurer, who
was lately foreclosed on by the coffin-maker,
hated his family with a fine healthy hatred, and
during his life resolutely refused to have any deal-
ings with them. He liked to see all his clerks
well dressed, and whenever he found one wearing
shabby clothes he invariably bought him a new
suit —if he hadn’t done so just immediately before.
No clerk was ever known to ring-in a double on
him. But Isaac loathed his brothers and sister,
and the only time he offered one of them 6d. was
when he volunteered to pay the youngest brother’s
passage to Russia provided he would stay there
for life. He had a wonderful belief in himself,
and when told that he hadn’t a week to live
he scoffed and informed the medico that he
would see him under the sod. So he disregarded
the warnings, and went on his way to death cheer-
fully, and neglected to make a will. As a conse-
quence his four brothers and one sister retire from
any further financial worry with £lOO,OOO each.
It will make Isaac curse in his —well, wherever he
is. Nearly £30,000 was found in Gordon’s rooms
after death. He had to keep it there. Bankers
are not a sensitive race, but latterly the name of
the ferocious usurer smelled so badly that no
reasonably-safe institution would take his account.
To keep it meant losing so many other accounts.
When you treat a friend do not offer him
anything counterfeit. Helidon Spa has many
imitators, but none possess the valuable properties
which have won for it the public’s approbation.*

R. Torhoe : The British, French and Russians took part
of the Chinese territory (Hongkong, part of Manchuria,
&c.), and the Chows want it back. The British took
much Boer territory (Natal and the Diamond Fields, to
say nothing of the Cape itself), and the worst thing
alleged against the Boers is that they wanted it back.
The Boers objected to the Outlanders who came among
them to get gold. The Chinese objected to the Outlanders
who came among them to get converts. Both wanted to
get rid of the foreigners. Both did the first act of open war.
But the Boers only gave the Outlanders notice to leave
quietly by train ; the Chinese went in straight for slaugh-
tering them wholesale. And yet this country went raving
mad against the decent white opponent, while it doesn’t
rave worth a cent against the murderous yellow one

“A.G.T.” criticises the Sydney Paint-ers’-
scholarship pictures:
The general impression no doubt is that Wolinski should
have got the scholarship. But the scholarship was for
three things—painting of a head, a landscape, and com-
position. The composition, naturally, would rank first.
As it turned out, Wolinski did the best composition,
Lambert the best head, and Howard Ashton the only
landscape in the show. Wolinski threw the scholarship
away; looking at his work as a whole, it is difficult to
realise that the same man painted “ Life’s Fitful Fever”
(it deserves all praise)—the utterly empty, gaudy land-
scape on one side of it, and the flat, badly-handled head
on the other. They were things he had by him, and his
landscape was painted years ago. Lambert’s work is
more or less equal, and the head that got him the scholar-
ship is a capital piece of painting : for his composition is
not much more than meritorious. The color is not too
good, and for such a draughtsman the drawing in the
figures is poor ; the girl’s hip has lost itself in her lumbar
regions, and one arm might have been modelled in wax
and melted out of shape a bit, and the legs of both figures
seem out of proportion. Howard Ashton came second.
His landscape is there—skilful, simple, very
refined and poetical; if his composition had
been anything fat all. Unluckily, it can’t be
excused. What was Howard Ashton’s was common and
gross in conception, badly drawn, and violently and
crudely colored : the rest of the picture was, more or less,
Greiffenhagen’s, taken from “ The Judgment of Paris,”
and not improved in the transition. The best man has
got the scholarship: the “Black Soil Plains” proves
that; only it’s a pity he did not make a better show.
Embryonic Art.—A Black-and- White man’s son
and heir, aged 7. displays a turn for the morbid.
Compare Australian treatment of Presi-
dent Kruger—the lampoons, the effigies, the in’
suits—with this from one of “Smiler” Hales-
letters to the Daily News :
In many of the public places pictures of our Queen and
members of the Eoyal Family have been hanging side by
side with portraits of notable men, such as Mr. Gladstone,
Lord Salisbury, Mr. Chamberlain, and Mr. Rhodes.
During the course of the war all kinds and conditions of
Boers hxve had free access to the rooms where those por-
traits to be seen, but now I find that no damage has
been done to any of those pictures, excepting those of
Mr. Rhodes and Mr. Chamberlain. This has not been an
oversight on the part of the Boers, for I defy any person
to find a solitary picture of the two last-named gentlemen
that has not been hacked with knives. But the Queen
and Royal Family photos, have in every case been treated
with respect.

“It was stated a few days ago that
the distinguished Chinese statesman, Li
Hung Chang, had been summoned from
Canton to Pekin, and that he was medi-
ating for a cessation of hostilities, but in
response to the requests of tfie foreign
Consuls in the southern city, who feared
that his departure would be the signal
for a revolution there, he abandoned the
journey."
Poor old Li ! After what happened to
his yellow things during the war with
Japan he will be lucky if-he has a stitch
left to his back when the present compli-
cations are over.
Premier Lyne is a man of mystery. He
is always “ declining to express an
opinion ” or receiving secret cablegrams,
the contents of which wild horses could
not drag out of him. Let’s see : How
would he make up as a Sphinx ?
The Chinese have a humorous way of cutting off the feet
of their prisoners of war and then dumping the stumps
* into boiling tar.
Small Bo? (to returned veteran): “ Here S bite
boots yer ordered before yer went to the
war, and the boss says he must have the
money.”
Melbourne, Sunday.—Energetic preparations
are being made among the Victorian naval forces
for the despatch of 200 men, accepted by the
Imperial Government, for service in China. The
Government has decided that the rate of pay shall
be 7s. 6d. a day for the men,
with proportionate increases
for warrant - officers and
officers.

A scheme for combining Melbourne,
North Melbourne, Flemington, and Kensington
in one municipality, and thus saving three need-
less mayors and town-clerks and other appurten-
ances, is trying to get into shape. But it is a
solemn fact that, though Sydney, Brisbane, Mel-
bourne, and Adelaide have all been thinking hard
for a long time about consolidating their much
sub-divided and misgoverned cities under one
strong municipal government on a democratic
basis, nothing at all has been accomplished as yet.
There have been tons of literature and acres of
conversation about union, and everywhere the
thing is still only “ in the air.”
An Australian lady of alleged pleasure died
some time ago. For 30 years she had been the
strange woman, mentioned by Solomon, whose
footsteps lead down to Gehenna, and Solomon
was an authority on strange women. But at last
she felt the cold, skinny fingers of approaching
dissolution at her painted throat. She sent for
some of the good Sisters of the class who go round
fighting Satan in the last ditch of an ill-spent life.
They came quickly, the assistant sirens were sent
away, and the large bull-dog-faced gentleman-
boarder Jackey, who was wont to keep the unruly
guests in order, and wallop the lady of pleasure
herself when she required it, was placed on a
limited supply of beer and stole about the mansion
in awe-struck silence. He told gents who called
in the small hours that “ the old woman was going
to peg-out and that the place wasbust-up.” He also
informed them that no matter what happened he
was orlrite—the “old woman” was worth
thousands, and he was to be the heir. The lady,
with the contrariety of her sex, had paid him good
wages for spanking her, till at last he felt that she
really owed him a debt of gratitude. The
only thing that troubled him was that he
might have to share the lot with a certain
Christian Chow for whom the “old woman’
had always evinced a most singular affection.
The Chow was the other standing curiosity of
the establishment. He had been converted by
the Salvation Army, and had called at the den of
pleasure to remonstrate with the owner on her
sins, but, having come to pray, he stayed to j
whoop. Thereafter, he remained a converted
Chinaman, but with a weekly relapse—every
Thursday evening he came in and became drunk
at the “old woman’s” expense. Outside the
house, Jackey and the Converted Chow and the
Sisters who were wrestling to save the old harri-
dan’s soul were supposed to be about equal
favorites for the money. At last, the painted-one
died penitent and made a blessed and beautiful
end. Her will left nothing to the Converted
Chow, and stated that the deceased was so shocked
at the wicked life led by her assistant and
partner, the bull-faced one, that he wasn’t to
get a cent. It went on to say that the writer
was grieved and heart-broken at the wicked-
ness of “the girls”—the former denizens of
her house, out of whom she had made her money
—and that if any of these fearful creatures ever
approached the now-purified domicile, they were
to be deservedly “run-in.” The document ex-
pressed the testator’s undying gratitude to the
Sisters who had tended her, but sajd nothing
about money. It bequeathed £lO to testator’s
very aged mother, and expressed a hope that the
decrepit old party would repent. And then it
left the rest of the pile—a good many thousands—
to a Protestant mission in China ! And the things
thfit various people said, about the painted woman
who had repented were numerous.
For IDore Than One Hundred years
SOAP
has maintained its supremacy in the face of the whole
world’s competition. Such a record could not bt
achieved without cause. Temporary successes are com
paratively easy, but for an article to maintain its popu-
larity generation after generation it must appeal to
something more than passing fancy. This is the case
with Pears’ Soap. It is, and always has been, an honest
product. Everywhere abroad it has found a place in
public favour equal to that for so long held in England.
Men and women alike find it good and reliable. The
man who has once tried Pears’ Soap in the form of a
Shaving Stick wants no other, he takes it with him on all
his journeys. The woman who travels and fails to take
a supply of Pears’ must put up with substitutes until
her burning, smarting skin inexorably demands the
« matchless for the complexion.”

POLITICAL
POINTS
Seddon advocated sending a contingent to
China “because it would make the whole outside
world talk of us, and ask ‘ Where is New Zea-
land?’ “Butchered to make a Maoriland
ad vertisement. ”
John Norton, judging by a letter printed
in his own paper, is evidently a candidate for the
Federal Senate. Also, he will probably be Mayor
of Sydney if election is determined, as proposed,
by the vote of the people.
For the Federal Parliament, S.A. Labor
Party has decided to run five of its own men—
three for the House of Representatives, two for
the Senate. The Party takes a preliminary ballot,
choosing among 16 Barkises.
The irony of it ! W. W. Collins, M.P.
for Christchurch (M.L.), is bringing in a Bill to
abolish capital punishment. Yet Collins is an
ardent Jingo, and was quite eager to send hun-
dreds of men to S’Africa to kill perfect strangers.
Looks very much as though Kingston and
Holder have broken at last, or must break. It is
wonderful that they have worked in harmony so
long. There will not be room for both Kingston
and Holder, two South Australians, in the first
Federal Ministry, and if Barton forms it Kingston
ought to be a certainty, if he cares to go in. Hol-
der’s chance disappeared when Reid’s chances of
the Premiership were frozen out. The long S. A.
figure-juggler will, it is said, .reach after an
inter-State commissionership.
Laurenson, member for Lyttelton (M.L.),
says that Federation would be a
serious matter for his province,
because in a Federal Parliament
Maoriland could always be out-
voted by the other provinces.
Obviously, therefore, the only
remedy would be to give Maori-
land so many members that it
could always outvote all the other
provinces. But Laurenson should
look nearer home where the same
painful state of things exists. His
own town of Lyttelton (popula-
tion 4200) is always liable to be
outvoted in the Maoriland House
of Reps by the other parts of the
couutrj (population 780,000).
Therefore it is quite plain that
Lyttelton will never be safe till
it gets enough members of its
own to outvote all the rest of
Maoriland. In fact, no place will
be safe till every place has more
Parliamentary representation
than all • the other places put
together. This matter should be
looked to.
Dr. Cockburn’s term of
office as S.A. Agent-General ex-
pires next April, and, with
Federation so near, it is doubtful
whether he will be re-appointed,
although he is pleased with the
po3t and has been a great success in it.
He would like to be Federal High Commissioner,
but some local statesman, of whom the dominant
party in Parliament wishes to be rid, will most
likely get that cosy and lucrative billet.
Friends who are in Kingston's confidence
state that, when the right time comes, he will
stand for the Federal Parliament, as every M.P.
is willing to release him from his hasty pledge that
he will stav in local politics until the Leg. Council
agrees to Household Suffrage.
Most opulent Australian city Chinese resi-
dents are believed to be favorable to the Reform
Party. The few interviewed decline to express
definite opinions for fear of assassination on their
return to the Flowery Land, or even in Australia,
at the hands of the secret societies.
The engineer of Vic. water-supply depart-
ment presents a huge scheme for making an arti-
ficial river, about 365 miles long through the sad,
dry west at a cost of nearly 2| millions. This
great work is to amble about half-way across Vic-
toria and moisten, in the west country, 3860
farms of 2560 acres each.
Minister Crick’s announcement that under-
stamped letters addressed to members at Parlia-
ment House, Sydney, will be considered official,
and that members will thus escape the shortage
and fine, amounts to an invitation to all corres-
pondents to so address members and send their
letters understamped for economy’s sake.
After being knighted, only two South
Australians have ever formed a
Ayers, in the early seventies, and Jbhm-Downer,
in the early nineties. And neither Ministry
lasted much over a twelvemonth. In-Ayers’ case
it was also simply a reconstruction, though not a
single one of his old colleagues rejoined him.
The result of Bundaberg (Q,) election
shows that Glassey(who achieved an almost 2 to 1
majority) picked his constituency well. Bunda-
berg is a sugar-district, living on kanaka labour,
and all the employers’ influence went for the
former opponent of kanaka labour. Boodlewraith
came out without disguise to help its new ally.
A newly-appointed member of N.S.W.
Leg. Council admitted the other day the proba-
bility of the abolition of that Chamber on the
establishment of Federation, but asserted as an
opinion common among the Councillors that
when the Chamber is abolished all members then
6 k® authorised to retain the title
Hon.” and carry a free railway-pass while they
livtf.
There will be much heart-burning in the
Q. Labor camp when the wily Philp introduces
his private-railway schemes. There are certain
Northern constituencies hungering and thirsting
for railways State lines, if possible, but railways
at all costs. Some of aforesaid constituencies are
represented by Labor legislators, who will be
sorely tried and tempted when the scramble
comes.
Such entries as “ unforeseen expenses ” in
N.S.W. Estimates should be closely scrutinised
by members of Assembly. Under such headings
are crowded most of the mean little jobs which
Ministers wish to hide. Although paltry in
amount, the. way the sums are sneaked in is often
so contemptible that if Parliament were brought
face to face with the fact tricky Ministers would
lose office.
Chaffey Baker, President of S.A. Leg.
Council, considers himself almost a certainty for a
similar billet in the Federal Senate. Baker is not
so young as he looks (he is within a year of 60),
and has been in S.A. politics for over 30 years.
He was Education Minister in Colton’s Govt, of ’B5,
his other colleagues being Kingston, Coles (now
Speaker), Playford and Rounsevell. Baker has
ever been opposed to the masses ; he practically
founded S.A. National Ass.
N.S. YVales Parliamentary roll-collectors
should, while collecting the names of males for the
forthcoming elections, collect also the names of
females of full age. It would be very little
extra trouble, and a comprehensive roll would
then be ready for the first election after the pass-
ing of the Woman-Suffrage Bill. If the Bill fail no
harm will be done, whereas if women be now
omitted the whole ground will have to be gone
over again before females can vote.
When some Victorian Government rises
up and really builds the Mildura railway, that
province will be about as much staggered as
Sydney will be when its North Shore bridge and
city railway are finished. Most provinces have
some petrified proposition on hand—an old stock
job which has been so much
talked about that the way to
do it has been forgotten. The
North Island Trunk railway
and the Otago Central are
Maoriland’s petrified propo-
sitions; the Mildura line is
Victoria’s ; the Sydney city
extension is N.S.W.’s.

The Westralian Parochialists have two
tariff jokes. One is that under Federation the
high tariff of Victoria will be fastened on Wes-
tralia, and that province will be ruined by taxa-
tion. The other is that under Federation the low
tariff of Victoria will be fastened on Westralia,
and that province will be ruined by insufficient
revenue. And both yarns seem to do equally
good service.
A Popular Convention is suggested to
formulate a scheme for effecting Constitutional
economies rendered necessary in S. A. by the com- -
ingof Federation. Tory-boss Stirling, dictator
of the Council, however, boasts that he will pre-
vent the Convention from receiving power to con-
sider either the extension of the Council franchise
or the advisableness of doing with only one House
after Australian union comes.
Under the U.S. shipping laws no foreign-
owned vessel can engage in the coasting trade— i.e.
can trade between one U.S. port and another!
The U;S. annexation of Hawaii makes the trade
• between that port and San Francisco a coasting
trade which can only be carried on by American-
owned vessels. So the U.S.S. Co. of Maoriland
has to drop out of the San Francisco trade or else
leave out Honolulu as a port of call.
The statement in the Australian dailies
that Admiral Seymour intended waiting for Sept,
before marching on Pekin reads very queerly to
anyone who knows the locality. The Peiho River
and a large part of the Gulf bf Pechili are frozen
over m Nov., and even the inner waters of Chifu,
Port Arthur, and Wei-hai-Wei are not always free
from ice. So as much of the campaign as remains
unfinished by end of Oct. must needs stand over
till Spring—March, in that quarter.
Wharton, M.P.’s Flogging Bill was
rejected in the (Tory-controlled) House
of Commons by 123 votes. Eleven
years ago the same Bill triumphantly
passed its second reading, though for
other reasons it did not become law.
Even more significant than the
numerical turn over was the personal
value of the debate ; for the Home
Secretary, who backed the Bill in ’B9,
now spoke strongly against it, and ex-
plained his change of conviction by the
fact that “ he had seen something of the
administration of the criminal law
since he had become Home Secretary .”
South Australia, with but a
handful of people, and with a mania
once for changing Govts., gives some
men what surely come near being
fiolitical records. R. B. Andrews
afterwards Judge) was Attorney-
General seven times. Arthur Blyth
(afterwards Agent-General) was in 11
Ministries ; and so was Henry Ayers.
John Hart was in nine Ministries ;
and the whole lot lasted only 4’ years
42 days. A dozen men in the pro-
vince have held office for less than 20
days.
“ Brahmin ” to The Bulletin :
These Indian famines are chiefly due to the European
way of doing- things. Every spare ounce of wheat or
other cereal is bought up and sent out of the country,
when the monsoons fail, the empire is starving l in*a
month. In the days of good King Akber, Govt, grana-
ries were constructed which held millions of tons of grain
They used to laugh at famines then, but now the hat has
to be sent round all over the world for the starving ryot.
King Akber s granaries still exist, but they are always
empty. The money-lender and the merchant live on
India s famines.
A short Bill, now before N.S.W. Parlia-
ment, provides that any person passing himself off
as a doctor when he isn’t one may be fined £5O;
also £5 a day for the continuance of the offence, or
12 months gaol. When Australia becomes a little
more honest and thorough it will go further and
impose a £5O fine on the man who professes to sell
a bankrupt or salvage stock at 50 per cent, reduc-
tion when he is only selling his own old stock with
10 per cent. off. Likewise the man who sells the
fuaranteed hair-restorer which doesn’t restore anv
air.
Victoria has already been divided up into
23 electorates for the Federal Parliament which
have all received native-Australian names except
Brunswick and the Melbourne constituencies.
“ The hon. member for Joorweit, Yalooka, Indei
or Boonurrong ” will perhaps sound strangely at
first, but it is to be hoped that the admirable
precedent will be followed by N.S.W. and the
other federating provinces. Waterloo, Robertson,
Manning, St. George, Hay, and such-like old-
world names can be well left to the local legisla-
. ture.
The new Melbourne evening paper will,
it is said, be an accomplished fact ere long.
Mayor M‘Eacharn (a candidate for Federal
Parlt.) must have his organ to fight Fink, who
already has his organ—the Herald. Some queer
things will be seen in the newspaper world before
long. One man ambitious of political honors—he
has been a failure so far—has conceived the
brilliant idea of getting his paw on several local
papers, and wants to amalgamate two “society ”
weeklies. But if it comes off others are ready to
spring up.
Premier Seddon has received a decided set-
back from the Imperial Govt., which has declined
to be enthusiastic over that annoyed politician’s
scheme for a black-and-yellow South Sea Federa-
tion without first consulting thfe Commonwealth
of Australia. As the Australian constitution was
framed without reference to Maoriland, and as his
own impudent “protests” after the Bill had
passed to the Imperial Parliament were ignored,
the relative importance of Australia as compared
with his own little kingdom should now be forcibly
brought home to the gentle Richard.
Three anti-Federal yarns from Perth
Morning Herald published in this order :
The Federal Government will require £7,170,000 to
enable the departments taken over to be kept going, to
provide for the “ new services,” and to enable the quota
of three-fourths of the net revenue to be returned to the
States.
It. is admitted that, whereas £6,000,000 is now collected
by the colonies, £8,000,000 must be collected under
Federation.
We are told on the authority of Mr. Coghlan that the
cost of federal government will be at least £2,500,000 per
annum—probably much more. This implies th") raising
of a £10,000,000 tariff. °
If the Referendum had only been delayed i nonth
longer Brother Alick’s paper would pi ibably
have swelled its prospective Federal taiiff to
£40,000,000 a year before it was finished.
Tasmania proposes to extend its Leg.
Council franchise by giving a vote to every holder
of a freehold worth £lO a year (instead of £2O) or
a leasehold worth £3O a year (instead of £80). It
is further intended to establish manhood suffrage
for the House of Assembly, whereas the present
suffrage gives a vote to persons who are on the
Assessment Roll as property-owners, or who have
put in 12 months residence and have an income of
or exceeding £4O a year. Also, the Bill now
before Parliament abolishes the time-wasting pro-
cess by which Ministers have to seek re-election
on taking office, and enacts that no member shall
hold a seat in State and Federal Parliaments
simultaneously. Which is good, so far as it goes.
If it went further and put both Houses on a man-
hood-suffrage basis, the Council to be elected by
the island as one constituency ; provided for a
joint sitting in case of deadlocks ; cut down the
membership one-half, and doubled the salaries, it
would be still better.

which seems inseparable from Parochialism.
Gardiner stated that out of the 11,515 persons
engaged in manufacturing operations in Wes-

George Throssell, the loudest and bitterest
Parochialist of the Westralian Govt, and a leader-
of the anti-Federal movement, in a letter to Perth
Morning Herald :
While I am as certain as you can be that we cannot for
long keep out of the union, that the mere impact of four
millions of people upon 180,000 must, of necessity, sooner
or later force us into it, and, further, that we are probably
exaggerating the evils likely to attend federation for this
colony, our resources being too great and too varied to
permit ruin to result—still, believing all this, seeing that
it is acknowledged that our position will be much inferior
to that we now occupy, and possessing the privilege of
the referendum, I conceive that we should make a bold
fight in support of our convictions.
When even the leaders have taken to calling
theirs a lost cause what cheer is there for the
human beasts of burden who hump the ammuni-
tion at the rear of the Parochialist army ?
About the finest sample of Provincialisb
assurance and mendacity up to date is the declara-
tion of Perth (W.A.) Morning Herald that the
Federal Govt, will introduce cheap colored labor
and “all our (Westralia’s) restrictive laws in
regard to alien labor will go by the board.”
This is especially good coming from the Chow
and Afghan and all-manner-of-miscellaneous-
nigger-lnuinted land of slavery—the land where
they “indenture” the unsophisticated black-
fellow. The evidence assigned is that section 26,
clause 51 of the constitution gives the Federal •
Parliament power to make laws dealing with
The people of any race, other than the aboriginal race
in any State, for whom it is deemed necessary make
special laws.
The clause obviously gives the Federal Parliament
power to shut out, expel, tax, or otherwise dis-
courage the cheap black and yellow laborer, and
it was obviously inserted for that purpose. Bub
the anti-Federal gang, with that glorious men-
dacity which makes a big halo round its head,
generally speaks of this as a clause providing for
the wholesale importation of niggers. Sydney
Telegraph started that amazingly impudent per-
version, and Perth Morning Herald borrows th&
second-hand lie of the East and passes it off
proudly as its own lie.
VROW’S STOCKINGS AND ORDINARY
MAN’S SOCK.
A correspondent at Draghoender writes: “These are
regarded as one of the curiosities of the campaign. They
describe more fully than words the fatness of the Boera.
This photo, is of an average size of many stockings that
are kept for constant sale by the local storekeeper ”
Sydney Evening News, June 30.
This is surely the climax of the war-correspondent's
exploits.

Newcastle, April 24,1900
Mr. S. A. Palmer.
Dear Sir, —For the past twelve years I have been a
sufferer. I have been treated by doctors in Wallsend,
Hamilton, Sydney, Rockhampton, and various places, but
without success ; they all failed to diagnose my case.
Some said I suffered from kidneys out of order, ulcers on
stomach—enlarged liver —pleurisy, &c. ; but Vitadatio
soon showed the disease, and I am now heavier than ever
I was in my life. The hydatids have come away in great
quantities, as witness to this I have given two bottles of
same to your manager in Newcastle. I am thankful for
the good health Vitadatio has given me, and will be 1
willing at all times to tell others of its marvellous virtues.
(Signed.) EDWARD ELLIS,

Jessep, M.P. for Waverley, whoso fervidly
denounced the action of the Lyne Govt, in (as he
averred) illegally providing funds for a new
operating-theatre at St. Vincent’s Hospital,
Sydney, and who was mainly responsible for the
sectarian outburst last week in N.S.W. Assembly,
was made to look very foolish by the publication
of a letter he wrote in June, ’9B, applying to Govt,
for 50 pairs of blankets for the local branch of the
(R.C.) St. Vincent de Paul Relief Society. Jessep
had a great run for the Waver ley seat in July, ’9B,
and his religious views did not prevent him from
cadging votes from the R.C. portion of his con-
stituents. As a matter of fact, there was nothing
illegal in the Government’s grant to St. Vincent’s
(which, by the way, is the hospital used by the
men of the Australian squadron), and as to Govt,
inspection, it is clearly set forth in the Act that
any institution accepting Govt, aid is inspectable
by Govt. In justice to Jessep, it must be added
that his pretext for the attack (this hasn’t been
pointed out in the papers) is that the Ministry,
instead of openly making the grant, stowed the
item away among “Unforeseen Expenses.”
The Acetylene Miners’ Lamps, introduced by
the Acetylene Gas 00. of Australia, Ltd., have proved a
great success and are now largely used in the mines
throughout the colony. The company has lately been
rushed with orders for lighting up gold-mining dredges
owing to the many advantages offered by the light for
that purpose. It would be well for mine and dredge
owners to ascertain full information as to cost, etc., from
the Head Office of the Company, 36 Pitt-street, Sydney.*
The Bundaberg Philpocrats made a bad
blunder when they nominated and subsequently
withdrew Duffy as Ministerial candidate. Up to
this remarkable stroke Glassey had posed as the
Independent Democrat, but the withdrawal of
Duffy at the instigation of the Govt, (in the
interests of Glassey) placed the ex-Leader of the
Labor-party in his true colours as a political rene-
gade, pure and simple. But a few months ago
the Q. Govt, organs were unanimous in deriding
Glassey as a legislative blunderer of the first water,
and most sensible Labor men were uneasily con-
scious that the strictures were well deserved. All
of a sudden the Courier, and the small fry that
follow in the Courier’s wake, discovered that
T.G. was a heaven-born statesman ; that he was
“ still true to Labour principles,” but possessed of
the “ true spirit of manly freedom,” &c., &c., &c.
The change of attitude was Prompt and Beautiful.
The chairman at the recent 12th July
Orange meeting at Sydney Town Hall announced
that his body intended to raise the standard of
the men representing the people in Parliament,
and among the means to be adopted was the
raising of the sectarian cry at the Federal elec-
tions ! With such examples as Wilks and Jessep,
M’s.P., before him the Grand Master can be well
excused for stating that local politics are on a low
level. The rottenest era in N.S.W. political his-
tory was when Parkes and his vulgar, unlettered,
sectarian puppets ruled the land. Those were
indeed the halcyon days of Orangeism—the days
when at a 12th of July shivoo you saw on the
platform perhaps 30 of the gang all a-row, and
more than proud to exhibit themselves. Now,
you can only find three M’s. P.—people like
Jessep, Wilks, and Law—game to parade their
old-world rancour, and try once more to rend
asunder a peaceful community. Even Brother
Perry, the Minister for Education, finds it i >n-
venient to stay at the meeting or show himself on
the platform—he has important business in the
Assembly.
Brisbane Govt, organ on Chinese troubles:
"The Boxe s consist of the scum of the people, and have
adopted as cheir motto —“ China for the Chinese.”
The audacity of the Chow ! Perhaps, however,
he has heard from his brethren in Australia that
some folks in this country are also possessed of a
motto built on similar lines.
Queensland, the first of the Australias to
offer armed help to England to crush the Boers,
was the last of the Australias to agree with the
Imperial idea of managing the Australian
Squadron.
The stupendous Malmesbury (Vic.) reser-
voir, which supplies Taradale, Fryers, Maldon,
Huntly, Sebastian, Chewton, Bendigo, Castle-
maine, Eaglehawk and Haywood, did a rather
serious burst lately ; and by the time it is repaired
all the above towns may be going about with their
tongues hanging out and be compelled to wash
their faces with lemonade. The official in charge
thinks the burst may be due to the fact that no
repairs have been done for at least 14 years, and
it is suspected that certain ironwork meanly and
surreptitiously rusted itself through when the
Department wasn’t looking.

As officially returned, the British expendi-
ture over the S’African war to end of March totals I
£23,250,000 —quite a million a week. Since then 1
the British taxpayer has had to find money to buy
and ship to S’Africa 40,000 horses to replace those
expended by Gen. French during the 100 miles
march from Orange River to Bloemfontein.
These horses cannot cost less than £4O a. head
landed in S’Africa, and that means a trifle of
£1,600,000 ! French has, so far, destroyed,
say, £4,000,000 worth of horses.
How history repeats itself ! Take a few
extracts from the story of the Highland Jacobite
rising of 1745. . The invaders excited the greatest
terror in tlie hearts of the English people because
of the horrible stories of their barbarism industri-
ously spread throughout the country. “ Every-
body said the Highlanders ate little children.”
We find this in the Chevalier Johnstone’s
memoirs:
They affirmed in the newspapers of London that we had
dogs in our army trained to fight, and that we were in-
debted for our victory at Gladsmuir to these dogs, who
darted with fury on the English army. They represented
the Highlanders as monsters, with claws instead of hands.
In a word, they never ceased to circulate, every day, the
most extravagant and ridiculous stories with respect to
the Highlanders. The English soldiers had indeed reason
to look upon us as extraordinary men, from the manner
in which we had beaten them with such inferior numbers,
and they probably told these idle stories to the country
people by way of palliating their own disgrace.
The religion of the Highlanders was a subject of
endless scoffing, too, and tales of their disgusting
hypocrisy in kneeling and praying “like Chris-
tians ” were recited from one end of England to
the other.
Concerning George Reid as a barrister :
Much has been written and said of ex-Premier G. H.
Reid as a politician and even as a “ practising ” states-
man. Let us look at him as he appears at the Bar, earn-
ing his bread and holding his briefs with no small measure
of success.
Calumnious critics, in the years of Reid’s Premiership,
were wont to taunt him with having failed in his profes-
sion. Within two terms he has effectually silenced them,
and the generous-minded will congratulate him.
Reid in his wig, gown and eye-glass is a comedy to
begin with, and when he addresses the Court, or cross-
examines a witness, he is funnier still. Also, he manages,
every time, to have the laugh with him.
Reid’s return to the bar created much interest. His
most resolute parliamentary opponent, Attorney Crick,
arranged his first brief. The case was a capital one, and
Reid managed to get a verdict of manslaughter. . Since
then he has been in much demand both in the criminal
and civil courts. As an advocate Reid’s methods are
entirely original. As a rule barristers, from the latest-
joined junior to the most seasoned Q. 0., employ exactly
the same terms, put points in the same way, are as con-
cise as a geometrician and as fixed in their legal course, as
stars in the firmament. Of course, they display varying
degrees of ability and brilliancy—of acumen and aptitude.
But they are followers of a fashion professionally regarded
as immutable.
Reid, on the other hand, is legally, intellectually, and
constitutionally unconventional. While he evinces a
knowledge of the letter of the law and a clear conscious-
ness of its scope and restrictions, he is deliciously un-
trammelled by barristerial tradition. He amazes the
habituated Bench, startles the strictly formal Bar, a,nd
strongly interests those having a daily acquaintance with
court-procedure. But his knowledge of human nature,
his appreciation of the niceties of his cause, and his grasp
of detail serve his purpose with surprising effect when he
puts the issue to the jury. His methods may petrify the
Bench, inconvenience rival counsel, and outrage all
tradition ; but it is little George cares as long as he gets
his points home with the jury, and frees, their, minds of
any obscurity or doubt. If the boom in Reid's briefs
continue, the ex-Premier threatens to revolutionise the
musty advocacy of the N.S.W. Courts.
Legislative sanction is sought for the con-
struction of a “mono-rail express-line” between
Liverpool and Manchester (Eng.) The single rail
is to be placed on steel trestles 4ft. high with guide
rails lower down to steady the cars. It is proposed
to run at 100 miles-per-hour speed with a 10-minute
service. With rail-speed like this and turbine-
driven steamers which can be constructed to do up
to 60 miles per hour, it looks as if reality were
shortly going to out-do even Jules Verne’s imagin-
ative flights in this direction.
