SPORTING NOTIONS.
The V.R.C. handicapper scored a record this
ame m the Grand Nationals. Hurdle and steeple were
each captured by aged “duffers” carrying the 9st. mini-
mum, inn the two runners-up humped only IBst. lolb.
DMween them. Last year Valdis and Mysore won the
double ' under burdens that averaged 9st. go by
these tokens handicapper Dakin is beginning to “ err on
the side of leniency ” (as the newspapers say) in his treat-
ment or horses entered for big jumping races with no
form ro credit. Or he errs on the side of severity as
regards those that run often and win occasionally. The
fault is very evident in the history of the G.N. Hurdle
ltaee, anyhow. Of the 20 winners listed from 'Si to 1900,
only nine have carried more than lOst.; ten were favored
with something less than lOst.; and the other one started
from the lOst. mark. Owners of recognised good per-
formers over the little sticks get little encouragement to
enter them for the swell hurdle race of the year.
* * * *
The V.R.C. Grand National Steeple pro-
duced as big a surprise as G.N. Hurdles a week before.
H inner—Rosebud—carried little or no money—hundreds
to 8 going begging. Time (6.39 J) is a record. As
already remarked, successful nags in the big“lepping”
race don’t come .from lower-weighted ones as a rule.
Fearless 11. (9.9), Daimio (9.3), and Mysore (9) carried
the lightest burdens home previous to Saturday,
when Rosebud humped 9st. Event was instituted
ln ar *d since_ then only two mares have won
Ruby (12.7 up) in ’BB and Rosebud. Race, as such,
proved a big disappointment, for Butler, Chelsea (an
amateur in the saddle), Leeway, 8.8., Euro, Pirate and
Crusado came down, and the hot-pot Mysore ran off—
six . ou ? of 16 Btar ters to fight out the finish.
Worth mentioning that three previous years’ winners
Hayseed, Floater and Mysore—were among the “ field,”
and former ran third. Daimio credited himself with two
successive victories in ’94 and ’95. No other nag yet
scored twice.
• • • •
When Rosebud won the Steeplechase, two
lengths in front of Wallaby and eight lengths ahead of
weary Hayseed, there came a long, ghastly silence, broken
only by the fitful, gladsome blasphemies of the “ books.”
And then the assembled thousands of punters had a
sudden inspiration. It came to them all at once, or
nearly so, and on every part of the course the question
was put by everybody to his nearest neighbor, “ What
would Rosebud have paid in the Tote? ” Ikey Mo’s price
about Rosebud was a modest 33, or in some cases 50 to
I—the same old orthodox “ h’any price” that he had
offered against Aquarius for the Hurdle Race. But,
probably, Rosebud was a more general “ skinner ” for the
Israelites than even Aquarius had been. Tub Bulletin
knows of at least one leading metallician who actually
laid a £lOO bet against Aquarius on the course, and was so
wroth at having to pay away anything at all that he in-
sulted the lucky punter. If the same bookmaker failed to
scoop the whole pool over Rosebud his anger was probably
awful. Mysore, of thedoubtful temper, didn’t mean to try to
win the G.N. Steeple, as it turned out. The favourite was
decidedly loth to take the logs and the stone wall in front
of the Stand, still his jockey managed to keep him up to
his work for about 2j miles. In fact, he was going fourth
with an excellent chance of victory when Crusado fell at
the fence in front of him, and he—ran off. Which was
just what Mysore had been expected to do at Caulfield
where, however, he ran kindly enough. He is a good
natural jumper, like all the Wellingtons, but being
troubled by the remembrance of an unlucky fall which
happened him last year, he runs off quite as naturally as
he jumps. This is more creditable to Mysore’s head than
to his heart. Also it is distressing for his backers.
• * • *
Aftermath of Aquarius’ win in G.N. Hurdles is
still d Appears that both he and second nag—
Rackstraw—were lame the day before, and latter could
only take swimming-exercise. Rackstraw went so
gingerly on race-morning that it looked even then as if he
might not line up. Aquarius’ people admit that the
gelding wouldn’t have been sent had he not been paid-up
for—they thought they might as well have a run for their
money. Said that a blacksmith at Mordialloc (Vic.)could
have had Aquarius for nothing. Nag had broken down
several times, and on one of these occasions was so bad
that owner Connelly got full and tilled, and abortively
asked Vulcan to take him as a gift.
• * * •
What an unsatisfactory game it is ! Chit-Chat,
the Ballarat horse from Scobie’s stable, started in three
flat-races at the V.R.C. meeting. In the first two goes
Chit-Chat got a good run and an easy win each time, but
in the third something brought the flier down, and he re-
turned to the birdcage with blood on his head. Signifi-
cant fact that the “ books ” were always ready to lay 10
to 1 against Chit-Chat for the race wherein something
brought him down.
* * * *
The backers of coincidence should have flattened
out Ikey Mo badly over the G. N. Hurdles. One Wagga
man landed a wager over Aquarius, which he backed
because his town was mostly under water, and the people
were roosting on the fences waiting for it to go down.
Best handicapping seen about Sydney for many
a day was in the big event at Warwick Farm on Satur-
day, which fell to the rankest outsider of the race—
Turbine. Four finished almost in a line ; other three
were Ambition, Coal Queen, and Wat Tyler. Owing to
similarity of colors, judge had Promise’s number hoisted.
Mo., fearing that the “skinner” was slipping from his
grasp, started howling as he only can howl for the
mistake to be rectified. His hig retinue of “ guns,”
“ whisperers,” “ lug-biters,” &c., &c., joined in, and
things were very hot until the change was made.
* * * *
Extract from letter from Lieut.-Col. Kelly,
Commanding Officer Vic Contingent, A.I.R. ; “Transport
Victorian, Off Beira, 21 5/’OO. We are all (horses and
men) in first-class trim ; but we are fretting very much
over the one horse we lost. He was dying at Langwarrin,
and was condemned, but in some unaccountable way
smuggled himself up. to. Flemington, where he was con-
demned again. Then he turned up on Port Melbourne
Pier, where he was condemned again ; but, when we got
out to sea and counted our horses, we found we had one
over, and, hang me !if it was not this very brute. We
nursed him and pampered him for our reputation’s sake ;
but, notwithstanding all, the brute died—l believe from
pure spite.”
* * * *
The fight between Bill Doherty and dark-hued,
lathy Peter Felix for Australia’s championship filled all
available space in the huge Metrop. A.O. Hall, Ultimo
(Sydney), Monday night. About 1500 turned up and paid
£l, 10s., ss. and 2s. 6d. to see the show, which was lively,
interesting, and at times very exciting from first to last
of 20 rounds. Felix (13st.) had 6in. advantage in height,
goodness knows how many in reach (for his length of limb
is abnormal), and a stone the better of weights ; but his
defence was bad, and Doherty got in on him time after
time when he had no right to. Odds of 6to 4 were laid
on the white, and they were justified, for never at any
period was he in serious trouble—albeit, he often bothered
the other map.' First ten rounds saw things fairly even,
the black,, perhaps, holding a slight lead. Felix lost his
head frequently in critical situations. Once he had Doherty
going if he had only known how to make the most of the
opportunity. Cognoscenti were satisfied that Doherty
was a good and dangerous fighter after the second 3min.
The fourth round was redhot throughout, and onlookers
were crazed with excitement. Doherty had the eighth all
to himself, and 2 to 1 was freely offered on the “ little
’un ” as he sat down for the much-needed minute’s spell.
After the tenth, Doherty was in front throughout, treat-
ing Felix’s lefts with contempt, and getting in on him
every time he (Felix) flung his right, which he did often—
foolishly (as it left him very open) and ineffectually, be-
cause it,wasn’t much good. The only knock-down of the
battle occurred in the 17th, when Doherty got home hard
and true on the point, dropping the sable giant like a
cock. Felix stayed down ldsec. and came up dazed.
Doherty might have won then but for the gong’s sound.
Subsequently there was nothing much done. The black
weakened a bit, and didn’t display the same zest. Referee
McDonald declared in favor of Doherty, and the decision
wa» applauded. The feature of the fight was the wonder-
fully good condition both* were in. Mick Dooley chal-
lenged the winner, and they’ll probably be matched this
week, as Doherty is agreeable.
* • * •
“Old Timer” : Saw the Felix-Doherty affair on
Monday night and was interested. Doherty fought a
fighting-man’s fight from end to end—cool, gritty, and
aggressive. Felix was the gymnasium boxer as far as
effect went, though not in point of skill, for he has a lot
to learn. His legs are wider apart, bringing his 6ft. 3in.
of stature down to perhaps only two or three inches more
than Doherty’s sft. 9in. He kept using his right foolishly
and aimlessly, thereby giving Doherty opening after
opening. Never saw a man lead so much with the right
as Felix did, and then it was only a chop—something
after Paddy Slavin’s style, but not carrying anything like
the same amount of power. If he had been
content with sending in his left only the result might
have been different, for Doherty couldn’t have scored
so often. There wasn’t much damage done. Felix’s face
was puffed up a lot, while an old cut under Doherty’s left
eye bled freely, as also did his left ear. Doherty shaped
a heap better than most people expected, but the black’s
showing was disappointing. He hasn’t improved much
—if any—since the “ Slogger of the Seas” (Tom Ball—a
foul worse-than-average fighter) made him look so silly in
Sydney years ago. Peter Jackson looked on and com-
mended the decision, which he said was beyond cavil.
Doherty deserves the honor, for he is a fighter and a man
—every inch. Though Dooley beat Doherty in Westralia
a few months ago, he’ll have his work cut out next time
if Doherty is as well as on Monday night.
* * * *
Struggle between heavyweight butcher Tim
Nolan and that fat, slow back-number, Bill Jennings,
drew a big crowd to Metrop. A.O. (Sydney) last week, but
as a fight 'twas a farce, though genuine enough. The
methods evidenced were very crude and suggestive of the
way pugs, fought before Fig’s time. Both would get up
against each other, with faces smothered by arms, and
push and shove for all the world like a pair of old billy-
goats butting; if one slipped t’other punched. They
would come from their chairs for every round—left
shoulder up and chin buried behind it in mortal dread of
stopping one with the “point,” and indulge in more
charging and propping, while the crowd roared again.
The only feature of a goat-fight absent was the sound of
the clashing horns. It was undoubtedly the most primi-
tive-looking affair lately seen in Sydney, and lasted till the
end of the eighth round, when Jennings capitulated,
asserting that his left hand had “gone again.” His
mauleys have been very crooked for years. Jennings
could fight once, but can’t now. Nolan is simply a bul-
lock and nothing else. There’s not the faintest tinge of
intelligence about anything he does beyond the effort to
keep his chin protected. It was nothing but tearing and
forcing from end to end, and left one marvelling how
such men could possibly be designated boxers.
• * « *
“Cocker” Tweedie bad a soft snap at Golden
Gate A.O. (Sydney) Saturday night, when he faced
Meares—another “ Rocks ” production, but a very “ sick ”
exponent of the game. Meares caught one punch on the
point half-a-minute after shaping up, looked for a soft
spot, fell, and lay there amid a pandemonium of hoots
and jeers. Referee courited-out the prostrate man, de-
clared the thing “ no fight,” and gave Tweedie a fiver
as compensation, thus punishing “Cocker” for the
management’s mistake m making such an absurd
match. Meares never was a fighter, and, physically, he’s
unfit to undergo any severe ordeal.
* * » •»
“Gore”: “Old Timer” (B. 7/7/’00) is hardly
fair to “ Snowy ” Sturgeon, whom he seems to class as
wanting in gameness for sprinting away from his opponent
in the ring. Well, doesn’t the fact that Sturgeon was
able to keep away from such a “rusher” as Cribb show
that the former is as good on the defensive as the latter is
on the aggressive ? It takes an artist of the first water to
successfully elude another in the ring and save himself
from going over the ropes during over half-a-dozen
rounds. Sturgeon showed marked ability in eluding
Cribb ; and Cribb showed that he was not so good on the
aggressive as many think him. [“Old Timer” said
nothing about want of gameness. He merely remarked
that Sturgeon’s heart never appeared to be quite in it—
and, as for Cribb being “ not so good on the aggressive,”
the man hadn’t entered for a foot-race !]
• * * *
How Australian pug.-material has deteriorated
since Foley and Jackson left off showing ’em how to do
it is evidenced by recent happenings in ’Frisco 1 Un-
disputed middleweight premier of these parts, Tim
Murphy, was handily licked by Al, Neill (whose reputa-
tion was purely local) a few months back. Now latest
mail tells that last month Neill went under to Moffatt,
a third-rater from the Eastern States, who was thought
so little of that nobody came along to back or “ manage ”
him.
* » * •
Dear Bulletin.—Present writer, during his
ring-side experience, has seen a great number of “knocks-
out,” and is certain that 50 per cent, of them were abso-
lute flukes ; many of the others also being open to much
doubt. Extreme nicety is required to get home a real
“outer” on a man dodging all over a 24-foot ring ; and,
anyhow, the knock-out is not boxing—it is a premedi-
tated attempt to kill! “ boxing” contest nowadays
presents mostly the pitiful spectacle of a pair of heroes
hustling and shuffling and smothering around after the
chance of a “knock-out.” Foot-work, head-work, and
“science” generally are “not in it” to-day. Stand stiff
as stone and hump your shoulder up to cover the “ mark”
seems to be the golden rule of the ring nowadays. Result
—either a long-drawn and wearisome worry to a draw,
or one man flatten ed-out more by accident than other-
wise. People who do not seek murder or sudden death
are getting tired of these one-hit exhibitions.— Theo. B.

Cabled last Thursday- that Australian premier
sprinter Stan Rowley annexed a 100yds. scratch race at
Stourbridge (Eng.) in lOsec. This is about up to Rowley’s
best form here and equal to British amateur record-
shared in by Wharton,' Bradley (twice), Downer and
Cooper. World’s best (9 4-5) belongs to American
amateurs Owen Wefers and Duffy, and J. H. Hempton
(M.L.) and W. T. Macpherson (N.S.W.).
X * X *
Dear Bulletin, r—Let me point out re the
N.S.W. Public Schools’ Annual Sports Meeting(l9th Sept,
next) that masters and teachers of each school which has
pupils competing should ein attendance to see that the
boys get a chance for the events they are entered for.
The advertised programme should be followed instead, as
was the case last year, of half-a-dozen events being com-
peted for at the same time, to the shutting out of boys
entered for more than one of them. Also, for obvious
reasons, no events should be judged by masters of schools.
And the running in the walking contests—which was last
year so freely permitted—should be strictlv dealt with.
In this year’s 440yds. Age Championship Walk 13-year-
old boys have 15yds. start, 12-year-olds 30yds., and 11
years and under also 30. Surely if a 13-year-old has to
give a 12-year-old 15yds., the 12-year-old" should give at
least 45 to the 11-and-under. The throw at the wicket
also (advertised this year, as it was last, as from cover-
point) will not, it is to be hoped, be again contested on
the full throw from wicket to wicket. Why advertise
conditions and then ignore them ? This annual school-
gathering is a marvellous one, and it is a pity that its suc-
cess should be marred by such defective administration.
—II. Stockdale.
* * * *
M.L, has a strong man “Samson,” who pro-
mises well. He is only 21, stands sft. 4in., and scales
lOst. 71b. So poor a showman is he that the weights of
his several lifts are not exaggerated an ounce. After four
months’ practice he joined Dix’s Variety Co., and is with
them now. The M.L. man puts up 1451 b. with one hand,
and 2271 b. with both, also holds out Jcwt. with an arm at
right angles from body. Youth and consequent imma-
turity of development considered, his measurements are
eye-opening : Biceps 15in., forearm 12in., and shoulder
18in. T’other night Wellington man of brawn and thew
Doyle Ftested “ Samson’s ” weights, and voiced this :
“ My experience of strong men is that they are the
greatest frauds in the show business. This man evidently
doesn’t know enough to be a fraud. I consider him a
prodigy of strength.”

The Hordern Premiership Cricket Shield, won
last year by Central Cumberland C.C., was handed over
to that club recently. During the evening it was men-
tioned that the premier club will next season be aug-
mented by many notables, including Iredale (of Waver-
ley), Coningham (who is running a duck farm in the dis-
trict), and other well-known players.
• * \ * *
Joe Darling is at present in Tasmania, which
province will almost certainly hold him for good. He
has practically bought a station, and S.A. cricket will see
him no more. Whether he will play for Australia again
is a question probably of form at the time. Joe has
embarked on station life again at the earnest solicitation
of ex-miller John Darling (his father), who is living in
retirement at E. Melbourne, and is a very wealthy man.
* * * *
Bowler M’Kibbin has secured a billet with an
Adelaide timber-firm, and will probably play with Pt.
Adelaide club next summer.
• * • •
Albert Trott has only been a moderate success
with the ball f&r Middlesex this season and has been al-
most an absolute failure with the bat. His best bowling
so far is 9 for 190 against Somerset in the first week in
June.

“ Coo-ee ”: A member of present N.S.W. Govt,
was once patron of Tambong (N.S.W.) C.C., and promised
a guinea bat for season’s highest average score, but the
prize never materialised. This occurred three years ago.
Winner has long since given up all hope of ever treating
the natives of Tambong to a day’s leather-chasing with
that bat; but politicians have a weakness for this sort of
thing. Remember one possessing a Hibernian-flavored
French accent promising a 20-guinea prize, to be won
three times by swimmers. Clubs he it to were so
impressed with his liberality that they banqueted him.
The 20gs. were won, but they never came, though said
politician’s memory—like that of present Minister—was
jogged often.
* * * •
Interprovincial football struggle between Q. and
N.S.W. on Sydney Cricket Ground Saturday attracted
15,000 people. There was nothing fetching about the
match insofar as fine points are concerned, but it was
lively enough. But many ordinary club contests have
more to recommend them. Forwards occasionally shone
when spread out, but combination was totally absent on
both sides; in this respect Q’landers —though playing
and practising together for weeks—were worse offenders
than mother province’s reps., picked but three days
previously. Prior reports were to the effect that the
Bananalanders formed one of the best sides produced by
their province—despite the factof the forwards being some-
what light, and, as the constitution of the N.S.W. team
met with general approval, something extra was looked
for. But it didn’t materialise. Game ended 11 to 10 in
favor of N.S.W. The visitors with just a little luck in the
last few minutes might have won. There was too much
“gallery play” by individuals who frequently made—
or tried to make—themselves prominent on occasions
when combined work might have effected a big difference.
■*■***
Maoriland football is in full swing. At
Waitekauri v. Auckland match five of the Paeroa players
were injured. Leg broken—smashed scalp—3 ribs broken
—broken nose ; the fifth man was rendered unconscious
for four hours.
* • • •
N.S.W. Champion of Champions bowling
tourney—wherein only champions of different affiliated
clubs competed—ended Saturday in a boil-over. Smith
(Strathfield) and Bradbury (Vic. Park) were the two left
in. Bradbury had the “ soft things ”of the competition,
while Smith accounted for what seemed the toughest
tasks—Howe (Redfern) and Reeder (Balmain). Both
opened and kept on playing below average form for some
time. Each seemed nervously anxious. Bradbury often
played on the back hand when the opposite was the
proper game, and once he “drove” without the slightest
prospect of benefit accruing. Result was a miss, and
Smith scored with every bowl—4. Scores were now 12—
11 in favoj; of Bradbury. Smith never notched another
point, and Bradbury won with 10 to spare. Outcome of
this match illustrates the luck of the game. Dozens of
bowlers would readily play winner, but comparatively
few would care about tackling Smith.

Melb. C.O. is promoting a bowling tourney for
the individual premiership of the provinces and expects
200 devotees to forward their half-sovs. The £lOO thus
subscribed will be cut up into £5O (first), £2O (second),
£7 (third) and £3 (fourth) prizes. Nothing is said as to
what will be done with the other £2O, though ’tis stated
that if less than 128 entries come to hand a pro-rata
reduction will be made in second, third and fourth prizes.
M. is wealthy enough to have done the thing properly
(if it wanted to do it at all) and chanced the number of
competitors.
* * • *
Latest from Australian Fred. Lane doesn’t in-
dicate much likelihood of great deeds (so far as he is con-
cerned) in Eng. during present swimming season. Effects
of influenza are still with him. Doctor examined his
chest and lungs and said they were perfectly sound.
Necessity for seeking informatio n in this connection
points to the possibility of the little top-notcher’s health
not being as heretofore, especially when same communi-
cation tells that it is not Lane’s intention to do much
swimming this year, that he has lost all enthusiasm for
the sport, and when the summer is over will return to
Sydney. Lane’s physique never did strike writeras being
that of a champion, though he did some very big things.
AtlastTowns and Wray have come to terms about
a match for the English championship. They’ve agreed
to get to it next month on the Thames for £2OO a-side,
and sports in the old country are agog re the event, as it
is over 12 months now since Towns annexed the title from
Barry. Towns’ objection to the Tyne was the immense
amount of traffic there. “ Might as well row from London
Bridge to Chelsea,” said Tom Sullivan—Towns’ close pal.
“ I know something about it. I rowed Harding there
five years ago, and our race had to be postponed day after
day for a week.” Tom thinks it’s a good thing for the
N. man—Towns.

Yankee mail last week brought news of sculler
Ned Hanlan’s resurrection. What memories of far-back
days and rowing happenings the name conjures up ! Ned
has been in Boston some time coaching college-crews and
has made such a good impression that moneyed men have
offered to back him against champion Jake Gaudaur for
5000dol. a-side. Said that the old-timer is getting into
such prime trim that he is sure to give the wily Canuck a
great shaking-up, and if Jake won’t come, then Ned’s
going after some of the other big guns. Newpaper reports
are that Hanlan will get very close to rowing in his old-
time form. Difference in age won’t cut much of a fi<mre
in the result, for Gaudaur is no chicken.

S.A. and Victoria meet at lacrosse on Melb.
C.C. ground this Saturday (21st), and probably the best
game yet played in Australia will result. S.A. team is
the strongest available if Dawson, its champion attack
gets over. Included in Vic. team are Drew, the erstwhile
S.A. champion centre, and Bounin, another one-time S.A.
crack. The S.A. team is the fastest yet turned out bv
that province, and, unless Vic. lacrosse has greatly
advanced,.should win.
Phin Thompson —elder brother of bookmaking
Joe, and Jack, and Barney—who died in his sleep some
nights ago, was at one time a brevet-colonel in the U.S.
army. He was a white man and a humorist. On one
occasion he started a subscription-list to obtain funds for
the relief of “ a dear old friend ” of his, who was seriously
ill and unable to attend to his business. Phin was
popular, and everybody he applied to responded gene-
rously. But he would not take the money immediately.
“ I am only the secretary to the fund,” he explained.
“ I’ll put your names down, and you can pay your sub-
scriptions to the treasurer when he conies along at the
end of the week.” In a few davs Phin’s list was yards
long. At the end of the week some subscribers called to
pay over. “I am glad to inform you, gentlemen, that, I
see by the cablegrams, my friend —my dear old friend,”
said Phin, wiping a joyful tear from his eye, “ has almost
recovered from his illness, and will soon be able to resume
the duties attached to his high position. Your generous
offers to assist him in his time of trouble need not, there-
fore, be carried any further.” Then it turned out that
Phin’s “ dear old friend ” was the late Czar of Russia, who
had been about that time attacked by one of the fits of
illness which presaged his end. The father of Phin and
the rest of the Thompsons is, by the way, still alive, and
nearing 90 years of age.

Dear Bulletin. Wal.” (B. 30/6/’00) errs in
saying that the “ Tom Morriser” style of top is not now-
obtainable, and that the art of making such tops “ must
have died with the ancients.” Herewith I send you a
couple of such tops, and further point out that at the
time mentioned by “ Wal.” there was in Liverpool-street,
Sydney, a turner named Steel, whose tops were quite as
famous as the Morris, and used to fetch from Is. 6d. to
2s. The accompanying tops were made by a son of this
Steel. It’s the cheap made-in-Germany article that has
knocked these tops out. —A.G.S.
m m m *
Hundreds of attempts have been made to pro-
duce and market “ puncture-proof ” tyres, the idea pre-
vailing that the perfect tyre is one which is impervious to
puncture. It is impossible, however, to produce a tyre
that is proof against puncture without sacrificing the
resiliency or spring, which is the all-important feature of
a pneumatic tyre, and, in fact, the present perfected
Dunlop Tyre so seldom punctures that cyclists only waste
time in seeking for patent puncture-proof device.*
m * * •
Smiler Hales —now picturesquely romancing in
London Daily Mail about things South African — takes a
lively interest in boxing (which, by the way, he seriously-
injured in Sydney by the luridity of his reports), and
when he was in a bad temper would ask a man in training
to “ have ’em on,” and would beseech him to “ hit me—
dam it, hit me hard ! ” He thus once besought that
brilliant amateur, Dick Sandall, at Sydney Am. Gymnas-
tic Club, and the result was that Hales came staggering
into the Referee office so much dismayed in his
“ thinker ” that he believed he was in Broken Hill, and ex-
plained that he had been among the miners, and that
they had stoned him with slag. Then somebody, who
had been at the club when “Smiler” and Dick were
boxing, came along and explained that lOst. 31b. Sandall
(who couldn’t box light, anyhow) had landed Hales a
couple of his awful rights on the “point.” “Smiler”
wasn’t properly himself for a week after. Another time
he asked George Dawson to “ hit me hard,” and “ Gentle-
man George ” promptly banged him on the solar plexus,
and pretty nearly killed him. These two painful experi-
ences of the difference between the artist and the dile-
tante cured Hales of going for “keeps” with crack
boxers.

The carelessness of certain steam-users in not
looking after their boilers and the results of neglecting to
clean them regularly when incrustation takes place has
often been pointed out in Tub Bulletin. A disencrustat-
ing boiler compound known as “ Litofuge, with a world-
wide reputation, is being placed upon the market by vy.
and J. Lempriere, 506 Little Collins-st., Melbourne, who
upon application will be pleased to forward catalogues to
those interested. “Litofuge" compounds are powders
without soda, fatty matter or injurious chemicals, which
injure rather than improve boilers. Its action is mechani-
cal and its introduction into a boiler causes no foaming.

The directors of Mt. Welcome mine, Mitta
Mitta, Vic., who are also directors of the Oassihs Go. at
Glen Wills, believe they have got hold of a similar mining
proposition. With a knowledge of the Oassihs mine they
have determined to erect a complete gold-recovering
plant. A 20-head battery has been bought, a new and
up-to-date cyanide plant will be established, and a small
chlorination plant will also be installed. A Tangye pump
will draw enough water from the Mitta Mitta all the year
round to amply supply all the various processes. The ore
can be carried from hoppers at the tunnel mouth direct
to the battery, and the delivery will be automatic.

New Moon (Bendigo) : Last half-year—l4,4so
tons, 48860 z. ; average, 6dwts. 18grs., enabling divs. to
the amount of £9600, or Bs. per share, equal to about 60
per cent, of the value of gold produced. This represents
a shrinkage of 18grs. per ton on previous half-year.
Grand total to date : 213,012 tons, 86,8900z5. ; average a
little over Bdwts. Divs., £104,128. Amount called up,
£11,560. Upwards of £20,000 spent on machinery-all
came from gold. Statement of receipts and expenditure
an object-lesson to directors extravagantly managing
in London mines thousands of miles away. Cost of
management, including directors fees, £149 16s. mere
is a pathetic side to the picture : ‘The development
work of the mine has been well attended to, but, so tar,
the lower levels have not shown any of those rich runs we
have had, and still have, in the shallow workings But as
these lower workings are having the advantage of a good
method of working from the start, and there being a little
gold distributed throughout these workings, we have
every reason to expect some of them to develop payable
quartz before the present richer veins are exhausted.
Writer thinks very little of the hope.

A party of confiding miners are at piesent
sinking for gold at Clifton Springs, an incipient township
on the western shore of Port Philip Bay, \ ictoria about
the last place to look for gold. They are thus expending
their labor and money on the word of a spiritualistic
medium. Some years ago another seer induced a syndi-
cate to prospect for coal at the Red Bluff, just across the
Bay. They raised several tons of red clay and sand, but
nai-y a black diamond. The gold-seekers have, so far, got
down 140 ft. and have got several promising specimens of
granite, also a few of good road-metal. Spirits know
nothing about real mining.
Bendigo, Victoria’s chief reefing field, proposes
to celebrate the jubilee of the discovery of gold in the
province by holding an industrial exhibition in that city
in 1901-, Gold to the value of £68,000,000 has been won
from the Bendigo fields.
Otago dredging boom has opened up a new field
in answer to the cry of “what to do with our boys. A
voung dredge-master has gone to Canada to run a dredge
there, at £7 a week, passage paid—half-salary during the
voyage. Two others have been engaged for Vancouver on
similar terms. A high premium is now charged to those
wishing to be taught dredging work. #
Maoriland dredging has had a big slump.
Causes, over-flotation of ce.’s and difficulty of getting
dredges built. Although contract* have be*n let for
building all over M.L. and Australia, demand is at least
two years ahead of supply ; the plague *care and conse-
quent erratic Australian steam service have accentuated
the trouble by keeping many dredges without portions of
machinery, the contracts for which were let in Australia
12 months ago. Many really first-class claims floated last
vear, and which saw big premiums, are now at a dis-
count, owing toscrippers being “weak” and unable to
hold on. Meanwhile, the dredges working con-
tinue to get good results. Hartley and Riley
averages almost lOOoz. weekly—Vincent runs from 90 to
160oz.—Junction Electric, to say nothing of that pheno-
menal 12340 z., has had returns varying from 350 to 150 oz.
weekly, whilst the lesser lights get handsome divs.
Heaven keep Britain from ruling the waves of the Moly-
neux 1

Geologist Brown has returned from Tarcoola,
S.A.’s latest nor’-west goldfield, “ strongly impressed,”
and believing that the rich finds go down. Brown is in-
variably over-cautious, and his dictum will lead to the
new field being thoroughly tested. The Holder Govt, con-
templates the test of a battery—the best of all.
* • * *
The S.A. State gold mine at Mount Torrens,
which is worked in order to keep the Govt, battery and
cyanide plant going, is by no means a high-grade show ;
usually a trifle over lOdwt. is got in the battery, and 2Jdwt.
by cyanide. Yet it more than pays, though at times the
reef pinches exasperatingly.

Hannan’s Star—now working the Diehl process,
Capital £225,000—182,000 shares issued ; selling in London
a month ago at 275. 6d.—have been double the price.
What they are worth is an entirely different matter.
Reconstructed and made many calls in one form or
another. Never a div., and no hope of one. Magnificent
machinery, but a poor mine, as proved by its erushings,
the highest of which was 5910z5. for a month, the average
being about 3000z5., and for the last four months, none
have been reported. Next to the Btfulder Main Reefs,
and Chaffer’s, and there is nothing in that to recommend
it.

Northern Associated : The developments of
this mine continue good at the 400 ft. level. “ The stone
is literally covered with gold and teiluride, and good
judges give the lowest estimate as worth between 30 and
40oz. per ton. The face of the drive looks like a jewel-
ler’s shop. In consequence of the rich ore now being
worked in the mine a changing-house for employes is
being erected, and the rich ore is bagged as it comes to
the surface.” No doubt this is the gilded pill. Neverthe-
less, it has better developments than any other non-pro-
ducer on the field, and is controlled by a man who knows
his business.
••• 9 '
Golden Horseshoe—June production, 5552 tons
milled, 57210 z. ; 3286 tons cyanided, 22400 z. ; 2184 tons
slimes, 1058 oz.; from concentrates, 2990 z.; total gold out-
put, 93180 z. A magnificent return, somewhat lower than
last month’s; but if it were all profit it would not pay a
fair mining interest on the price of the shares, which
gives the mine a value of nearly £4,000,000. The shares
are known to be dangerous selling, and that alone
accounts for the present price. A great mine with greatly
inflated value.
• * * *
Lake View Consols June output — 3ooo tons
sulphides, 42740 z. ; 415 tons oxidised sands, 2170 z.; 2813
tons slimes, 9410 z. Total bullion at mine, 54050 z. ;
shipped ore, 108 tons, 2800 oz. ; total gold-value, £31,468.
Costs —expended on plant and machinery, £7030; on
mine development, £2633; on working costs, £13,016.
Total cost for month, £22,679. It is appalling to think
that this big yield only shows a profit of some £9OOO,
equal to £lOB,OOO a year. Of course the expenditure on
plant and machinery will be reduced, but it will never
cease altogether, and apart from this the return proves
the great cost of working mines at Kalgoorlie.
• * * •
The Westralian market. One paper: “The
market was extremely quiet to-day consequent on the
non-arrival of London quotations.” Another: “To-day
was the quietest experienced on ’Change for some time
past. In the absence of cable quotations from London
little inclination was shown to operate and business was
very limited.” A third : “ The share-market in Sydney
was very dull yesterday because no late prices were cabled
from London. Sydney and Adelaide markets are mere
reflections of London in Westralian ventures and weak
reflections at that.” At last their eyes are opened to the
palpable truth to which they have been so long- blind.
What a falling off in a fortnight! Lake Views were then
£l6, they are now just over £l3—a drop of nearly three-
quarters' of a million. Ivanhoes ten days ago touched
£l3; they are now down to £10 —a £600,000 drop—and
when they drop another £600,000 they will be quite up to
their value. Associateds, at 655., can stand
another drop of half-a-million and still be half-a-
million too high on what is now showing. Per-
severance show a drop of less than £l, and may be con-
sidered worth the money; Boulders, too, are very
steady. Associated Northern, 45s. —a small fall in the
face of splendid developments. Market absolutely dead
in Sydney, and very quiet throughout Australia, all on
account of cable interruption and London. When this
occurs again writer suggests that the members of the
Sydney Stock Exchange should employ their time in
playing marbles in Martin-place to keep up the excite-
ment of business. At time of writing buyers and
sellers were standing off one another, like a ship away
from the coast in a storm. In some instances there was
a difference of nearly £1 in their respective prices.
Ivanhoes —buyer, £lO 5s ; seller, £ll 2s 6d—and 200,000
shares in the mine. Rabbits !
* • * •
Expert v. expert is the latest phase of the W.A.
Associated management muddle. Expert Angove (ex-
perienced mine manager) states that it is unnecessary to
have an underground manager while employing a general
ditto, with three shift-bosses to supervise things under-
ground. Expert Frecheville opines that the first thing is
to secure a practical, active underground manager. And
both are right—when the mining knowledge or otherwise
of the general man is taken into consideration. With a,
practical man in charge, an underground man is not
necessary, as three shift-bosses are employed in any case.
But when, as it is so often in W.A., the gen. manager is a
metallurgist or something, with no experience of under-
ground work, it becomes a necessity to appoint one man
as head of that department. In Vic., practical miners are
put in charge of the mines, while one general manager
supervises the affairs of a dozen ; else they could never
haul 6dwt. dirt 3000 ft. and pay divs. out of it.
* * * *
Boulder Perseverance (chairman, Frank Gard-
ner). Last year’s output, 22,734 tons, yielding 45,2830 z.,
bv milling, smelting and cyaniding. Gross prone,
£105,131; average yield, exclusive of tailings, loz 12dwt.
per ton. Average value of tailings, 14dwt. Profits applied
to the erection of the new sulphide plant, the largest on
the field, with a capacity of 250 tons a day. Min ®
great shape, has more ore in sight above 500 ft. level than
an v other 24-acre block on Kalgoorlie. Oxidised ores almost
exhausted. Not much doubt about success of sulphide
plant, though writer has reason for believing that the cost
of treatment will be greater than has been estimated, and
that insufficient attention has been given to depreciation
of plant, which will be rapid and great. Undoubtedly a
great mine, well-managed above and below. It has five
lode formations, that known as the Lake View being the
largest, and, taking it all round, the best. Manager
Nicholls lately declared that he could maintain with the
new plant, for three years, a 7500-ton monthly output of
ore averaging 30dwt. Nicholls is a man who says very
little, and nothing that he does not mean. Apropos, some
rich oxidised-ore developments are reported from tne
Perseverance.

The Austral Otis Engineering Co. have just
completed a concentrating test on the Wilfley Table in-
stalled at their South Melbourne works of a parcel of
crushed ore representing the second product from the
concentrating mill at the Conrad Mine, owned by Mr. J.
Howell, which hitherto has all been stacked on the mine
waiting the advent of a machine that would profitably
treat it without recrushing. The result of the Wiltley
test is a high grade concentrate, and tailing waste show-
ing very little values, and the Otis Co. have despatched
the tables for erection on the mine.*

A new amalgamating-table is being used with
considerable success in W.A. Instead of being sluiced
over the mercury, the crushed ore is forced through it.
A much higher extraction is claimed for this method than
is obtainable per ordinary table. The fact that it can be
forced through—the specific gravity of mercury being so
much greater than that of stone andwater-iscluetotbe
construction of the table. An elaboration of tlm dea
which the inventor claims will amalgamate telluncle as
easily as free gold, is promised.
The cure of piles by the new “ Cannalin ”
treatment is fully described in the pamphlet,
“Piles and How to Cure Them,” by Albert
Sharp.’ Copies, with testimonials, sent free to any
colony from the depot, 257 George-street, Sydney.
The claim that in libel actions N.S.W.
juries shall have power to pronounce not only on
the damages but also on the costs is likely to
receive early attention in Parliament, as the
rough draft of a motion now circulating indicates.
This is not the first time the question has been
raised. N.S.W. records actually show a case
legally determined in accordance with that claim.
In the libel action Whitfield versus Caswell tried
before C.-J. Dowling in 1837, the jury returned
a verdict of “ one farthing damages and_ each
party to pay his own costs.” The Chief-Justice
demurred for a while to taking the latter pait. of
the verdict, but after argument agreed to certify
in terms which corresponded with the jury s
finding. Therry appeared for Caswell, and
Plunkett for Whitfield.
“ Bung ” to The Bulletin :
In Austin Chapman’s Hotels - Diminishing (N.S.W.)
Bill there is provision for the removal of a licence
from a district where there may be too many hotels to
another where one may be more urgently needed—such
as a newly-developed mining field, a new railway town-
ship, or the enlargement of an existing town or
village (see sections 3 and 4). It would be
well if this bill were passed before the Darling
Harbour and Rocks Resumptions took place. It is pro-
posed to allow a removal of licence to hotels within the
area resumed. According to the local option clauses of
N.S.W. Liquor Act, removals are limited to the ward.
Place all the licences now in the wards within the unre-
sumed portions, and competition, already intense, will
prove intolerable. Chapman’s Bill would admit of a wide
field for removal—the whole province instead of portion
of a ward. Should there be a city railway the same diffi-
culty must be met. By the way, this is really Copeland s
Bill,' but on quitting N.S.W. Assembly the present Agent-
General bequeathed it to Chapman.

Herald strives to make out that Cronje’s
father—ye ken I —was born at Auchencairn, and
bore the name of M‘Crone, and adds that
“ save for accidents of birthplace and upbringing
he (Gronje) is a pure Galloway man, and perfectly
qualified to figure as the hero of Mr. Crockett’s
next novel. . . . After all,” complacently con-
cludes the pawky screed, “ there is something im-
pressive in a Scotchman however disguised ; and
no doubt it was the subtle and magnetic influence
of his captive’s ancestry that made Lord Roberts
Eut on his gold-hilted sword for him and order
im a champagne lunch.”

Be certain proposed railways : ”
Dear Bulletin.—Good as a play to note certain N.S.W.
N. Western and Western papers’ persistent agitation for
railways calculated to benefit their own particular local-
ities. Some eight months ago, Wellington started the
bill rolling with a scheme to connect itself with Werris
Greek, and consequently with both the N. and N.W.
lines. Well, the Public Works Committee made a feeble
attempt at an “ inquiry,” and cheerfully trailed a herring
across the concern by suggesting a line from way-up the
AW. line to— Brewarrina! Fancy that! Wellington,
with the moral support of Armidale, Tamworth, Dubbo,
Orange, etc., still agitated, with result that, some months
later (in May) part of Works Committee held another
inquiry, both at Wellington and Dubbo. Now, at that
i lentical moment, some more of the Works Committee
were inquiring into the whooping of Curlewis (a platform
and about eight houses a dozen miles from Gunnedah)for
railway connection with (don’t faint) Gilgandra
(population 200). Said whooping was from a paltry
few w th petty coal-mining interests, and was joined in
the chorus by Gunnedah, which is big enough and old
enough to know better, but which also has private petty
e tal-mining interests to consider. Curlewis is a one-eyed
little hamlet which will never be any bigger, and the
awful Gilgandra is 50 miles from a railway or anywhere.
After considering the pros and cons for the past six
months, writer is of opinion that, if the Lynk Govt, is
serious about any of these lines, then one from Werris
Creek to Dubbo (or Wellington) will confer the greatest
and most lasting benefit on the greatest number of people ;
but as to whether it will pay or not he isn’t prepared to
give an opinion. In any case, the Govt, should come
down with all the weight of its feet on the wild Curlewis-
Gilgandra proposal.—H, Dunlop.

The recently-published autobiography of
the late 0. H. Pearson, ex-Victorian Minister of
Education, and ? able leader-writer for Melb. Age,
is the wail of a disappointed man. He complains,
among other things* that in early life he was
jockeyed out of the Chichele Professorship at
Oxford by Bishop Wilber force, and that m
Victoria he was promised the Agent-Generalship
Which was given to Murray Smith. Pearson was
a literary artist, and a speaker of culchah with
the Oxford drawl, but he was no more fitted for
Parliament than was John Stuart Mill. In no
sense a man of affairs, he wanted to he tlm intel-
lectual head of the Liberal party, and he became
(apart from his masterly newspaper articles) the
weakest joint in its tail. He never got oyer the
story told of his attempt at sheep-farming m
South Australia, just after he first shook the dust
of the old country from his feet. Having m a
paddock a lot of ewes which refused to breed, he
asked a waggish neighbor what was the matter.
“ Turn the wethers in” was the advice given and
promptly acted upon. J. B. Patterson got him
his fin t Assembly seat, and as this service produced
none of the gratitude which his sponsor looked for
the pair were enemies ever after. When Pearson
resigned his seat in the Assembly to make room
for Graham Berry on the latter s return from
England seven years ago, Premier Shiels offered
to make him Parliamentary Librarian increasing
the salary from £6OO to £lOOO a year; but Pearson
preferred to be secretary to the Agent-General
(Andrew Clarke), at £BOO believing that he
would eventually become Agent-General. And
he went to London, despite medical warning that
his lungs were unfit to bear the winter. The Pat-
terson Govt, tried to make use of its hold over
the Age protege to secure the support ot that
journal. Failing to obtain the much-needed
assistance, Patterson dismissed the unfortunate
professor, who died a fortnight afterwards. His
widow receives a small pension from the English
Civil List, in recognition of her late husbands
literary performances, which were high-class, but
pessimistic.

Newfoundland, with its iron, coal, silver, gold
and lead deposits, its great pine forests and its
well-watered agricultural lands, is certainly rather
different from the dry wildernesses of Westralia.
And if Newfoundland went down heavily under
the provincial system the chances are that Wes-
tralia will go down with a much worse smash.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page

Mr. A. F. STOCKS,
SURGICAL & MECHANICAL DENTIST,
492 OXFORD ST., PADDINGTON.
What the Telegraph says : “Mr. Mattick, a pastovalist of
Hargraves, possesses a valuable American ram, which has
found great difficulty in masticating his food, owing to
the loss of two front teeth. To get over this trouble, Mr.
Mattick called in the services of Mr. A. F. Stocks, a
dentist passing through Hargraves on his way to Hill End.
Mr. Stocks was highly successful in inserting' two artificial
teeth in place of the ones the ram had lost. No sooner
was the operation completed than the ram demonstrated
the success of the experiment by vigorously attacking
some fodder which was supplied. This is probably one of
the first, if not the first experiment of the kind in the
colony.”
A gentleman while going to Hill End on the mail-coach
asked a fellow-passenger, who was a parson, if any of the
sheep in his flock wanted a set of teeth.
What the Mudgeb Guardian says : “ Mr. A. F. Stocks.
This gentleman has established a large and extensive
connection during his visit to Mudgee, as surgical and
mechanical dentist.”
May be Consulted at 402 OXFORD BTREET,

in the above Company will be held on TUES-

EUSTACE BENNETT,
Manager.
Business: —
To receive Directors’ Report and Balance-sheet ;
to elect Two Directors in the room of H. B.
Cotton, Esq., and William Gallagher, Esq.,
whose term of office expires by effluxion of time
(both these gentlemen are eligible for re-elec-
tion, and offer themselves accordingly); to elect
two Auditors for the coming twelve months
(Messrs. J. B. C. Miles and J. H. Davies are
eligible, and offer themselves for re-election);
and to transact any other business that may be
brought before the meeting in accordance with
the Articles of Association.

“ Sfogato”: In B. 14/7/’OO Signor Della Robbia is
credited with comparing Lalla Miranda’s acting in
Rigoletto to that of Tietjens.. If there was much simi-
larity, Lalla must have been a funny Gilda, for the part is
that of a timid, shrinking young girl, whereas the
characters which Tietjens pourtrayed were all of heroic
mould, such as Norma, Lucrezia Borgia, Semiramide, &c ;
about the lightest role she ever essayed being that of
Margherita, in “ Faust.” Tietjens was a woman of com-
manding presence and immense tragic force, and had a
voice which for resonance has seldom, if ever, been
equalled. Della Robbia’s comparison is as if one should
say that an actress who had played, say Sweet Lavender
recalled Ristori in “ Medea."
A Southern paper alleges that an Australian songstress
(name not given), who went ’Ome to study under Mar-
chesi and electrify the Old World, is now drawing the
twopenny bitter beer in a Pimlico (London) bar. But so
many prospective Queens of Song from this end have dis-
appeared in London that it is difficult to identify the
beer-puller. She seems to have disappeared not much
worse than a good many others. Anyhow, she is evi-
dently meeting her fate with grit.
“ Musical ” Sydney is a cold corner for musicians. After
many years of good and conscientious work yet another
has “thrown up the sponge” and fled. The talented
Aug. W. Juncker sailed last Saturday for London. For
many years he was conductor of the Dunning Opera Co.
His charming song, “ I was Dreaming,” achieved a suc-
cess seldom equalled in Australasia, and is now known
and admired all over the world, but, as is often the case,
most of the profit found its way into other pockets. His
opera, “ A Romance of Corsica,” a most melodious com-
position, was left severely alone by the Sydney public.
Juncker was bora in Cairo, Egypt, where his father was
German Consul.
“ 8.5.” : Re The Bulletin’s remark of last week that
“ Queen Elizabeth ” is “said to have been written” for
Ristori. That is so. See Ristori’s biography, p. 272 :
“In 1854 I thought of adding to my repertoire the part
of Queen Elizabeth in the drama of that name by my
illustrious and much-lamented countryman, Paolo' Gia-
cometti ai. 1 further on : “ Everything was ready and
the public were eagerly expecting the new play." Ris-
tori, in analysing the play, certainly does not say that
she died in darkness. Indeed, she elaborately describes
the facial expressions she used, and mentions how “ after
a brief struggle with death I sank to rest and lay with
glazed eyes surrounded by my courtiers.” The glazed
eyes wouldn’t have been very effective in the dark.
In the interests of fair play all round the booming of
Nance O’Neil calls for a little timely ridicule. Business
is business, but that’s no reason why a travelling show
with an abnormally large leading lady and a number of
new tactics should get a too easy loan of the Australian
public for a few weeks. Nance O’N. is going to play an
Ibsenic heroine for the benefit of Melbourne Hospital, and
the advt. of the event states that as Miss O’Neil is de-
termined to reserve ‘Hedda Gabbler’ solely for the
Hospital matinee it will be the only occasion on which
Australian playgoers will have an opportunity of seeing
the brilliant American artiste in one of her most famous
characterisations.” Melbourne doesn’t want any visitor
who is hustling for a living to miss the chance of working
a “ famous characterisation ” for all it is worth. If
“ Hedda Gabbler ” is any good as a money-making show,
it would be taking advantage of Nance’s generosity for
the Hospital to accept a sole performance of the cranky
drama, and if it isn’t a great popular attraction in other
countries wjiy make a special favor of the determination
te reserve it for a charitable purpose ? Nance’s advt is a
little too thick, all things considered. There may be a
few people who crave for what they will call the
intellectual treat of an Ibsen drama, but Melbourne as a
rule would prefer to see Nance wave her gory hands in
the penny orful version of “ Oliver Twist ” which
Sydney people have reported to be “ one of the most re-
markable performances ever witnessed on the Australian
stage.”
“W.E.P.”: Re observation (8., SO/6/’OO) that Nance
O’Neil could draw crowded houses to a naughty play like
Magda. This scribe’s experience is that the naughtier the
play the more the Philistines flock to see it. But it
remains to be seen whether Nance could draw crowded
houses for a week to Ibsen. Hedda Gabbler is one of
Ibsen’s finest plays, and the one least calculated to pain Fat-
man. And as Hedda we shall have an opportunity of
judging Nance as a “ restrained ” actress. Hitherto she
has let herself go pretty freely, but she can hardly do this
in Hedda. There isn’t even an opportunity for that voice
of hers. Hedda’s voice was just a plain, ordinary one,
but the things she said were calculated to hasten a man
to the devil. There is nothing melodramatic about
Hedda except her final exit, and that’s behind a curtain,
and without a scream. If Nance can satisfy the Ibsenites
in her rendering of Hedda, and fascinate the people who
say there is nothing in Ibsen, she might be voted a real
front-ranker.
“ Ingomar,” it seems, is to be seen again in Melbourne,
with Nance O’Neil as the Parthenia whom Essie Jenyns
pictured so charmingly. “ Macbeth ” was promised, but
Rankin pointed to his embonpoint as a reason why he
couldn’t play the name part, and they are not likely to
look elsewhere for a Mac. All the same a tall strong
Shakespearian actor with a mighty voice is usually to be
found in Melbourne if anyone wants to stage “ Macbeth,”
in a hurry. Augustus Glover is that sadly misused
tragedian. The present generation has no idea of what
Glover did (when he played With Bandmann years ago)
and could do again to-day. ’Gustus looks about as young
as ever. Born in Scotland, of a Spanish-Italian mother by
a French father, the big fellow who lately swaggered
around as Porthos in “ Three Musketeers ” is a fine robust
artist—wasted.
Ernest Hogan, the “coon” comedian, who tookaco.
to Honolulu, sued the Oanadian-Australian Steamship
Co. for 20,000d01. damages, for refusing to carry his co.
to Vancouver in the Miowera in terms of the agreement.
He got a verdict for 2500d01., and the co. did a grand
united sand-jig and wing-dance thereover. The S.S. Co.
was bound to “ fall-in,” anyhow; for, though it pleaded
“ plague ” to prevent the passage of the coons, it had
made no such demur in the case of whites.
F. : Sydney’s rotten Corporation charged Wiegand five
guineas for the use of the city organ for his benefit con-
certs ; and, not satisfied with this, actually grabbed out
of the first takings (before a protest could be laid)
another guinea for the use of the instrument for a half-
hour’s rehearsal in the afternoon preceding one of the
concerts.
Probably the line which evokes the biggest yell of ap-
plause in the “Absent-Minded Beggar” (Sydney Royal) is
that in which Frances Ross says that she loves the British
flag because under it all men are equal, and the white
man, and the black, and the brown, and the whity-brown,
and the blotched, and spotted, and mottled enjoy liberty
and justice alike. And probably, if Miss Ross fired off
that beautiful remark at Perth, in the slave State of
Westralia, it would fetch quite as loud a cheer. More-
over, it is quite likely that the Westralian nigger-owner
and nigger-driver would wave his enthusiastic hat in
absolute sincerity. Even in the Southern States, in the
days of Uncle Thomas, you could raise a deafening cheer
for the Flag of Liberty any day.

When Miln, the ’Murkan actor, was playing in Mel-
bourne, the now-defunct Daily Telegraph of that city
complacently announced the celebration of the thou-
sandth anniversary of his performance of Hamlet.
Nobody had previously supposed him to be the senior of
Methuselah. Apropos, a notable Chicago criticism of
Miln’s Hamlet was—“ Mr. Geo. 0. Miln played Hamlet
last night. He played it till 12 o’clock.”
Major C. F. N. Macready, of 2nd Gordon Highlanders,
now in S’Africa, is a son, per second wife, of W. 0.
Macready, the great tragedian. He is a good amateur
actor and very fond of the stage, though his father deter-
mined that no child of his should tread the boards. A
son by the first marriage was in Melb. in the early sixties,
when Barry Sullivan gave him a show as Oapt. Absolute,
in “ The Rivals.” He facially resembled his father, but
was a frost as an actor, and descended from high comedy
to super-ing.
The latest part written to suit Divine Sarah is that of a
well-preserved grandmother who falls in love with a
middle-aged bounder, and upbraids her son for chasing
the bounder from her amorous arms.

Wirth’s circus is returning to Australia after seven years
absence. The,big show and the great menagerie of sea-
sick animals are just now supposed to be rolling some-
where in Torres Straits. Opens at Cooktown and works
the coastal towns to Melbourne.
It is understood that Nance O’Neil will appear as Tess
of the D’Urbervilles in Melbourne.
When Barnum was running his big museum in New
York his band was generally understood to be the worst
on earth. A musical aspirant applied for a billet. “How
much,” asked he, “is it worth to play in your band?”
“ Well, about lOdols. a week for you.” “ All right, I’ll
join.” On pay-day the new hand went for his money.
“ Oh,” said Barnum, “ you must have misunderstood me :
it’s lOdols. you owe me. I can’t let you fellows torture
my visitors for nothing.”
Young Raimund Pechotsch, a one-time Sydney “ juven-
ile prodigy,” is now solo-violin at the Chevalier Recitals
in Queen’s Hall, London.
A New York paper' reports that the taking of one
famous Boer-war picture, which has since been greatly
appreciated for • its accuracy and life-like character—that
of the battle of Colenso—led to serious trouble. It was
taken at Orange (New Jersey, U.S.) The manager hired
an immense number of British soldiers in new uniforms
with guns, and Boers with guns and false whiskers ; but
just as the armies were drawn up, and the struggle was
about to begin, the Boers struck for 8/- a day. Not
getting it they put their whiskers in their pockets and sat
own. The British troops also struck and threw their
guns over the fence ; and it was only by the exercise of
great managerial tact that there was any battle at all.
A novelty is a floating theatre which is being
built in England to tour all the fashionable watering-
places. It is rather a small affair as an experiment, being
of but 100 tons burthen, and drawing only 20in. water.
The little auditorium is to seat 338 people, while another
three or four hundred will be able to look down from the
promenade-deck above on to the show beneath. It might
seem a doubtful sort of investment till it is stated that it
will have a license to supply “ refreshments.”
Heard coming away from “ The School for Scandal ” :
“It’s funny; but I liked her in ‘ Elizabeth’ much better.
My oath 1 she did go to market! ”
It is related of Quinn, the celebrated comedian, who
was a very famous Falstaff, that, asked to play the part
in his old age when he had lost all his teeth, he said,
“ Gad, sir, I’ll whistle Falstaff for no man.” Huge Nance
O’Neil, when asked to play in “ The School for Scandal ”
again, should say, “ I’ll overwhelm Lady Teazle for no-
body.” Nance, playing the graceful, frisky heroine of
Sheridan’s masterpiece is like a steam-roller playing the
part of a child’s go-cart. Had the author contemplated
such a massive Lady Teazle he would not have let her
hide behind a mere screen in the great scene in the fourth
act, but would have prescribed a hoarding.

Srice and volume of business likely to be done.
leanwhile the co. has received good grain from

There is a general idea that the French
are the only people who know how to eat, but,
judging by an astounding return received from
the North German Lloyd Steamship Co. what the
Germans and the passengers of all nations who
are within their ships don’t know about the art is
not worth learning. During 1899 the provisions
put on board for use on the voyage inoluded
4,417,9581b5. meats of various sorts, besides 12,290
hams,_ 35,301 calf-heads and sweet-breads, and
1726 live cattle and animals killed en voyage. In
the fish line the passengers seem to have had
either the voracity of pikes or the numbers of
the sea-shore sands, for they devoured 486,9251b5.
besides a million of oysters, crayfish, fowls and
game. But these are sordid details—it is when
the co. begins to reel off the list of good drinks
that the soul of the listener is stirred within him.
Sherry; Madeira, Port, 9387 bottles; Cognac,
13,579 bottles ; liqueurs, 34,581 bottles ; Claret,
87,147 bottles and 211,392 litres ; Rhine and
Moselle, 100,749 bottles; Champagne, 35,854
bottles; while the lager-beer bottles numbered
1,238,739.
WHAT IS “ D.C.L.” ?
“ D.C.L.” is a brand applied to whisky, and is a
guarantee of genuine merit, of good value, and of
a pure and wholesome spirit. Ask for “D.C.L.”
(Black Bottle) Scotch.*
Bigjohn Forrest has turned Federationist,
but the kind of Federationist he has turned is
hardly worth having. Recently the Westralian
Premier delivered an alleged Federal oration at
Bunbury, though it was mostly a wail that Federa-
tion was inevitable, and wound up with :
I would be sorry, however, if any words of mine should
influence anyone either one way or another.
To put it another way : “I hope I haven’t con-
vinced anybody, or converted anybody, or ex-
plained anything, or thrown any light on
anything.” In the other provinces even the most
dead-alive politician hopes that his words will
have some influence.
An ounce of Scrubb’s Cloudy Ammonia
added to the bath water will thoroughly cleanse the
skin when it is irritable and soap is objected to.
It will make the flesh white and firm in tone, and
is also an excellent water softener; in fact, a
little of the ammonia is a great improvement
even when bran, almond meal, or oatmeal are
also used; and when there is a tendency of the
skin to excessive perspiration—especially during
hot weather—it is invaluable as a||remedy.*
There is an interesting piece of paternal
biography in a courageous letter by Prof. Orme
Masson (Chemistry, Melb. University) on the
Marshall-Hall case. Mason explains the “Kreut-
zer Sonata ” incident: when M.-H. recommended
his students to read the book, he had forgotten all
about the drift of the story and remembered only
the passages about music ; and when the rest of
the book was brought to his recollection, he with-
drew the recommendation to read it. Says
Masson:
I remember a complaint in Edinburgh that the pro-
fessor (now emeritus-professor) of English had prescribed
“ Troilus and Cressida ” to his ladies’ class as the subject
for an essay. The great play was objected to as contain-
ing indecent passages. Nobody suggested that these
were in the professor’s mind when he chose the play, but
it was thought that he ought to have remembered them. I
believe he changed the subject for the essay, and the
incident closed.
The reference is, of course, to the chemistry-pro-
fessor’s father, Prof. David Masson, the author of
the standard life of Milton, and editor of the
standard edition of Milton’s works.
A N.S.W. Labor-caucus is not always
ruled by a majority vote. Sometimes, intellectual'
and aggressive weight count against mere plu-
rality, and five or six members have dictated to i
the fourteen or fifteen. The rank-and-file are not
infrequently unequal to the pressure of the
leaders. A threat of resignation of several seats
in the House once enabled five Solidarities to
bring an overwhelming majority to sweet reason-
ableness. That’s how Reid got Left.
At last week’s Sydney Orange celebration
Grandmaster Wheeler had much to say re the
S’African war, and the Australian contingents,
and the loyalty of Orangemen who, he inferred,
were the only people who had shed their gore to
any extent for the Empiah. Mr. Wheeler care-
fully avoided any allusion to that other Orange
G.M., the county Cavan auctioneer, who was in
command of the Boers at Nicholson’s Nek.
To prevent Bubonic Plague wear Metzler’s
“ Protective Anklets,” a certain protective against
the access of vermin and disease germs from the
ground. They are worn by several members of the
medical profession, including doctors of the staff of
the Board of Health, and are worn also by mem-
bers of the N.S.W. Police Force on duty in the
plague-infected quarters of Sydney, Obtainable
from all first-class chemists or from Head Office
of the Proprietary, 14 Castlereagh-street, Sydney.*
The fact that Newfoundland, the one
province which stood out of the Canadian Federa-
tion, has declined miserably, and the inference
that Westralia, if it stands out of the Australian
Commonwealth, may do the same, has roused up
a lot of Groper parochialists to explain. This is
Moorhead, M.L.A.:
Newfoundland had been trotted out on Wednesday
night. Of all the great federal unions, Mr. Gardiner held
up this miserable little rat. He held up that miserable
little rat as a horrible example of stopping out of a federa-
tion. Did his friend know where Newfoundland was ? It
was not in Collingwood. Newfoundland was a wind-
swept, fog-enveloped island. Federation could not give
land to a country ; gold in its mines, or timber on its hill-
sides. If the Lord made it poor the devil could not make
it rich.


LA GRIPPE
Comes Like a Thundei’b oXt
Expulsion
Rapid and Complete.
The Case of
MRS. MARGARET GATLIN.
( Investigated by a Reporter.)
No more mysterious or insidious disease
has come to curse the modern world than
influenza. How it arises no one knows ; its
nature none can explain. With brutal im-
partiality it slays high and low, rich and
p oor —all are liable to attack—each cf us
sooner or later falls a victim, so that cases of
remarkable cure and recovery have universal
interest; and surely no more desperate case
has been recorded than that of Mrs. Margaret
Catlin, of 121 Perry-street, Collingwood,
Me bourne, an old and respected resident of
that neighbourhood. Our reporter, hearing
of the case, promptly called at the pretty
cottage residence, and heard from Mrs.
Catlin herself the details of her case. Mrs.
Catlin said :—“ The influenza struck me
like a, thunderbolt, there were no prelim-
inary sjinpcoms. I remember I was in the
garden hanging out some things to dry ; my
hands were raised to peg some garments to
the line, when my legs went from under me;
it was as though a violent blow had been
struck across the back of my knees, causing
them to collapse, for down I fell all of a
heap. After that I became very ill, for over
three weeks not a bite of food passed my
lips that did me good, for all I ate was
immediately vomited up again. I suffered
from au agonising headache, accompanied by
“'burning flushes; my heart would go pit-a-pat,
pit a-pat, like a little bird fluttering inside
my chest; then this feeling would turn to a
lump, which would rise and rise till it came
to my throat. Not only had Ino appetite,
I came to loathe the very sight aud smeli of
food. Even tea and toast that they brought
to my bed to tempt me made me feel quite
sick when I saw it. Neuralgia pains took
me in the forehead, and settled over one eye
with a piercing agouy, as though a red hot
nail was being driven there. Then these
pains would spread all over the top of the
head with a feeling as though a heavy weight
was pressing on me there ; and so sore would
my head remain after one of these attacks,
chat I could not comb my hair. This neu-
ralgia would spread to my face, jaws, and
neck, so that I hardly dared to draw my
breath.”
“How did you sleep, Mrs. Catlin?” en-
quired the reporter.
“ I could not sleep at all, for every bone
in my body ached and was racked with pain,
every bone from the crown of my head to the
soles of my feet. I became thinner and
thinner from want of nourishment, tjll I
was nothing better than a living skeleton.
My eyes got red and raw, they burned like
fire, and hob water ran from them con-
tinually, so that Btrangers thought I was
crying.” „
“What remedies did you try, Mrs. Gatlin.
“ Quinine and iron, and other tonics of
that kind, but with no good resulb, till the
thought occurred to me to try Clements
Tonic. I first came to hear of that wonder-
ful remedy when I was on a visit to my son
in New South Wales. He is in a country
district in the railway service, and I found
that amongst the employes Clements lonic
is the accepted remedy in all cases of sick-
ness. At my wish my family sent out at
once for a bottle of Clements Tonic, and after
taking only two doses I was able to eat a
little for the first time after three weeks’
starvation ; andtheneyt day, that is, on the
Monday, I was able to leave my bed and sib
up in a chair. The effect of Clements Conic
was something marvellous, so rapid and
complete was the change it effected in my
condition ; it seemed to pour life and energy
into my veins, to brace up my shattered
nerves, while at the same time it chased all
the pains I have told you of out of my body.
Neuralgia, headache, lassitude, weariness,
sleepiness, all flew away like evil dreams to
the recuperative power of Clements Tonic.
And this is not all. In my own family with
my sous and daughters Clements Tonic has
done wonders in cases of diarrhoea as well as
influenza, and you may be sure I have not
been slow in recommending Clements Tonic
to my friends.”
“ Will you allow me to recommend
Clements Tonic in your name, Mrs.
Catlin ? ”
“You mean in the papers, I suppose.
You are quite welcome to report and print
what I say—for the truth must do good—in
any way you like.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Margaret Catlin. of 124 Perry-st., Coliingwood,
Melbourne, in the Colony ot Victoria, do solemnly
and sincerely declare that I have carefully read the
annexed document, consisting of three folios and
consecutively numbered from one to three, and
that it contains and is a true and faithful account
of my illness and cure by Clements Toaic, and also
contains my full permission to publish in any
manner my statement—which I give voluntarily,
and without receiving any payment; and I make this
solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions ofan
Act of the Parliament of Victoria rendering persons
making a False Declaration punishable for wilful
and corrupt perjury.
Declared at Coliingwood, in the Colony of
Victoria, this 24th day of January, one thousand nine
hundred, betore me,
~ J. GARDINER, J.P.,
A Justice of the Peace in and for the Central Baili-
wick of the Colony of Victoria.

which gives such convincing proof of a marvellous cure being effected by a remedy as the one we
publish from Mrs. Maria Weekes, of Springfield, Eugowra, New South Wales. That lady has been
literally rescued from the jaws of death by Bile Beans for Biliousness, and it is the positive duty,
not only of those who are ailing, but of the medical profession, to carefully read her symptoms and
perceive the miraculous cure achieved by the world’s renowned remedy, Bile Beans for Biliousness.
The following is what Mrs. Weekes says in a letter dated May 26th, 1900: —

pains in my chest, like a heavy weight pressing down, accompanied by a fullness after eating. I began
to get pains in the back and side which were almost unbearable, and bursting headaches would follow.
1 tried many remedies for these complaints, but none of them did me any good. As time went on I
continued to get worse, and nearly always felt tired and drowsy. My limbs were powerless and con-
stantly aching. I was very much depressed in spirits, and suffered dreadfully from biliousness, which
would make me feel very sick, and my head would swim around. I was miserable, disagreeable with
