The Labor-saving Jones.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jems wag the most energetic man in our
Railway Station. He was in charge of the
(roods Department, and was considered the smart-
est book-keeper in the service. Auditors were
afraid of him, for Jones had travelled much, and
had been in turn oh every section of the railways of
his province, and had kept a book in which he
carefully requested all auditors who were not satis-
fied with his system of accountancy to write down
their proposed amendments. In course of time,
he found that the auditors contradicted the in-
structions they gave down South by those they
gave up North, and that the errors of the back
country were the correct methods of the coast
section. To Jones this seemed an absurdity, and
when the latest auditor attempted to jump on him
in a superior manner, Jones told him he was suf-
fering from a very bad memory, and went on to
speak disrespectfully of auditors in general and
that one in particular. The auditor suspended
Jones, and that young man went to headquarters
and interviewed the Commissioners. Then the
Commissioners interviewed the auditors, and the
auditors felt very sore on the subject. In future
they always addressed Jones as Mr. Jones, and
treated him as a man of great attainments.
The local stationmaster was also afraid of Jones,
and addressed him as “Mr.” And on one occasion,
when far gone in whisky, he informed the writer
that he prayed every night and morning that
Jones might be seized with a fit or a desire to go
to the far interior of Brazil.
Just subsequent to Jones’ triumphant rein-
statement, after wiping the floor with the
kings and councillors of the Railway Depart-
ment, I was placed under the command of this
paragon. His office was a peculiar place. The
walls were covered with all sorts of mysterious
ready-reckoners, which I never learned to under-
stand. Jones understood them thoroughly, and
by a glance at them could give the correct answer
to any railway-problem that might be sprung upon
him. He also had a mania for labor-saving
machinery. He used the first fountain-pen I ever
saw, because it saved the labor of continually
dipping the pen into the ink-pot. He rode round
the shed on a bike because it was a saving of
labor. He fixed up small electric-indicators which
told him when a train was approaching, so that he
should never waste time looking out of the window
at the end of the shed, or going over to the platform
10 minutes too soon and then idling away valuable
minutes in talking with the imbeciles who ran
that portion of the station. In his home, every-
thing was done by machinery, and his wife made
her own dresses on a sewing-machine which was
worked by a baby engine driven by a tiny gas-jet.
When I had been about three months in the
office, I found that Mr. Jones had one weakness,
as great as all his attainments together, and it was
Beer. He never enjoyed beer that he had to pay
for. When he had to pay for it he never drank
more than one glass, but if he got it without pay-
ment he never stopped drinking till the beer was
finished.
There were two breweries—Cygnet and Sons,
and Steele, Jacks and Co.’s. Both sent enormous
quantities of fluid joy up-country by rail. The staff
had the run of both breweries, and, on an average,
received about a quart per man per day, but that
was not enough for Jones. It involved a dreadful
loss of time to bike around two breweries, and
Jones brought his giant intellect to bear upon how
to broach the casks in an autistic way. He solved
the problem by producing a glass tube about nine
inches long and an inch in circumference. It was
just the thing. Through it one could get a good
mouthful of beer at a pull, and thereby escape
the sudden intoxication which overtakes a man
who drinks beer through a straw. Mr. Jones ad-
mitted to me, in a burst of confidence, that there
were only two ways in which beer ever made him
drunk—drinking it through a straw, or eating it
with a spoon out of a soup-plate. The straw set-
tled him the quicker of the two.
He was a generous fellow, and kindly allowed
the rest of us to have the loan of the tube when
there were more than six barrels in the shed at
once. True, he used to interrupt us about every
five minutes for the loan of the tube to save his
life. One day Barton, the loader, let the tube slip
into the barrel, and for the life of him could not
get it out. Jones nearly went mad, and tried all
sorts of ingenious tricks to rescue the instrument.
It was one of Cygnet’s barrels. Cygnet always
examined the interior of his returned empties,
and we wondered what he would think when he
found that glass tube in the barrel. Two weeks
later Cygnet and. the manager appeared with the
glass, tube, and asked Jones confidentially did he
think any of the engine-drivers had a spare gauge-
glass which they kept for tapping tlie beer in
transitu. Jones said lie shouldn’t wonder if they
had, and looked very shocked, and asked them to
leave the tube with him as a clue. Cygnet said
he thought he would take it with him and keep it
as a curiosity. Jones looked disappointed.
Next week Jones appeared with an improved
apparatus. It consisted of a glass tube with
a flap of indiarubber round the end of it,
and joined to this was a long, thin indiarubber
tube, with, at the end, the mouthpiece of a
baby’s feeding-bottle. When the staff gazed upon
this they were paralysed. Jones, with a bright
smile, proceeded to show how it could bo worked
without leaving the desk. There were six hogs-
heads in the shed, and they were rolled up
against the single-board wall of the little office
wherein we worked. The tube of glass was in-
serted through the spigot-hole, and the india-
rubber flap held it in position and kept air out.
The rubber tube came up through the wall and the
desk, and Jones could sit on his high stool with
the mouthpiece in his mouth, and swallow beer all
the time he was working. It was a splendid idea ;
it worked perfectly—for Jones. None of the rest of
us could get a pull at it on any account, and were
forced to go across to the brewery to slake our
thirst.
It was 3 o’clock ; Jones was hard at work with
the baby’s-bottle arrangement in his mouth, and
the rest of the staff were in an agony of thirst. As
Jones gave no sign of slacking-off or taking a spell
we went across to Steele, Jacks and Co.’s. . We
stopped there about half-an-hour, and had several
glasses of half-and-half. Then we strolled down
to the cellars and got a big bottle each and started
back leisurely for the shed. We had just got to
the door when we noticed that the head of Jones
was not visible through the window of the office.
Then we were staggered to see him lying on the
broad of his back with a silly grin on his face,
while from the baby’s bottle apparatus trickled
a steady stream of beer over the warehouse
book down into his mouth. We looked at
this terrible apparition, and rubbed our eyes to
make sure that there was really beer enough in the
world to upset Jones. Then, as we were about to
enter the office and pick him up, we heard a step
on the gravel, and, looking up, saw the station-
master approaching. Realising that we were none
of us fit for inspection, we hastily scrambled be-
hind some sacks of potatoes and drew a tarpaulin
over us. ,
The stationmaster walked down the shed and
seemed astonished at the deserted appearance
thereof. He peeped into the office and would
have passed on, only Jones gave a gurgling sound
which caused him to glance up at the roof and
then look in at the delivery window. Even then
he would not have seen Jones, but that infernal
mouthpiece stood there on the desk, pouring forth
a thin stream of beer. This remarkable sight took
the stationmaster in at the door, and he saw Jones
lying at the foot of the desk. He looked at him
for a moment; then an expression of intense
joy spread over his features, and he rubbed his
hands. He stepped over to the tube and smelt it.
Next he wet his finger with the beef that trickled
from it and touched it with his tongue. Then he
took a good long pull at it himself. He seemed
to like it, and had another. Next he got out a
few sheets of foolscap and wrote for about tea
minutes. Having finished his writing he shifted
Jones out of the way, and sat on the seat with
the tube in his mouth, and a pleased smile spread
all over him. I was just beginning to get drowsy—
I think I had nodded—when there was a sudden
crash. I looked down into the office with a start —
the stationmaster had fallen off the stool.
We waited a few moments, and as he didn’t
rise again we climbed out of our hiding-place and
went into the office. Jones was snoring loudly.
The stationmaster was sitting against the leg of
the desk, and the beer was trickling down the
back of his neck. He looked at us with an owlish
expression, and remarked in a thick husky voice :
“ Yesh, I rang; I’ll have a long beer, pleash.”
(Turning to an imaginary companion): “ Whatsh
you goin’t’ have, my dearsh?” We laughed at
this, and asked him who the lady was. “ Eesh in
the drinks and some hamsh and other zhings,”
he said. We held a hurried consultation
—some of the public might arrive at any
moment —and while we were talking the S.M.
spoke again : “ Ish it any obligation on your partsh
to sherve ush ? ” I handed the beer-tube down to
him, and he seized it hungrily and put it in his
eye. At last he got it into his mouth, and about
two minutes later fell over on his side, dead-drunk.
We made a little square of goods and hogsheads,
laid the two drunks in it side by side, and left
them there to recover while we read the papers
the boss had left on the desk. One was a docu-
ment suspending Jones for drunkenness on duty,
and the other a full report of the state in which
he had found the “ auditor’s terror” ; also a strong
reflection on his dishonesty in stealing beer and an
urgent recommendation that Jones be dismissed
the service as a warning to others. It furthermore
said that he could not undertake to manage the
station as it should be managed unless an example
was made of Mr. Jones.
Next' morning the stationmaster tore those
papers up, and Jones resumed duty at 9 a.m.
The new time-saving beer-drinking apparatus
was destroyed.

«« j- *» to The B ULLETIN re the “ change of
front in Rudyard Kiplmg:
Eleven years ago the American way of speaking English
was unfamiliar and repugnant to him, and then he wrote
fchat the Americans who professed to pity him for speak-
with „ an E n gi; s h accent,” were themselves speaking
ag j ar ag j ie wag concern ed the “ language of thieves.”
To _ d Kipling is Americanised (through living in Ame-
rica) and he uses the nasal accent and the dialectical
phrases which sounded vulgar or “flash” in his ears
aforetime. This is not necessarily a change of front on
Kipling’s part. He has acquired the Americanisms of
phrase and accent because they are infectious. Man is
an imitative animal, and if a man gets settled among
le w h oS e language is different from his own and
as f ly imitated> he can > fc help speaking as they speak.
p oa i language is very infectious, likewise nasal twangs,
provincial dialects, and the language of the cockney
gutter, or the stable. Touching the comparative ease
with which English provincial dialects are imitated, I
r a 0 n m q^° a te le 3
middle . a | e d Italian who spoke “ Yarksheer” English like
a na tive of the country, He had spent only 15 months in
England, he said, but all the time he had been in York-
shire. The artificiality of these country dialects lends
itself to imitation, as every character-actor knows. The
Italian aforesaid could not have learned to speak high
English— i.e., Gladstonian English—fluently m 15months,
rj,[ ie accen t 0 f we il-bred, unaffected people in any couiltry
i s the hardest for a foreigner to speak without a foreign
tone. Kipling’s tongue probably is not Americanised
up to the highest American (the Southern Staters’) stan-
dard. I guess that-he talks the Godarn di-a-Zect of
Amurkan cits, m general, which is remarkable for its all-
round sameness. The accent and the voice tone of the
average New Yorker seem to be identical with every
other fellow’s tone and accent. Language and utterance
are both of them machine-made. If Kipling ever had a
vocal individuality he has lost it, but there’s no evidence
of his having elected to lose it. More likely he has be-
come the victim of his imitative faculty.

ENGLISH TESTIMONIALS.
INCIPIENT CONSUMPTION.
FROM A WELL-KNOWN CHEMIST OE
LEEDS.
76, Briggate, Leeds.
Dear Sirs, —For some considerable time I have
taken an interest in a poor widow, Mrs. Medd, 24,
Ney-street, Leeds, whose husband died some
years ago of Consumption, leaving her three chil-
dren to provide for. About two years ago the
eldest was taken ill, and died of the same disease.
A year ago the second child began to show similar
symptoms—no appetite, cough, and loss of
weight. Fearing there was little chance of doing
her good, but determined to try, I took her to
Dr. Rollinson, who was then practising in the
town, who examined her. He found very distinct
symptoms of Phthisis, and on hearing about her
father and brother, gave very little hope of her
living more than a few months, advising cod-liver
oil as her only chance. On hearing from me later
that the child was wholly unable to take the oil in
any form, he very fortunately recommended your
Emulsion. lat once procured a bottle, and think
it is only due to you to let you know how very
satisfactory the result has been, quite beyond ex-
pectations or hope. She has gradually but regu-
larly increased in weight, her cough is gone, and
her appetite come back, and I can hardly tell you
how grateful her mother is.—Yours faithfully,
(Signed) J. C. REINHARDT.
CONSUMPTION.
98 Algernon-road, Newcastle-on-Tyne.
Dear Sirs, —Some two years past last Novem-
ber my health and strength broke down. I was
received into the Royal Infirmary of this city,
where I was treated for Incipient Phthisis. My
case was rather an anxious one, being reduced in
body to almost a perfect skeleton. While I lay in
this low state my physician prescribed your Emul-
sion, making the kindly remark that I was the
first patient they had ordered it for, and that he
hoped and looked for great results, which were
fully and practically realised. By the end of the
first week I felt my strength gradually returning,
very soon after was able to stand up, eventually
to get about, and at the end of two months able
to leave the institution. I returned to my work,
and have kept at it ever since with the exception
of one or two short intervals. During the whole
of this time I have off and on used your Emulsion,
which I have proved to be my sheet anchor. I
can fully endorse all you claim for it, whilst lay-
ing special stress upon the fact it is pleasant to
take, and (the most important of all to invalids)
there are no eructations after taking it, such as is
common with the best of cod-liver oils.
(Signed) RONALD McDONALD.

The Ship of My Heart.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She has been sailing years and years;
Hull-down on a bygone sunset she vanished with
all my dreams,
I have followed her wake with wonder, I have
hidden her track with tears,
I have watched from the lonely cliffs for a million
ages, it seems.
Oh, the joy of her scented sails !
She was fettered abeam with roses, she was laden
with lilies abaft,
She carried the blue of the hills and the pink of
the blossoming dales,
And the dearest of all girl-lovers looked down
from her decks and laughed.
And I watch on the cliff-tops brown
For a silver sail in the offing, for a gleam on the
rainbow foam,
For the sweeping dip of a seabird’s wing when
the whispering tide goes down,
To bring me the message I fain would hear —that
the Ship of my Heart comes home.
Now the grey mists cover the sea ;
I am almost weary of waiting, with eyes long set
to the tides,
But I know she is sailing homeward with sea-
winds singing a glee
In the heart of her rocking ropes, and the seaweed
over her sides.
And I know she will come some day,
And the beautiful love of my boyhood’s heart on
her plunging bows will stand
To fling me the snow-white roses she wore when
she went away,
And shout me a word of her laughing love, a cable-
length from the land.
And the anchoring chains will fall,
And the wind will lull and the horses white feed
slow to the purple sea ;
And I shall know when I hear her voice, when I
hear her passionate call,
That this is the only love of my heart that is
gathered again to me.
N.S.W.
W ILL H. OGILVIE.
Engaged.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There was nothing for him, and he turned away
from the stage-door in bitter disappointment. It
was the last theatre left, there were no companies
going out, and he was at his lowest ebb.
It was a bright morning, and all the world
seemed working. He could not endure these
active streets, where money was clinking on all
sides, and returned to his lodging. This was on
top of a large barrack-like building, given over to
lodgers of all sorts. On the ground-floor was a
laundry, where brawny, bare armed women ap-
peared through a mist whenever the door opened.
He threw himself on his bed, thinking how to
avoid thinking. There was no glimmer of a
chance for him. A country actor of small reputa-
tion, without clothes, money, or the ingenious
brilliancy that does without either, he had been
employed by second-rate managers and no others.
A clerk in a warehouse ten years ago, he had
beoome stage-struck. Most of his former fellow-
clerksbythistimewore frock-coats, had money, and
were prosperous. He had thrown away his chance
of their prospects —and for what ? He turned
over his notice-book —taken that morning to the
rejecting manager—for answer. It was bulky
with cuttings of papers, from Thursday Island to
Kanowna—some written by his managers, some
in return for drinks ; a few genuine. Everywhere
his name, Tom Midcliffe, was underlined in ink.
There was his first hit—in Feeney, at Charters
Towers. What pains he had taken with it —what
a triumph the drunken Irishman who wanted to
punch his head for the performance. Ten years
he had been in the business, and had learnt to
“ take a situation, ” let humorous lines sink into
back-country brains, and claim credit for
the consequent laugh ; make - up for all
the types of conventional melodrama, from the
red-nosed comedian to the aristocratic heavy;
to even play a lover and hold the attention
—of Cobar—and had at all points the merits
of that despised mediocrity, “ the good
useful man.” He always wrote in applications
for engagements “ late Geo. Rignold’s C 0.,”
as he had once doubled a corpse and a gaoler
at the tail-end of a season with that manager.
Actors he had started with had passed him and
got on—some by talent, more by or with the addi-
tional aid of audacity, style, women, or the good-
fellowship of hard drinking. But though Mid-
cliffe had dazzled a barmaid in Binalong, she had
no metropolitan influence, and a rebellious liver
kept him sober ordinarily, so he lacked male
friends.
He would have to pawn his wardrobe for his
immediate wants. He emptied it out of his box
—a collection of motley shabbiness, stained with
the dust of all Australia. He heaped the articles
on the floor on a newspaper. Besides clothes
there were old letters, plays, stray parts, a few
wigs, a “ Hamlet ” which he had once studied.
He recalled his early enthusiasm when he went
into the bush and spouted scenes of it. At the
bottom was an old album.
The first photograph in this was a faded one of
a girl posing in tights. A dainty face, a graceful
figure tapering down to exquisite ankles and
pointed feet, could still be traced in the dim silver.
She had been the immediate cause of his being
stage-struck. The clerk’s salary was spent in
seeing her, and buying her photos and sending
her Howers. He dreamed romances of going on
the stage, meeting and marrying her, and both
being stars together. For he had been that
modern rarity—the young fool all-faithful to his
folly. But they had never met, and he had
almost forgotten her. He pulled it from the
album and read the name—Yi Fane. Turning it
over, he had quite forgotten the lines on the back,
written in elementary stenography, which he was
then studying for the office. It was a vow in
verse to dedicate his life to the drama and—Yi
Fane.
As he deciphered them—for one never forgets
stenography altogether—the wasted years passed
in review. His rosy youthful imaginings stood
side by side with the present cadaverous reality.
A bitter feeling of injustice at being made a
superlative fool without being able to blame him-
self swelled within him. For the absence of any
active vice denied him this solace —it all seemed
so cruelly unjust, so spitefully malicious. Some-
thing like a sob came into his throat as he gazed
and thought. With sudden passion he tore the
photograph into fragments, kicked his wardrobe
all over the room, and flung himself on the bed,
swearing and sobbing by turns.
Most of us forget our ideals, luckily ; but when
they do make themselves remembered, their
mockery is in malicious proportion to their
illusiveness.
•
“Young man, would you oblige us with helpin’
of us with the mangle ? We has 60 shirts to get
done by to-night—most of ’em polished, too —and
our young man has left us all of a sudden . . .”
It was Mrs. Chirrup, the ground-floor laundress
—very short, fat, and out of breath from the three
flights of stairs.
“ But I come up myself, ’cos the exercise keeps
me down.”
She explained her demand more fully, glancing
at the scattered clothes and Midcliffe’s appearance.
The daughter had gone out to find where Bob—
the defaulting mangier—had gone, and this made
them more short-handed.
“ Would the young man .”
Midclitfe assented active exertion would
deaden thoughts of despair. Mrs. Chirrup led
the way, with many apologies for troubling him.
“You’ll never know when it’ll come in useful.
Mangling is a h’art. As, when you’re married to
a well-edrcated young woman, you’ll find out,
when the sheets ain’t smooth.”
Midcliffe followed her into the soapy steam of
the laundry. Through the haze he made out piles
of linen being counted and made into parcels by
Mrs. Chirrup’s youngest daughter, and an ironing-
board where an amazon was busy with a heap of
collars. The irons were heated on a special stove
holding a dozen at a time. The air was hot,
moist, suffocating, and peculiarly pungent. Mrs.
Chirrup explained that this was from the leather-
cuttings which fed the stove.
“ Which we get from the factory at the back.
They burn well and we get ’em for the trouble
of carting them away. Besides, the smell is
healthy.”
Then Midcliffe was introduced to the mangle, a
huge affair with a weighted box dragging on
rollers by chains from a wheel with an alternat-
ing action. It was fed by another daughter.
“I used to do the mangling myself, as helps to
keep me down—only I like to do the polishing
myself, and that pile will take me till 11 to-
night.”
Midcliffe turned the handle, the little girl
placed the articles under the rollers, and Mrs.
Chirrup resumed her polishing-iron again, thank-
ing him in gasps between each stroke, and the in-
terrupted operations proceeded. Outside on
the verandah another amazon was busy with a
washing-machine and a wringer. Mrs. Chirrup
screamed occasional directions and received
screaming replies without a break ; the
child fed the mangle and attended to
customers without giving Midcliffe a mo-
ment’s spell. The energy of the place took him
away from himself, and he began to feel the
healthy-mindedness attendant upon tired muscles.
Then came dinner. Midcliffe’s plate was well
filled and kept so. The talking was as hearty as
the eating. He was told all about the truant Bob,
who appeared to be an erratic youth ; how he
could play the concertina and do a breakdown.
Midcliffe was complimented by Mrs. Chirrup on
his staying-powers. Bob, it appeared, always
wanted rests when the work was heavy. He also
gathered that the daughter who was away looking
for Bob would be intolerant of a successor. They
also spoke of the rise in meat, the competition of
the Chinese, the establishment of a rival laundry
where they used a patent starch, a gold stud
found in a shirt that morning .... but Mrs.
Chirrup always harked back to her troublesome
proportions.
“It’s me happetite, and it’s the leather that
gives it to me. This morning, I was 34 and a
bit. Do you know, I’ve tried everything, and I
cannot get thin.”
“ Try my business,” said Midcliffe, but without
bitterness, because he had fed well and was smok-
ing some of Bob’s tobacco, which Mrs. Chirrup
had given him.
“ And what might your trade be ? ”
“ I’m an actor.”
“ I knew ! ” screamed Mrs. Chirrup. “ Didn’t
I say so, Maria ? ”
The amazons stared with new interest.
“Don’t I know ’em ! Why—l was on the stage
myself once. Look, there’s my picture over your
head. Maybe you’ve heard of Vi Fane ? ”
Midcliffe looked at the picture—it was a replica
of the one he had destroyed !
“ You—Vi Fane ! ”
“Yes, that was me before I got stout. I gave
up the dancing and went into drama, but only got
worse, and at last I had to give that up too, as
Vi Fane wasn’t going to play comic old women.”
Midcliffe very slowly recognised in the good-
natured and generously-proportioned figure of
Mrs. Chirrup, features of the dainty dancer that
had lured him to the footlights. Instinctively he
looked towards her feet. They were lost in
bluchers, but the skirts somehow suggested ele-
phantine extremities. Gradually he noted traces
of her old smile lurking in the pendulous cheeks,
and even the bluchers made an effort to point the
toes and take the first position.
“ Me darter what’s away looking for Bob is mad
to be a dancer too, but I won’t have it, as she
takes after me. The stage is all right if you ain’t
got a h’appetite—once your waist reaches 25,
managers is cold to you and the opera-glasses turn
on someone else.”
A light-footed young girl skipped in through
the shop.
‘ ‘ Mother, Bob’s gone on the stage —he’s a Roman
soldier with Julius Knight’s company.”
She was like the Vi Fane of former days, and
she was plainly envious of Bob’s luck.
Mrs. Chirrup then offered Midcliffe Bob’s place.
He accepted. While turning the mangle he heard
a whispered colloquy between mother and
daughter.
“ He’s an actor, too, my girl.”
“ And he’s going to work in a laundry—he can’t
be much good.”
The girl came in to inspect Midcliffe. He
smiled—which was foolish, because it wrinkled
his eyes. She went out again.
“Mother, isn’t he gray? ”
